CAMEO CIGARETTES
THE RED PAGE.
Somebody writes wondering: ‘what it would be
like to spend an hour in The Bulletin den, when
the lions are gnashing their teeth over the rejected
MSS.’ Well . . .
Whew! here’s a pile—artioles, essays, tales,
sketches, paragraphs, verses . , .
Sir, I have written a novel about bush life whioh I
would like to publish in The Bulletin. There are 193
chapters, and you could print a chapter in each issue. It
is very exciting, full of fights with bushrangers, etc., etc.,
and lam sure it would be very popular. Please let m
know how you pay for novels, and kindly reply by retur
post, as lam anxious to have the matter settled. Yours
respectfully, Henry .
No stamps, no addressed envelope—no fear
And a reply by return mail. Say 200 contribu
tions reach The Bulletin every day—and some
times there are more—and all, or half, or a quarter
of the writers were answered by return mail, or
any mail at all—with the personal explanations
and detailed reasons whioh the average writer
desires so badly when, pray, would the
paper be edited ? So Henry’s letter is
briefly answered in the correspondence column,
and that sorb of Henry never thinks of looking ir
the correspondence column. So he pines, and
denounces The Bulletin to his neighbours as a
paper whioh does its best to smother the aspira-
tions of native genius.
* * * *
If Henry had eyes, and wits, and used his
eyes and wits, he would see and know that The
Bulletin never dreams of printing novels in 193
chapters—that it never priuts serial stories at all.
Bub that sort of Henry never has eyes or wits—
for things external to himself and his story, any-
way. There is pathos, of course, in Henry’s idea
that for the time being he and his novel in 193
chapters are sitting at the centre of the uuiverse,
and the solar system *nd the earth and the
editors are revolving round him ; but the edge of
the pathos is dulled by habit. There are so many
Henrvs, whose literary necks have to be wrung in
so many seconds if the paper is to come out on
Thursday, and the literary cook rarely has time
for a qualm . . .
Oh ! and here you are again—
Dear Sir, —Three days ago I posted to The Bulletin a
MS. drama in five acts, entitled ‘ Abdur-rahmao, or the
Moor of Cadiz.’ As I have had no reply, and there is no
mention of it in the correspondence column,
(She posted it on Wednesday, and devoured
next day’s correspondence column—once, with
delicious thrills of anticipation : a second time,
with gradual misgivings : and a third time, with
blank despair.)
I write to ask whether you have received it.—Yours
truly', Jane
No man living or dead has ever known The
Bulletin to publish a five-act drama, or to give the
least sign of wanting to publish a five-act drama ;
but Jane knows that her drama is literature, and
she knows that The Bulletin prints literature,
and she adds two and three together to make
four with all the dexterity of her sex. Likely
enough there will be trouble with Jane. If her
MS. has gone astray in the post—as MS. some-
times will, generally' because the contributor sends
it on a long journey so flimsily-wrapped and
slightly-tied that the least shock sends the packet
sprawling open—or if in the multitude of contri-
buttons (many of them requiring very careful con-
sideration) there is delay, Jane is quite capable of
oalling in person ; and that, under the circum-
stances, is a thing to be avoided at all costs. So
We will hunt Jane up . , , yes, here she is . . .
»nd pack her off the premises as speedily as
possible.
' • * .
And there are many persons who, if not in
quite such novel and dramatic extremes as Henry
and Jane, yet think and act in similar fashion.
It is only when an amateur writer has sent in ten
or a dozen rejected MS. that he begins to realise—
to really realise—that there are writers in the
world besides himself. Before that he only thinks
he realises. But to nearly every beginner the little
tale that he has taken so much pains over (or has
dashed off ’ the Australian beginner has quite
a talent for ‘ dashing off ’) seems the most moment-
tous thing in existence. He never thinks of look-
ing at it as it lies on the editor’s desk—swallowed
m the surging heap of vastly (the chances are)
more interesting and important contributions. If
he did, probably, he would write so that he
oould be read with the least pains possible.
Fancy a tired editor-man, whose brain should
De always at its best, whose nerves should
be always strung, and who never has time for
half the things he wants to do—fancy him wading
through forty pages of crabbed script like the
tracks of a spider walking in its sleep. Yet
that is what the cheerful contributor asks
him to do every hour of his life. With the
best, will in the world, the editor-man can't
do it. He has not time and energy to puzzle
a meaning out of every separate word when
he should be catching the message of a sen-
tence or a page. As a matter of duty, he tries
to form some opinion on everything submitted to
him; but sometimes it is impossible. And a
writer never loses anything by making his script
legible, his copy attractive. To see the way some
people crowd their words and narrow their lines
you would think there were only two or three
sheets of writing-paper in creation. Sometimes,
of oourse, in the far bush that is practically all
there are; and then allowances must be made.
But there is doubtfully an allowance for the
anaemie-inked person with only nine black
corpuscles to a sheet, or for the lead-pencil person
with the smudge. These are they who put out
editors’ eyes—conscienceless, remorseless. .
Now, here is a man who writes plainly, in solid
black ink, on good white paper . . . Ha !
1897.

Dear Sir

line you must look on it with leinent eye and if it suits
you pray let me hear from you in your Correspondence
list With as much encouragement as you can spare.
And Will Oblige

E. Bbancournb (alias)

Just near the River Banks, I stood
While waiting, for my Love
When off toward a heavy Wood

What did I do, but straight there Run

And marks of a struggle saw
My thoughts formed quite unpleasant dreams
Of happiness no more
4th Verse.
The idea, came to my mind at once
• That she had been assaulted
When a pain shot me, like a lance
For not having gone to meet Her.

in the vain attempt to see?

I could scarcely see a yard
But still I hurried forward

And Found Myself within a Laut

Very likely you will notice the want of principal
these few' lines. However you can judge whether Iha
any chance in this line or not.
Sig. E.B.
• * • •
Well, legible writing isn’t everything, it seems
but note, at all events, that ‘E. Brancourne
(alias) first contribution got printed—which
more than can be said for most first contribution
And he is a man of sense, too, as well as metti
(whatever his lack of ‘ principal ’—at which Lor
forbid that a scribe should east a stone), for h
writes his letter on the same sheet as his copi
Most writers send separate letters, and, in defiance
of rules, will persist in leaving their names o
their copy. Take 150 letters a day for three days
400 packets (probably delivered by differen
mails) without anything but writing and paper t
identify the senders; mix thoroughly; and . .
listen to the editor-man swearing! Listen i
little longer, and hear the contributors swearin
because the unidentified copy isn’t answered, o
isn t printed, or isn’t returned. But no contri
butor ever swears at simself.
• • • •
When, as an unknown contributor, you are
sending in copy to any editor whatsoever, don’
write a letter, but write your name and addres
legibly on the copy, and preferably on every shee
of the copy. If you have a signature which is an
anagram and a conundrum and an acrostic rollec
into one, don t use it: write legibly. Letters aren’
wanted : the copy is the only thing of impor
tauce : in 9999 out of 10,000 cases the copy tells the
edito* all he wants to know. If you desire a pen
n / line or initials to be used in replying, write it or
them at the foot of your copy, and your name wil
not be printed. But don’t write to say it’s youi
first contribution, or your last, or youi
middle one. Don’t write to say the inci-
dent happened to your aunt, or your mother
in-law, or your mate Bill. Don’t write to
r re *' urn unavailable, but tether a stampec
and addressed envelope firmly to your copy, anc
let it be your silent and effectual witness, ’in a
word, don t write letters, or if you feel you must
write that nothing :n the world will stop you
from writing, then write your name and address
on the copy as well, and the editor-man will cheer-
fully lose the letter and say no more about it.
But don’t write letters.
Now we feel easier. . . . What’s this ?
DOT AND CARRY.
They were at Jones’s ball—Dot, Carry, and their brother
Mil. Dot and Carry they were always called, but their
leal names were Diana Euphrosyne and Cleopatra
Mineria. Their grandfather Harris could not say those
names, though he says that he was able to before he lost
all his teeth. They had only one brother: his name was
Miltiades Caesar, which was shortened to ‘Mil.’ He,
with their mother and grandfather Harris, made up the
whole family.
It was near midnight, and Dot and Carry wanted Mil to
come away immediately after supper, because no one
had been to see if any of the cows were bogged in the
mud that evening. Mil had been over at Jones’s all
the afternoon, helping them to fix a tent to have the
supper in, and Dot and Carry had been in a state of
feverish excitement decking out their pink muslin blouses
with lace and ribbon. Also, their shoes wanted a lot of
attention, but bush girls are always ready to meet an
emergency, so Dot made the shoes secure with the aid of
an awl and some wire off an old broom-handle.
Carry had intended to look and see if anything was
bogged in the creek before she came away, but Joe
Brown was stripping wattle-bark on the other side of the
creek, and she would not let him see her in her dirty
clothes—especially as he would be at Jones’s that night,
and would be sure to tease her about it, He had helped
them once before to pull the old black bull out of a bog
She was very much ashamed of that, because she remem-
bered that when she saw him coming she made a rush to
get under the chock-and-log fence and was held fast by
the hips until he came up ; for the life of her she could
not get either way.
Ada, Nell, and Sam Merry came over early in the even-
ing to go with Dot and Carry to the ball, so they did not
bother any more about the cows, for they were proud
girls and aid not want to let the Merrys know how
poverty-stricken their cattle were. ‘lt would be such
fun for the old things to know that we had to go and
probe the mud-holes before we went to the ball,’ thev
said ; M e will come away early and have a look before we
go to bed. ’
Supper is over, and Dot and Carry are going, but they
cannot get a budge out of Mil, who is sitting in a remote
corner, with his arm around Dolly Manners’ waist Dollv
would not notice poor Mil, only now she wants to nark
tnat round-shouldered, limp-looking creature with the
burrs and bits of stringy-bark in his matted, red beard
He has a large bank balance, and Dolly is bent on having
shares m it.
Mil tells Dot and Carry to be going on : he • will follow
m a minute;’ but they know that he won’t come until
Dolly gets tired of his encircling arms. Poor fellow 1 ■
the first mash that he ever had the courage to do and h"
is almost in a trance. Dot and Carry wended their w,
homeward, talking ‘hesezand I sez,’ and ‘I sez and
sez,' until a ’possum ran across the road and made u **
clutch each other with fright. After that they tmdla
along in silence until they noticed a few streaky wind
clouds in the western sky, and w ondered if thev .
rain. **
‘ Did y° u Bee *he forecast for New South Wal™ i
to-day’s paper, Carry V • Yes : Pine, with clouds m 0 !
strong wind from South-West, rough seas in the extZ
nothing 481 The " both gave a heav > r sigh, but said
Now they have reached the creek, and sure enough
there is old Kit-only her back and head above the grid.
mud. They stand and listen, but no sign of Mil
we’ll have to try and haul her out somehow ourselves.
Let’s get the leg-ropes and the long-handled shovel ’
They took off their shoes and stockings, and skirts-
their blouses did not matter, they would wash, and were
already dirty from the men’s hands. They pulled and
tussled for about half-an-hour with very little success.
Kit would not help to free herself, but she would bang
at them every time she got the ohance, with her bd
crooked horns. ui *
‘Lemme give yer a ’and ! ’-and Joe Brown sorambied
down the bank of the creek. «Oh !it was cruel 1 ’ Carrv
said afterwards: • I felt a cool thing run all over me.’
And to make matters more ridiculous poor Carry sank un
to her waist in the mud, and Joe had to pull her out
and with their assistance he got Kit out also; but she
was cramped from being in the mud so long, and had to
be shot next day. The hide only fetched 3s.hides were
a drug in the market.
Dot and Carry made Joe take a great solemn oath that
he would never breathe a word of that night’s doings.
Joe promised, but did not keep his word. Dot and Carry
think he did, so 1 where ignorance is bliss, 'tis folly to be
wise.’
The poor girls did not think it worth their while to go
to bed: they did not know what time it was, because
they forgot to wind the clock ; but the jackasses were
laughing and they knew it must be near day.
After they had washed the mud off themselves, Dot
went to let out the ducks, and she saw Mil coming home.
He would kick up his heels every now and then, and
laugh and shake his hands. She felt furious as she looked
at him, and muttered to herself: • Look at that silly old
ourang-outang acting like a lunatic I He can’t see that
Doll Manners is only making a joey of him. I suppose he
is satisfied, now that his unfortunate sisters are dis-
graced. I have a good mind to pelt a stone at his head
though I suppose I would miss it?’
Then old Nanny, the cat, came up and brushed against
her dress, and cried ‘ meow ’ very pitifully, and Dot felt
inclined to cry also. * Only what good would it do? ’
* * * *
Now, this is a pleasant little sketch which
makes one love the author. It is almost safe to
wager (such wagers are never quite safe, so erratic
is Dame Nature) that Maggie is a fresh, buxom
young Australian girl, brimful of health and
spirits, and without a taint of morbid ‘new-
woman (not that all ‘ new-women ’ are morbid
by any means). But six times in ten contribu-
tions similar to this would barely pass Bulletin
muster, for want of just a little shaping and
polishing to take off the crudity of inexperience.
Half-an-hour s editing may be all that is needed
but even half-an-hour is something that a busy
editor-man continually signs for. So one has fre-
quently to regret, and write “sorry unsuitable"
and pass on. But Maggie may certainly be
encouraged to persevere, and to study the style
of more practised writers, for there is real grit in
her, p
• • * •
Of course, the general level of rejected contribu-
tions is way below Maggie’s. And the pitiful thing
is that the writers cannot understand why they
are rejected, and it is impossible to give detailed
explanations The casual contributor has indeed
to tread a bridge of broken pillars such as Mirza
saw in his vision. This one pops through and is
lost at the arch of irrelevance; that one is
clutched by the harpy of bad taste; countless
numbers disappear simply because they have not
strength to carry them over. And very
often, and very naturally, the inability
to write well includes the inability to
discern what is written well, and wherein lies
the difference between bad and good. And some
contrioutions are lost with regret because they
are good, but not good enough—they would do if
there was nothing better, and there always is
something better. And possibly, sometimes, the
e icor-man makes a mistake; but that is very
rare ; for experience-the habit of handling copy
and the constant reference to the highest stan-
ar s , reed a knowledge, and knack, and judg-
ment wbiGh make it almost impossible to miss a
giHK thing. So the contributor may take courage
r °rp long list of good writers who have begun
in he Bulletin, and be sure that if the divine
v , rea l y ln hi* ll it will not smoulder long un-
noticed. Amen.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
OLD MOGILL.
Have you seen the mirage on Mogill plain?—
How it fades, and passes, and comes again
When the ground is cracked for the want of rain,
And the stock are hopeless dying.
When the phantom water covers all
It takes me back to my father’s hall,
To the broad, deep lake and the waterfall,
And the deer in the bracken lying.
Have you seen the flood in the Mogill creek ?
First it trickles downward a muddy streak ;
A tiny streamlet, impotent, weak,
It goes by the homestead slowly.
But wait a while, and a torrent flows—
A savage stream knowing no repose ;
With wrecks of fences and trees it grows
Till the banks are covered wholly.
Have you seen the cattle on Mogill run
When “cutting out ” for the buyers is done?
Ah, then is the time to be in the fun
With a good stock-horse below you !
What a rush you make thro’ the living lane 1
You are round and after and out again.
And the quick response to the bridle-rein
Shows your nag has learned to know you.
Have you seen the dingo near Mogill rise,
Where the store sheep huddle with frightened
eyes ?
Have you tracked the dead by the cloud of flies
Where the woolly heap lies coldly ?
Have you run him out of the tangled bush,
And seen him come with a snarling rush
To where the reeds grow long and lush
In the swamp he’s tackled boldly ?
Have you driven him close by the ruined hut ?
Severed his tail with a clever cut,
When you’ve dashed out his brains with your
stirrup butt —
Revenge for the lamb he’s eaten.
Or, tired out at the close of day,
Your horse gone lame, he has got away
To the dark scrub grove with his cubs at play—
You must own up that you’re beaten ?
Have you seen a grave in the Mogill yard,
Where a comrade lies whom you spared but hard,
Who could drink a nobbier or play a card,
Or ride right straight to a finish?
We count them over—the rank-and-file ;
We, too, may last but a little while ;
And the slow tear almost has drowned the smile
When we see how the ranks diminish.
Have you said good-bye to the dear old place—
Left for the city and gone the pace,
And forgotten a woman’s waiting face
On the Mogill road at even ?
“ She is better without me, perhaps,” you say ;
Likely as not! She has had her day ;
You have tried and tasted and gone your way,.
And no doubt she has long forgiven.
Of the old-time friends few are lingering still,
For the two-wire fences are sprung at will,
And cookies have overflowed old Mogill
Of the pick of the place they’ve reft her.
Like rats at a cheese they nibble and gnaw—
Select in the garden, for by the law
There is only the house can escape their maw—
And the luck and the land have left her.
Q-
M. BURKINSHAW.
“WHERE THE CAMP-FIRES BURN."
Where the camp-fires burn by the lonely track at
the close of a lonely day,
And the shadows gather again out-back on the
traveller’s dreary way ;
Once more the traveller’s thoughts take wings and
back to the old times fly
And rake from the ashes of dead past things the
life of the years gone by 1
He thinks once more of what should have been—
successes that never came
Of Honor and Love in a brighter scene that sank
in a flood of shame. ,
The reckless follies and years mis-spent when the
star of his life rose high—
The careless pleasures that came and went, and
hopes that were born to die ; „
Of nights in town with the old-time ‘ boys
Women and Cards and Wine !
Ever it is that early joys are drowned in the
tears of brine 1 . _, ... . .
For thoughtless errors in Life s dull track the
wages of sin we earn,
And many a payment is made Out Back to-night
—where the camp-fires burn !
In the glow of the living coals maybe a girl’s fair
face will rise ! . . „
That love of the past in his memory shall linger
until he dies — ~
For a heart’s true love in the early years to the
after years shall cling, _
And bring with its memory bitter tears that no
other thought may bring !
For a prize we lose—with ourselves to blame—we
suffer a keen regret, . . ,
When the pride and passion that died in shame
are strong in the memory yet 1—
And ghosts of the sweetest dreams that died come
back in the waning light;
Through the flick’ring shadows they strangely
glide—where the camp-fires burn to-night.
The camp-fires burn till they slowly die—the em-
bers are charred and black !
And dreams that deep in the dead past lie will
never again come back.
And brightest hopes of the long-ago have long
lain cold and white,
And dead as the ashes that lie below where the
camp-fires blazed to-night !
There are sheep to shear and a cheque to earn ere
the sheds “ cut out ” this year ;
But many a camp-fire yet must burn ere the shear-
ing time is here ! . „
In a howling spree where the shearers sink
their cheques there’s a brief respite
From the haunting Past, for the men who think,
where the camp-fires burn to-night \
White Cliffs Opal Fields.
R. B. HOFFMAN.

Privilege of Shooting Deer.
It was announced a few days ago that an
exalted personage had shot two deer at Cress-
brook. On inquiry in official quarters we
leani that “ permission ” has been
given to the gentleman in question to shoot
three deer. It will be interesting to
many who were enthusiastic in promot-
ing the introduction of deer and other
game, to learn who, save an absolute
and undisputed owner, has power to
grant “permission.”

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor 'will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other A rt icles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission

other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit

paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, MAY 1, 1897.
Federal River Control.
The argument which arose in the Conven-
tion as to Federal or provincial control of
Australian rivers, and the reasons adduced
for either course, and the unsatisfactory com-
promise finally arrived at, were eminently
characteristic. All the parties concerned in
the trouble alleged that they spoke and
voted according to their unselfish convic-
tions. Nevertheless it was noticeable that
the representatives of the provinces which
had no river-systems dropped almost entirely
out of the debate, and those personally in-
terested in the one river system which is
large enough to be worth calling a system
fought for their own hands exclusively, and
their convictions and their interests ran to-
gether. South Australia demanded that any
river running through more than one pro-
vince should be placed under Federal con-
trol along with its affluents, and that the
Commonwealth should superintend naviga-
tion and, if necessary, allot out its waters
for irrigation purposes among all the por-
vinces concerned. New South Wales, and,
in a less degree, Victoria, claimed that each
province should absolutely control that por-
tion of the rivers which lay in its own
territory ; that it should have absolute power
to draw off all the water for irriga-
tion purposes if it chose and leave no
river at all for the people of the province
below ; and that it should have power to
block any waters within its own territory,
and shut off river commerce with the ad-
jacent province if it felt so inclined, or could
advantage itself by so doing. New South
Wales, having the Lachlan, Murrumbidgee,
Edwards, Darling, Culgoa, Warrego, Namoi,
Glwydir, Macquarie, and at least a half-
interest in the Murray as its share of the
Murray system, was naturally in deadly
earnest as an advocate of the provincial view
of things, and claimed the right to use all
these rivers as it pleased, with no considera-
tion whatever for the South Australians down
below. Victoria was mostly on the same
side, but rather less in earnest, because it
has less water at stake. South Australia, as
the province whose water-supply these two
states claimed the right to cut off, and whose
river commerce they demanded authority to
block, naturally took the opposite view. It
was finally resolved, as a compromise, that
the Murray, so far as it formed the boundary
between New South Wales and Victoria,
should be under Federal control, but that all
its affluents should be state property. This
doesn’t definitely settle the vexed question
either way.
* * * *
The Bulletin, which firmly believes in
giving the Federal Government the benefit
of the doubt wherever there is a conflict be-
tween it and the provincial authorities,
reckons that the balance of logic is in favor
of the South Australian view of the case.
In other words, it holds that, where a river
flows in the territory of more than one state,
it and all its tributaries should be Federal
property. At the same time, it doesn’t sup-
pose for a moment that the South Australian
delegates took the particular view they did
for any other reason than that their interests
lay that way, or that they were one fraction
more patriotic than their neighbors. The
question of river control is not an urgent
concern just now, but in the future—possibly
in the near future—it is almost certain to
become a matter of the greatest importance.
So far as commerce is concerned, the rivers,
in this paper’s opinion, matter comparatively
little, and will by-and-bye matter nothing at
all. If the drought-cursed interior of Aus-
tralia is ever to carry a large and prosperous
population, instead of a thin sprinkling of
people living constantly on the verge of ruin
and famine, it can only be by irrigation on a
stupendous scale. And there is so much
land to irrigate and so little water to do it
with, that the time will almost certainly come
when, in ordinary seasons, all the contents
of the Murray and all its tributaries will fie
used for this purpose, and nothing will reach
the sea at all. The navigation question will
die a natural death for want of anything to
navigate, and the only matter worth con-
sidering as a permanent problem is the use
of the river system for irrigation purposes
and for the supply of the population which
will be by-and-bye.
• * * *
The Murray, Murrumbidgee, Lachlan,
Edwards, Darling, Culgoa, Warrego, Cam-
paspe, &c.,- are all one system, and have a
common outlet to the sea through South
Australian territory. This system forms the
only permanent water-supply of a small part
of Queensland and a large portion of New
South Wales, Victoria, and South Australia.
Considering the dire scarcity which often
prevails over this great tract of country, the
inhabitants are much in the situation of a
crowd of castaways on a raft in mid-ocean
with a limited water-supply to divide among
them—or at least they will be in that situa-
tion when irrigation works are established
on the large scale which the country requires,
and they almost certainly will be so estab-
lished sooner or later. Then the problem to
be decided is whether the supply shall be
divided as fairly as possible by the central
authority, or whether every one shall grab
what he can get and the one who is left shall
get none.
* * * *
If Australia is to be one nation, then the
only fair policy is Federal control and a fair
division of the precious asset. If it is still
to consist of five hostile states, and Federa-
tion is not to mean the making of a nation
after all, then the provincial policy of grab is
quite legitimate. In an ordinary dry season
it would be quite possible, under a complete
irrigation system such as this country re-
quires, for Queensland, New South Wales
and Victoria to use up the whole water-
supply of the great river system. Queens-
land would impound only a little of it about
the head waters of the Warrego, Culgoa and
Macintyre, and its share would not be worth
mentioning. New South Wales would get
the lion’s share, and Victoria would also get
a very fair proportion in the north and north-
western shires. S.A. would receive none,
and while the two neighboring provinces
grew prosperous and amazingly fertile, it
would be left in its present state of sterility.
Assuming that the idea of Federation is that
each province is still to go on grabbing all it
can at the expense of its neighbor, and ruin-
ing its neighbor all it knows how for its
own benefit, then the latter idea is perfectly
fair—up to a certain point. Even under the
narrowest idea of provincialism it is only
good up to a certain point.
* * * * *
Despite the almost continuous drought
curse even Australia has its flood seasons.
It is only a few years since Wodonga (Vic.)
was almost washed away, and the outlying
cornstalk town of Bourke was an islet rising
out of a howling waste of waters. On these
occasions all the irrigation systems that the
Murray watershed could hold would not
absorb the water-supply, and it must flow
away through South Australia. Therefore,
the purely provincial idea is this: The
waters of the Murray system, down to
the South Australian boundary, belong
exclusively to New South Wales, Vic-
toria, and Queensland. In dry seasons,
when South Australia wants water, they
have an absolute right to impound all the
streams east of the boundary line, and leave
the adjacent province without. In wet
seasons, when South Australia doesn’t want
water, they have an absolute right to let the
floods go through its territory and drown out
the low-lying lands along the Murray banks,
and swamp South Australian townships to
any extent that chance may dictate. South
Australia, with the best possible reason,
kicks violently against this arrangement. If
that province isn’t to have any claim to the
Murray waters when they are much needed,
it should not be bound to be a receptacle for
the Murray floods when they are not needed
and only cause disaster. If South Australia
has no right to use Victoria and New South
Wales as a catchment area, they have no
better right to use South Australia as a
storm-water channel. Supposing the pro-
vincial view had prevailed, and New South
Wales and Victoria had established an abso-
lute claim to use all the waters of the Murray
system as far as the point where the three
provinces meet, then it would obviously be
also their duty to dig a new channel in their
own territories to carry off flood waters to the
sea, instead of letting them loose on the ter-
ritories of a neighbor to whom, by their own
logic, these waters don’t belong. New South
Wales, having asserted its right to impound
for its own sole use all the Murray’s nor-
thern tributaries, and it being decided that
South Australia has no claim whatever on
these tributaries, is even now under a moral
obligation to do this so far as they are con-
cerned. Having laid down the law that
South Australia is not entitled to derive
benefit from these waters, it is equally logi-
cal that South Australia should not be incon-
venienced by them when they break loose, as
they do now and then.
* * * »
If Australians are to be one nation, with
common interests, then the whole Murray sys-
tem—the only interprovincial system in this
country—should be under Federal control.
It should be kept open for navigation, so far
as possible, by the Commonwealth Govern-
ment. When a large irrigation system is
established, and the available water is limited,
the supply should be allotted out as fairly as
possible among all the provinces interested.
If there is enough to water all the land cap-
able of irrigation, or all of it that is under ir-
rigation at the moment, all along the main
river and its affluents, then there "will be no
need for interference. If the water avail-
able is only, say, 75 per cent, of what is
needed at any giyen time, then the amount
permitted to be withdrawn should be reduced
one-quarter all round, and the agriculturist
on the upper reaches of the river
should have no preference over the
one furthest down. The theory that
each province should be allowed to make
what use it pleases of its “ natural advan-
tages ” is not only anti-Federal, but con-
trary to all the usages of civilisation. On
the same basis the large man should have
full authority to use his natural advantages
and assault the small one ; and the province
which produces a military genius should be
duly authorised to use the natural advantage
thus created to loot the province which has
no military genius ; and everything should
go, in a chaotic sort of fashion, to the
luckiest and the strongest.
Wanted, a Policy.
The N.S.W. Protectionists have been holding
a conference in Sydney. All the old party
hacks were present, and all the old posturing
mediocrities and deadbeats, and smellful, dis-
credited blatherskites, who hang on to the
National policy gathered together to whop the
drum in exactly the same strain as they have
done any time for ten years past. The meeting
read papers to itself about Protection. It de
livered long speeches to itself on the same sub-
ject. It threw great masses of statistics and
facts and evidence about Protection at its own
head. It proved to itself that Protection was
coming, and it shook hands with itself over the
good times that would necessarily ensue. It
passed votes of thanks to itself for its loyal
adherence to Protection, and it advertised itself
with a weird, wild yelp as the great Protec-
tionist party, and nothing else. W. J. Lyne,
leader of the National cause in Parliament,
stated that if he were returned to power he
would introduce Protection—of which fact
everybody was already aware. For the rest,
he refused flatly to state what his policy was,
or if he had any policy at all. As no one else
authoritatively jumped his place and offered a
policy, the situation is that the N.S.W. Pro-
tectionist leaders ask the country to take them
on trust—to “go it blind” on them as far as
concerns everything except their one hackneyed
theme. And, though the country has every
reason to be very tired of Foreign-trade, it is
hardly so tired as to give Lyne and Co. an ab-
solutely free hand in everything except Pro-
tection. It is too large an order.
* * * .
The N.S.W. Protectionist leaders, by con-
stantly putting themselves forward as men
with only one idea—Protection—and by misre-
presenting their party as a party of one idea
are doing their very best to reduce the
Protectionist body to the hopeless position of
the Single-taxers or the Prohibitionists or any
other one-fad gathering. No sane community
will ever put into power a party with only one
visible idea. It won’t trust itself to a leader
who states distinctly what he will do as regards
one essential question of the day, and offers no
explanation at all as to what he is o'oinc' to do
as regards the other 999 equally essential ques-
tions. Before it puts Mr. Lyne into power it
wauts him to show' some definite and progressive
views about the liquor-question, state banking
state paper-money monopoly, state fire and life
insurance, old-age pensions, minimum wage,
regulation of the hours of labor, irrigation
foreign borrowing, local borrowing or no bor-
rowing at all, absentee-tax, graduated land-tax-
graduated income-tax, increase or decrease of
the exemptions under the present taxes
exclusion of aliens, state land bank, bettemient,
thinm, Bo ' and many scores of other
tnings. It isn t necessary that he should hold
-x reme views about these matters ; if his views
aie even moderately liberal and are clear,
definite, and business-like he will at least be a
man with a complete policy, and even a very
moderate kind of policy gets a chance sooner or
later in the whirligig of politics. But h e
insists on posing as a person who has no policy
at all beyond Protection, and who is too barren
in the head to formulate one. And, unluckily
he makes a large section of the public believe
that his party is like himself.
* * * *
The mental sterility of Lyne is fast driving
intellectual men away from any open associa-
tion with the cause which is cursed by his sup-
port. To be a Protectionist in New South
Wales means being a follower of Lyne or at
least being regarded as such. Being a follower
of Lyne means approval of his policy and a
desire to lift him into power. And no intelli-
gent individual will undertake to approve of
Lyne’s policy, seeing that he absolutely refuses
to state what it is, save in one particular—an
important particular, certainly, but still onlv
one. Also, no person of brains and character
will pledge himself to help to lift Lyne into
power, seeing that no one knows what Lyne is
going to do w'ith the power when he gets it—
except in so far as concerns the one weary par-
ticular aforementioned. The man of one idea
is naturally followed most closely by men of
one idea —hence the gathering of cheap cranks
and mediocrities and notoriety-hunters who
loomed forth so conspicuously at the recent
Conference.
* * * »
The N.S.W. Foreign-trade party can’t and
doesn’t attempt to stand on Foreign trade
alone. It recognises that a man can’t be fully
clothed with only a boot lace, no matter how
excellent in its own sphere that bootlace may
be. It has kept in office by establishing direct
taxation, by civil service reform, by a liberal
land policy, by promising local government and
half-promising a State Bank, and by faking the
public accounts and lying about its surplus. If
it had professed to have no policy beyond
Foreign-trade it would probably never Lave
got into office at all, or if it had got in it would
have gone out again with i emai kable sudden-
ness. Lyne’s policy of being a Protectionist
and nothing more not only makes him impos-
sible ; it is also fast making his party impos-
sible until it finds a new leader. •
* * * •
Ten years ago the Protectionist party in
N.S. W. was distinctly the party of pro-
gress. It was the main support of every
great idea and of everything which was not
old and stupid and fossilised. Then Premier
Reid systematically borrowed its ideas, and as
Lyne’s only mode of leading an Opposition was
to oppose everything that was done by the
Ministry, he allowed himself to be jockeyed
into opposing, or at best giving a grudging
assent to, every reform which came under con-
sideration. No one save an eminently stupid
man could have drifted into a situation of the
kind. Premier Reid’s Liberalism was of such
a milk-and-water character at the best that a
clever opponent could easily have posed
as the progressive man of the two, and
shown up Reid as a mere apology for a re-
former. When the Premier brought in his
Income-tax Bill the leader of the progressive
parly should have demanded a heavier impost
on large incomes than on small ones, and a
larger tax on incomes derived from property
than from those realised by personal exertion.
When the land-tax came on the scene he might
have held up Reid to derision for his feebleness
and cowardice, and his pandering to the rich,
and his oppression of the poor, by making the
man with £5OO worth of property pay at the
same rate as the man with ,£50,000 worth, by
his failure to especially tax the absentee, and
to levy an extra impost on the land speculator,
as distinguished from the man who puts the
soil to profitable use. He might have forced
Reid to keep moving with his State Bank idea
—now apparently defunct. He might have
made himself and his party the advocates of
state insurance, old age pensions, and other re-
forms which the N.S. W. Premier, so far, has not
dared to touch ; and he might have created a
new political movement by laying it down as a
cast-iron law that the return of W. J. Lyne to
office as Premier meant the absolute stoppage
of the borrowing policy, and the construction
of all public works out of revenue. But, instead
of going one better than his feeble opponent, he
missed his opportunity every time.
* * ♦ *
On every'separate point, the chances of the
game—if he had only known how to play it —
were with Lyne. His fiscal policy is essentially
the policy of the artisan, the laborer, the small
farmer and the poor in general. It is also the
policy of the rich manufacturer, but that indi-
vidual doesn’t exist as yet in N.S.W. Free-
trade, on the other hand, is the policy of the
wealthy importer, the city magnate, the specu-
lator, and generally of the classes which are
not dependent on their daily toil. If Lyne, as
the representative of the poor, had demanded
an extra graduated tax on large incomes and on
incomes not obtained by labor, and an extra
impost on large estates and estates that are not
profitably used, and a crushing impost on
absentees, he would have placed Premier Reid
between the alternatives of alienating his
friends, the rich importers, by his radicalism,
or his other supporters, the Labor-members, by
his conservatism. If he had supported State-
banking, State-insurance, a State monopoly of
note-issues and a National Land Bank, Premier
Reid must either have thrown over his friends,
the city capitalists, by assenting, or his other
friends, the Labor-members, by a refusal.
Reid, supported one-half by the very rich and
the-other half by the representatives of the
very poor, had to steer a middle-course and to
live on compromises. Lyne, with no rich sup-
porters to propitiate, might have been wholly
democratic, and in so doing he could have
shown, by contrast, what a poor apology for a
democrat his rival was. And, instead of this,
he allowed his party—the party of the masses—
to drift into a tacit alliance with the Fat Men
of the Council, by which it gained nothing and
lost everything, and to pose as the party of
wealth without having any wealth.
* * * »
What the N.S. W. Protectionist party might
be under Lyne, if Lyne were not Lyne, may be
ascertained by considering what the same party


is in Victoria under Turner, in South Aus-
tralia under Kingston, in Maoriland under
Seddon. In these provinces it is the party of
progress and radical reform, and its policy is
associated with everything that is opposed to
fossildom and stupidity and bigotry and re-
action and senility. And in New South Wales
it is only the party which meets together and
throws at its own head great slabs of statistics
about exports and imports—and reads papers
to itself about exports and imports—and holds
conferences at which it talks about nothing but
exports and imports—and drifts.
The Equal-State-Representation-in-the
Senate-Question.
Mil Thomas Ewing, M.L.A., comments on
Mr. James Ashton’s Bulletin article on equal
State-Representation in the Federal Senate :
The real difficulties in the path of Australian Union are
so trifling that the ingenuity of our leading men has been
seriously taxed to conjure up phantoms (the term
“ Lions ” does not meet the case) which may delay Union.
« * * *
Mr. Ashton is usually both fair and logical. Why does
he not state the case justly? Under the proposed bi-
cameral Federal system, Tasmania, he estimates, will have
four members and N.S. Wales 42 members in the House
of Representatives, therefore, in that Chamber—if
numerical strength means power—N.S. Wales will have
more than ten times the power of Tasmania.
In the Senate, with a recognition of State Rights, these
provinces will have equal power. How can he fairly con-
tend that one-tenth of the power in one Chamber and
equal power in the other is tantamount to equal power in
the Government of United Australia? Such contention
can only be supported by the inference that the House of
Representatives will have no power at all. A < lance at
the proposed Constitution Bill, or that of ’9l, relutes this
at once —even if we were not furnished with a direct
denial by the experience of other federations. Therefore,
the basis of Mr. Ashton’s contention—that equal repre-
sentation in the Senate carries with it equal power in the
Federal Government—is incorrect.
* * * *
Under Federation, the State Legislatures will continue
to control matters of local importance, while the Federal
Parliament will be supreme in subjects of national
moment. Therefore, presumably, it is with regard to
National affairs that there is any probability of conflict
between the two Chambers. The anticipated friction
cannot be of serious importance, if the type of men in the
Senate be not widely different from those in the House of
Representatives. YVill such be the case ?
The members of both Chambers will be elected
under a system of manhood suffrage. Is it reasonable to
conjecture that the men elected by the people to the
Senate will be mean-spirited provincialists, and those
elected to the House of Representatives generous-minded
statesmen? If Mr. Ashton s contention be correct, the
same people will choose for one Chamber men pre-
pared to sacrifice Australian interests to localism, and for
the other reputable and broad-spirited nationalists.
The probability is that the people will choose much the
same type of men for both Chambers ; if this be so, al-
though there may be differences of opinion, the danger
of any serious crisis will be remote.
* * * *
Turning to another aspect of Mr. Ashton’s letter : If
some of the provinces are infested by an inferior type of
men, and the mother province possesses all the patriotism
and liberalism of the group, N.S.W. had better treat such
people as vermin, and begin a war of extermination
against the smaller states forthwith.
Mr. Ashton instances the Black Labor question. What
possible justification can he have for the assumption that
Senators representing such a state as South Australia
would vote for black labor? Is it not absolutely certain
that they would not do so?
It will not be contended that small states cannot be
democratic, therefore the Senators from the small states
will not be found delaying legitimate reform ; thus the
whole question of friction sinks into such a question as
instanced by Mr. Ashton, whether South Australian
Senators would be prepared to sacrifice the defence of
Australia in order to obtain a bogus expenditure in Wes-
tralia in hopes that the Westralian Senators would re-
ciprocate.
Does Mr. Ashton contend I hat Mr. Kingston, if elected
as a Senator, would enter into a rascally plot in opposition
to the public opinion of Australia, and in antagonism to
the true interests of the Commonwealth, in order to obtain
a useless expenditure in Adelaide ?
Mr. Ashton’s whole argument rests on the presumed im-
becility, fraud and disloyalty, not of the smaller states,
but of elected members of the Federal Parliament of
Australia.
The small states may be united in claiming equal state
rights, but there the mutual interest ceases and therefore
the combination disappears. To imagine Senator King-
ston voting for Black Labor because he happened to be
the representative of a small state is absurd.
* * * *
Mr. Ashton asks in conclusion : “ Hovr can a federation
last if state rights be recognised?”
Can he instance a federation without such recognition ?
Has not every attempt, at federation among similarly-
situated peoples failed without some such arrangement as
that which he condemns ?
• * • »
The phantom of Federal railway control is conjured up
because provincialists desire to make trade flow to ports
which are not the legitimate outlet for certain areas.
Attempts to make water run up hill usually fail.
The phantom of the Federal Capital has received a
serious check from the fact that it might mean the loss of
state prestige.
The phantom of state rights must also vanish if so able
a man as Mr. Ashton can find no tenable position from
which to demonstrate the possibility of any serious
danger.
Tuos. Ewing.
Legislative Assembly, Sydney, 23/4/97.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
N.S.W. National Art Gallery.
Editor Bulletin, —The N.S. W. National Art
Gallery has become a great public institution.
Nominally vested in trustees, the varied, and in
many instances, beautiful objects which adorn
its walls and floor space are in reality the pro-
perty of the people. No one can refuse its
directing power the credit of properly organis-
ing the daily work of the gallery. Its contents,
such as they are, have been judiciously housed,
tended, and displayed, and the interprovincial
picture exchanges, now a recognised feature,
must have considerably taxed the time, intelli-
gence, and energy both of the administrative and
executive sections. Nevertheless, the trustees
seem to have fallen into a frame of dangerous
contentment with a mediocre and perfunctory
discharge of the larger obligation of selection,
under stress of which they have almost ceased
to be conscious that the charge of wasted means
and neglected opportunities lies at their door.
For several years past their purchases have
mostly consisted of heroic canvasses of the
flashy order on the one hand, and mere triviali-
ties of the furniture-picture type on the other.
There would be no difficulty in selecting about
the area of a moderate sized back-garden of
painted canvasses from our N.S.W. pictorial
assets which would make the sorriest exchange
for Peter Graham’s single work “Glencoe
after the Massacre,” adorning the Melb. section
of the loan exhibits now on view in the Domain.
In the Daily Telegraph, the other day, Mr.
Sidney Long wrote that the artist is the only
rue critic of art, just as the lawyer is the only
rue critic in legal matters, or the doctor of
medicine. What is true of the art critic is
presumably equally true of the art buyer or
selector. Well, there have *been some few
occasions when the functions of eminent
professional artists and picture purchasers have
found themselves united under one set of hats.
The S.A. Gallery, when it first began buying in
London, spurned the idea of being represented
by common persons. Sir Frederic Leighton,
with, I think, two other academicians, was the
selector. But the selections unhappily proved
unsatisfactory, and the gifted selectors found
themselves rather promptly unshipped. In
later years Professor Herkomer was a buyer—
possibly he is one to this day—for Victoria.
The Professor’s reign was coincident—whether
post hoc or propter hoc is uncertain —with the
acquisition by the Vic. Gallery of a portrait of
Queen Victobia by “ Hubert Herkomer,
R.A.,” for w'hich, I believe, 1000 guineas were
paid. And to look on Queen Victoria as ren-
dered by the honored Professor makes a con-
siderable pull on my reserve of loyalty. Again,
artists, as well as laymen interested in art
matters, know something of the administration
of the “ Chantrey Bequest ” in England. It is
wholly controlled by the Royal Academy. The
annual purchases made from it are made ex-
clusively by Academicians. And I rather fancy
it would make an intelligent Londoner “smile
loudly ” were Mr. Sidney Long to assure him
that the selections were necessarily the best
that could be made, or that in some noted
instances they were nbt almost the worst.
From an abstract standpoint it would not seem
an extravagant proposition that an accomplished
professional artist should of all others be the
safest guide in picture-buying ; but it is one
which has not stood the strain of working ex-
perience. In the British market circumstances
which would never occur to people arguing
from surface probabilities in our own smaller
sphere are in constant, although perhaps tacit,
operation to thwart the natural play of effort
and motive. To put it plainly, the platitu-
dinarian references to the function of the art
critic are nothing better than an impertinence.
They have their origin in the same kind of brain
as that w r hich finds the literary critic inadequate
because he has not written a book; or that
which pronounces Macaulay’s Essays worthless
because when they appeared their author had
not written his History of England. So much,
then, for the view that w'orks once selected for
N.S.W. Art Gallery thereby become sacrosanct
—only to be commented upon by the outsider
with bated breath and in a spirit of whispering
humbleness. I may point out, in concluding,
that it is a most important question whether
Sydney is to be the recognised domicile of the
worthiest exhibits, or whether the Domain
collection is merely to be regarded as a con-
venient feeder for the supply of loan pictures
to the inland towns. This is the question sug-
gested by the N.S.W. statistician’s figures for
1894 and 1895—those for 1890 not being yet
available. In these years, while £3388 was
spent on works of art, the outlay upon wages,
salaries, frames, freight, insurance, repairs and
sundries totalled £4106; truly a portentous
proportion of sack to daily bread.—C.M.S.
4
Heaven and the Hat.
The latest prayer-romancist is Rev. Taylor,
of St. Stebhen’s, Newtown, Sydney—to com-
mence the building of which tabernacle the
Lord, he says, provided the £lOOO for which a
“ few of the workers joined their clergyman in
daily prayer.” At the end of ten weeks, at all
events, promises had been received representing
£957 165., after which it looks as if Heaven either
couldn’t lay its hands upon the odd £42 ss. or
considered it had done as much as could reason-
ably be expected from a Providence with so
many calls upon it. After another eight weeks,
however, when the flock held a meeting to
consider whether it was quite the square
deal to shut down upon them for such a
trifle, it turned out that “ God had really given
£57 16s. more than was needed.” Why this
discrepancy was not respectfully pointed out to
the Deity doesn’t appear ; perhaps the faithful
didn’t want to take any chances about having
to part-up again. But, in any case, The Bul-
letin wants to know why they didn’t pray for
what they needed, ready-made. To supplicate
for a church on Saturday, and to have a brand-
new edifice dumped dowui, all ready for service,
on Sunday morning, would go far to convince
even this ungodly paper, which has, however,
in the meantime, to remain blasphemously
observant of the fact that nothing ever happens
“in answer to prayer” that couldn’t just as
well have happened in the ordinary, non-
supplicatory course of events.
Another Financial Compromise.
After much infuriated conversation, the Con-
vention abandoned its original scheme for
dividing the Federal surplus among the states,
and built up a new one on a simpler basis. The
new plan, which originated from Premier Reid,
rightly assumes, as did the old one, that under a
uniform customs tariff the various states of the
Commonwealth will ultimately get to the same,
or practically the same, level, and the contri-
butions per head of population will be about
equal all round. It also follows the idea of the
financial committee in assuming that the level
will be arrived at in five years, and after that
the surplus can be distributed on a population
basis ; but instead of keeping accounts and dis-
tributing the surplus with mathematical
accuracy during the first five years, it is now
proposed to keep accounts for one year only
and average the rest. Supposing the first year’s
returns show that the average customs
and excise revenue for the year is £2
per head, but that N.S. Wales yields 50s.
Victoria 30s. and S.A. 355. per head, the surplus
will be distributed for the first year on that
basis. Next year no separate accounts will be
taken, but the N.S.W. revenue will be esti-
mated at 48s. per head ; Victorian revenue at
325., and S.A. at 365., and the surplus will be
divided accordingly. Third year it will be
divided on the assumption that N.S.W. con-
tributes 465. ; Victoria, 345. ; S.A., 375. Fourth
year the estimate will be N.S. VV., 445. ; Vic-
toria, 365. ; S.A., 38s. Fifth year the assump-
tion will be that N.S.W’. contributes 425. ;
Victoria, 38s. ; S.A., 395. ; and after that it
will be taken for granted that the tariff pro-
duces an equal amount per head in all the
states, and the surplus will be divided on a
population basis. N.S. Wales, at all events,
should come remarkably well out of this ar-
rangement. The first year of the uniform tariff
that province will almost certainly supply
more revenue per head than either Victoria,
S.A. or Tasmania, because it has fewer local
industries and is more dependent on imports.
The scheme assumes that it will take five years
for the discrepancy to vanish, and for five
years N.S. W. will get a larger share of the
surplus than it is entitled to on a mere popula-
tion basis. The experience of the Dibbs tariff,
however, shows that the inequality should
practically vanish andN.S.W.’s revenue contri-
bution get down to the level of its neighbors’
in less than four years, so that for the last year
or two of the arrangement N.S. W. should be
making a small profit out of its neighbors.
Still, the average is as near to the probable
reality as anyone is likely to get, and any dis-
crepancy will be more than covered by the
saving in book - keeping, profanity, ink,
stationery, salaries of clerks and borders customs
officers, &c. As the resolve not to federate the
railways and public debts makes a surplus in-
evitable, and as the surplus must be distributed,
Reid’s scheme is probably quite as good as any
other.
Tlie Defence of the Empire.
London Spectator has hit upon a scheme, to
which the Argus gives a timid approval, for
commemorating “ the present year of Jubilee.”
It appears that a petition that the Royal Cana-
dian Regiment should be resuscitated and domi-
ciled at Toronto has been signed by “ almost
every man of distinction in Canada,” and the
suggestion is that this should be accompanied—
By the permanent location of regiments of the Imperial
army in Australia, Maoriland, and Cape Colony. Local
enlistment would be the desideratum and would be fos-
tered as much as possible. The main intention is to
arouse in the colonies an active and practical interest in
the British army.
And the Argus thinks that—
From the colonial point of view it looks as if this would
prove a very economical method of partially discharging
obligations in connection with the military defence of the
Empire.
It is, of course, too much to assert that if this
plan were carried out the regiments would find
no one in Australia willing to recruit. We are un-
fortunately not without a certain class of persons
whose exotic and bastard patriotism delights in
seeing their country dragged at the tail of the
“ Hempire.” And doubtless many “men of
distinction ” in Australia covet the vicarious
glory of inducing other people to spill their
gore for “ Hingland.” But the average Aus-
tralian doesn’t feel that way. When he is
roused to take an interest in such matters at
all, his regret is not so much that he is unable
to further these benevolent schemes as that he
is afforded no reasonable facilities for kicking
the persons who invent them. And the sug-
gestion that the people of Australia are
oppressed with the sense of their obligations in
the way of Imperial defence which their unfor-
tunate impecuniosity prevents them from dis-
charging, and that they would be only too
happy to supply the blood and bones if John
Bull would find the money, is calculated to
make even the casual Australian scratch his
head and think. And when he starts thinking
he is apt to reflect that this much-enduring
country has for years been paying a huge naval
tribute for a squadron over which it has not
the shadow of control, and which, if we ever
should require it, will probably be at the
Bosphorus or Vladivostock or some other
equally irrelevant locality. For years past the
British army has failed to attract to its ranks
any but the most underfed, weedy, and shift-
less members of the population. And the sug-
gestion that a billet that is too mean and
miserable to attract an Englishman, an Irish-
man, or a Scotchman, should be offered to the
Australians is calculated to fill the people of
this country with gratitude and loyal fervor.
The “ unco’ guid ” Prof. Harper, of Mel-
bourne, on the act of the Federal Convention in
refusing to make a formal recognition of God
Almighty :
We are in truth working towards a state of things in
which another Wkrner., proclaiming himself the enemy of
God and of mercy, might arise, or perhaps more probably
a Napoleon of finance, who would use all the machinery
of free government to debase the will of the nation, to
bring the foul stain of monetary corruption into every
department of the state, and to exploit the souls and
bodies of the poor in the interest of his dividends.
In fact, if we do not pass an Act admitting that
God is, and insert an apology for previous
neglect in all the metropolitan papers, He will
send down, not a devouring pestilence, but
another Boom, and may the Devil have mercy
on our souls. Professor Harper should explain
how it happens that the land-boom crowd of
his own city were all GoD-fearing men who
made cheerful acknowledgment of the Al-
mighty in every prospectus, and never once
forgot Him when they had a good thing on.
Really, it is not so much an acknowledgment
of God that is desired as a recognition of the
parsons. A man need not recognise God so
long as he admits the clergy.
In Melbourne Practice Court, the other week,
Justice Hood smashed up the rotten old legal
fiction that Judges never read newspapers or
have any knowledge of current events outside
the court. The matter in hand was an appli-
cation by Colonel J. M. Templeton for leave to
transfer to a locum tenens sundry liquidating
jobs, whilst he (the plump warrior) went to
Hingland for the benefit of his health and to
“consult” with other liquidators. The affi-
davit in support of the application was a dis-
gustingly uncandid document. It stated in
effect that Templeton would probably die of
fatigue if he didn’t get a rest from the labor
of winding up those dead companies on whose
carcases he has been officially fastened for many
years, and Justice Hood’s gorge rose at the
thought of attempting to swallow such a
large quantity of balderdash. So his Honor
admitted, with an apologetic cough, that
he happened to be aware of a fact not
stated in the affidavit—viz., that Templeton
was going to England in command of a rifle
team. He had even read of a farewell banquet
being given to the said Templeton, in his
military capacity. Practically, Justice Hood
declared himself an up-to-date person who knew
quite well that Templeton seemed anxious to
retain some of his financial pickings whilst he
swaggered ’Ome with the rifle team, but not dis-
posed to tell the whole story in Court lest it
might appear rather scandalous. Justice Hood
went through a form of refusing Templeton’s
application until further facts were brought
under his learned notice. The missing
facts doubtless arrived in due course, and The
Bulletin doesn’t suppose that anything came
of his Honor’s little protest in this case, except
more costs. All the same, there is much com-
fort in the reflection that a Judge has—for the
first time on record, as far as The Bulletin
knows—allowed his actual knowledge of plain
facts to overrule his bogus judicial ignorance
concerning everything which doesn’t happen to
be put in evidence. By degrees the Bench may
work up to the frank and free system of law-
giving that flourishes just now in the remote
mining settlements of W. A. Way up there
the local magistrate always brings his personal
knowledge of any crime to bear upon the ad-
ministration of justice, and when a guilty
prisoner pleads “ Aot guilty ” to stealing a bit
of beef, the “ beak,” who has witnessed the
larceny, denounces him as a blanky liar.
And when this journal remembers how utterly
ignorant of facts Justice Hood used to be,
when dealing with a certain class of criminal
cases, this journal feels inclined to compliment
his Honor upon his newly-acquired acquaintance
with things generally known.
It is seriously proposed to reproduce in the
Commonwealth one of the worst features of the
Victorian Constitution by making the Senate a
continuous body, not liable to dissolution, and
with half the members retiring every three
years. The result of this system is that the
Senate will never be up to date ; at its best,
half the members will represent the sentiment
of to-day and the other half the sentiment of
three years ago; at its worst, one-half will
voice the popular feeling of two years and
eleven months back and the other half of five
years and eleven months back. Sudden up-
heavals of popular sentiment won’t reach it,
therefore it will grow steadily out of touch with
popular sentiment. If it grossly betrays its
trust, the people can’t rise up there and
then and call it to account; the best they can
do is to call half of it to account at a certain
fixed date, perhaps a long way ahead, when the
betrayal is ancient history, and the popular feel-
ing has grown cold. It will never have a whole
election—only a half one, and the average man
takes about as little interest in half an election
as he does in being half-married to a-quarter of
his wife. If the Senate has outraged popular
confidence and the public can reply by shifting
it bodily, the public will go in with a will and
take a live interest in politics ; if the public
can only half shift the obnoxious institution the
general feeling is that it is hardly worth while.
The only way to arouse a general interest in
the halves system is to split every senator
accurately across the middle with an axe, and
let his head and ribs remain in office while his
legs expire by effiuxion of time and go up for
re-election. That method of doing things
might arouse a good deal of excitement, as well
as make a great splash on the floor, but no
other mode of retiring the Senate by instal-
ments seems to hold out much promise.
♦
TheQ. Commissioner of Police, Parry-Okeden,
concerning over 100 blacks among whom he
distributed tobacco at Norman ton :
They were the most miserable, disease-stricken wretches
I ever saw, but I was assured these were “ kings and
queens” compared with those to the south-west, and
further along the coast west of and around Burketown.
I saw one leper who was segregated under police super-
vision near the town. His gin had followed her man
from their own country, 50 miles away, and was camped
quite close to him. She was in a fearful condition from
syphilis.
Mr. Parry-Okeden’s recommendations are the
very opposite to those made by Archibald
Meston. The latter would abolish the native
police ; the former proposes to strengthen the
detachments, but, instead of preventing the
native troopers from having any communication
with the myalls, the latter are to be approached
in a friendly manner, but with due caution.
The myall is to be gradually educated up to
the idea that the native trooper is his best
friend ; that, if he is hungry and game is
scarce, he can always get food at the
police camp ; and that, if a white man wrongs
or harasses a black man, the white man will be
punished as a black man would be. The police-
commissioner’s best recommendation is that a
Government doctor should be attached
police camp, to practise among the aborigines
only. He also advocates the forming of more
mission stations, and boldly tells his chief that,
instead of economising, lie wants nearly £2500
a year more. He extenuates the spearing of
cattle on the ground of a natural craving for
animal food, and because the blacks don’t rightly
understand that cattle are “property,” but
look upon them as “ food natural, and as their
lawful prey.” Parry-Okeden snuffs out the
idea of establishing a white police in this con-
nection. He would need teachers, expert bush-
men understanding the blacks and their cus-
toms and ideas, habits and superstitions, to
train up young men for the new force ; and—
To find even a few such men, it would be necessary to
recruit from the stations in the far North—that is, from a
place and from a class where and among whom, at the
present time, are to be found, masquerading under white
and yellow skins, some of the blackest scoundrels alive—
wretches who have wrought deeds of appalling wicked-
ness and cruelty, and who think it equal good fun to shoot
a nigger at sight, or to ravish a gin. So long as such
villains escape hanging and live in our country, the blacks
must be—and shall be, if I have a free hand and my
native police—protected.
Postmaster-General Duffy and Bard Fitz
Gibbon are reported to be candidates for the Vic.
F. Gibbon, however, is hope-
less ; his awful failure when he went to London
to float the Board of Works loan shows that he
isn’t a business man, and his poetry shows that he
isn’t a poet, and he has no qualifications for the
place at all. As for Duffy, his qualification is that
he is the worst possible frost as P.M.G., and to
make him A.-G. would be an affable way of drop-
ping him overboard. But Duffy would be almost
a worse Agent-General- than Gillies. The best
thing to do with Duffy is to make him a Governor.
