The luck of the house of Elphberg sticks
well by Her Majesty’s, and Anthony Hope’s dis-
sipated monarch is rescued from his dungeon every
night in the presence of a profitable audience.
The pageants all go smoothly, and the co. wears
its lovely clothes with calm assurance and does its
part remarkably well; and the grizzled old
Colonel, who still excites this paper’s especial
reverence, drives his conspiracy through every
night in a hard, remorseless fashion which makes
him the most thoroughly realistic feature in the
show. Anthony Hope’s book would have probably
been even a greater success—it would at least
have seemed a lot more real and a great deal less
like a martial dream—if he had placed the scene
in a real country, and borrowed a real monarch as
the central figure ; there are any number of real
monarchs who can be borrowed in these hard
times at less than 2£ per cent. Also, The Bulle-
tin grows more and more convinced that there
isn’t enough blood and battle in the drama, and
that Black Michael doesn’t howl so loudly and
persistently as he should do. Fully half the
show is sweet peace, and gentle love-making, and
the resting of the tired red head of a bogus king
on a good woman’s shoulder, and the love-making
is somehow the most unreal feature of the play.
Everybody feels in advance that it will come
to nothing. There are two love-stories and
they both end badly; the prologue goes back
two centuries or so to show another royal red head
(disguised in flour) resting on a shoulder where it
ought not to be, and the only thing each head
leaves behind it—save perhaps a hair-oil mark—is
woe and disappointment. Also, the ending of the
story is necessarily sickbed o’er with the pale cast
of melancholy ; it winds up with a shattered king
dragged out of his dungeon, and a broken-hearted
woman, and a good man who, by the force of cir-
cumstances, puts on his hat and walks out of the
story. Unfortunately, as this paper remarked
last week, most of the wild horse-and-battleaxe
part of the drama is unavoidably left put for want
of space. The dramatist has done all that cir-
cumstances admit of, and the result is a very good
drama, but the real “Prisoner of Zenda,” if its
best features are to appear, requires two square
miles of country to spread itself in.
** * *
Sydney Royal, which has been closed for
some time, re-opens to-night (Saturday) in the
variety business. This is quite a new departure
for the ancient house of melodrama, where for so
many years the heroine who went astray has been
thrown out weeping by the hard old father, and
the unjustly accused hero has declared his inno-
cence whilst his best girl threw her warm, white
arms round his neck, and had to be untied by the
police before the hero could be run in and sen-
tenced to 20 years’ “ hard.” A clean-shaved,
bright - eyed, eager - looking gentleman of the
American sex—one Melville Stoltz by name—is
running the new venture, and his company is
imported bodily from the land of the dollar and
the spread-eagle and the wooden ham. The
Flying Jordans, said to be something vast and
unapproachable in the serial line, are at the top of
the bill, and it is claimed that a certain triple
somersault act done by the most serial Jordan of
the crowd has never been equalled by any other
artist in “the Universe.” The Universe may,
therefore, consider itself knocked out, and may
take a back seat on the Dog Star and feel
small. The list further includes Virginia Aragon,
“ Queen of the Invisible Wire ” ; two Marios, the
“ athletes of the century ” ; the three Olifans ; the
two “ Kids ” ; the “ Little Fawn ” ; two “ King
Pins of Musical Comedy ”; sundry harpists,
singers, and dancers ; John J. Burke, “America’s
foremost comedian,” and various others. The
others include two “ delineators of negro charac-
ter”—which character, according to the Amurrikin
comic paper, has been much deteriorated by fowl-
stealing and unlawful water-melon and dishonest
huckleberry. If the two delineators are prepared
to delineate negro character with absolute accu-
racy, the present writer is prepared any night to
give them a leg-up over his garden-fence while
they purloin the discordant bass rooster next door
—a bird which interferes greatly with his sleep.
** * *
“ Charley’s Aunt ” goes as well at the
Criterion as it did on its first appearance, a year
or two ago, and is apparently fit for another
record run, if the management is willing to let the
he-lady in the black skirts and the wonderful cap
go for all she is worth. Under these cheering cir-
cumstances, no change is announced.
** * *
John Gourlay and his good old “ Skipped
by the Light of the Moon,” that have been
plodding round Australia together in all varieties
of luck for a good many years, commence a short
season at Sydney Palace this (Saturday) night.
“ Skipped ” was the first and about the best of the
alleged “ musical comedies,” but it began to rage
before the musical comedy was really invented,
and was thus very much in the position of the
primeval newspaper that had to suspend publica-
tion till Adam would be created. It wandered
around at first as a species of solitary what-is-it,
and had no distinct status, there being practically
nothing else of its kind, whereas now it is one of a
well-defined class, its principal misfortune, from
which it has never fully recovered, being that it
grew pretty old before its parents were born. The
Palace stage seems hardly large enough for the
occasion, and all the artistic loveliness of the
house looks rather incongruous as a setting for the
frenzied horse-play of Obadiah Dingle ; but if it is
a choice between letting the defrauded pie-man
chase Obadiah among these gorgeous surroundings
or shutting up the house altogether, it is doubt-
less the wiser course to let Obadiah go for all he
is worth.
** * *
After six nights William Cosgrove’s ill-
fated show pegged out at Sydney Lyceum. Up to
the last night of the season, which was Friday, it
was advertised that his theatre, which latterly had
hardly anything in it but the furniture and the
cold south wind, was “ filled from floor to ceiling ; ”
and that the melancholy exhibition was “ received
with vociferous cheers and tumultuous outbursts
of enthusiastic applause.”
** * *
At Melbourne Princess’s the short fare-
well season of Mrs. Potter and Bellew has
panned out splendidly thus far, and since those
indefatigable artists are committed to four more
changes of programme within the next eight
nights, they can reckon upon carrying away some
valuable souvenirs of Melbourne after all. “Fran-
cillon,” produced on Saturday, was a charming
apology for Mrs. Potter’s misdeeds in classic
drama. Her performance of the younger Dumas’
fin de siecle heroine is for the most part admirable
—as The Bulletin remarked when she did it in
Sydney—and the few points which are not admir-
able don’t obtrude themselves painfully. It is full
of pretty tricks and manners that appear natural
to the character. Also, the character itself, under
Mrs. Potter’s treatment, diffuses a fragrance of
feminine goodness, for the apparent nakedness of
the lady’s shoulders merely emphasises the fact
that she is clothed in chastity as well as the latest
Paris fashion. “ Francillon ” is a fricasseed
problem play served on silver with champagne
jelly. M. le Comte Bellew, bored by his young
wife’s devotion to the baby, goes astray just for
the sake of variety, and revels in the thought that
the comtesse is above suspicion whilst he sits
hugging a hired goddess in an oyster supper-room.
But there comes a cold, bleak morning when his
wife tells him that she tracked his movements
overnight and went astray on exactly the same
lines, with a beautiful young man whom she
picked up for purposes of connubial revenge.
Whereupon Bellew, being seized with violent
pains in his amour propre, staggers back against
the furniture and makes futile attempts to despise
the woman who followed his example. Presently
the lady reveals that she didn’t really stray half
as far as she had stated, but was merely
throwing out a hint for her lord’s future guidance.
Bellew, at the finish, feels infinitely grateful for
his wife’s moderation under the circs., and settles
down to a peaceful domestic life. The thought
that his venerable father and bosom friends will
from that time forward chaff him with mock in-
vitations to impossible sprees, doesn’t occur to the
converted sinner until after the curtain falls.
Many things are necessary to perfect happiness in
a married man, and perhaps the most necessary
thing of all is the power to say an occasional
“ Yes ”to temptation. But no matter.
• • • •
Bellew’s acting, as the Comte of easy vir-
tue, is so bright that it reflects great credit upon
Mrs. Potter by not putting her in the shade. The
stars shine together in “Francillon,” and the
people who are not stars stand afar off and shine
likewise. Cecil Ward, W. J. Montgomery,
Berkeley, Cathcart, and Miss Harrie Ireland
assist to make this a notable show. Unfor-
tunately it has run its short course, and will
appear no more in Melbourne unless present
arrangements can be altered. “ The Ironmaster ”
is now running ; “Merchant of Venice ” on Satur-
day ; “ Lady of Lyons ” to follow for one consecu-
tive night. And all the while “Francillon”
might have been booming away to the glory of
art, the advancement of healthy moral principles,
and the utter confusion of Mrs. Grundy.
** * *
Bland Holt’s conflagration drama, at Mel-
bourne Royal, is a revised edition of the “In
Sight of St. Paul’s ” that was originally produced
in .Sydney last year. The “ comic relief ” has been
written up, and a new scene showing the exterior
of the Aspasia Club, with a rather impossible
Tower Bridge in the background, is almost the
best item in the show. Orgies inside the club
aforesaid conclude with the sudden uprising of a
disorderly husband, who is about to break a bottle
across Cosgrove’s smooth round head when the
melee drops into darkness. Outside the club is
a panorama of hansom cabs, ’buses, free fights,
and donkey carts, extending over about five
minutes of wild excitement. The dialogue in the
new scene comes and goes in hasty swear words
by people who are all trying to tumble into the
wrong cab, and the curtain falls upon a general
impression of midnight madness. Take it al-
together, “St. Paul’s ” is a most deserving horror,
gorgeously mounted and effectively played in the
strongest parts, which are sinful to the last degree.
Miss Watson’s talent for luring lovers to their
ruin and dancing on their fallen characters shines
forth from several murderous episodes on this
occasion. Albert Norman, too, is impressive when
he drops his voice in the Panther’s boudoir, and
takes instructions re humping her husband’s body
to the cellar below, and Miss Vivienne, as a lost
sheep, would be rather impressive if she were only
able to drop her voice without losing it altogether.
Walter Baker is merely called upon to bear a
brother’s crime and snuffle in a manly tone, whilst
brother Cosgrove seems profoundly conscious of
being much too fleshy and short-winded for his
juvenile billet. Bland and Mrs. Holt, Miss
Frances Ross, and three or four minor performers,
give the show a push in the right direction. Two
or three other people are a regrettable nuisance, not
because they can’t act, but because they are
clumsily introduced into the middle aisle of St.
Paul’s Cathedral, and serve to make a weak con-
clusion longer than it need be.
** * *
“ The Beggar Student,” although it doesn’t
quite supply a long-felt want in Melb., is having
a pretty good time at the Bijou. The orchestra
and concerted singing numbers improve steadily,
and if the libretto were polished up with a bit of
literary sand-paper the entertainment might call
itself bright. Yet, even in that case (and a dull
libretto never does get polished) the ama-
teur legs of Sydney Ralfe, and the un-
humorous fooling of the chief gaoler, would
move the more fastidious section of the pub-
lic to tears. Miss Mabelle Darley, who ap-
peared as Laura during last week and retained her
place on Saturday, is an acquisition to the co., but
for some reason or other this clever little lady
doesn’t sing just now as sweetly as she sang under
her Lyric Club name of Davidson. The voice is
there all the same, and Miss Darley will certainly
achieve something considerable in due course.
“Nemesis ” is announced to follow “The Beggar
Student ” on Saturday.
The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The N.S.W. Income-tax Commissioners make
matters worse rather than better by declaring that their
extraordinary attempt to collect what they call “ ar-
rears ”is strictly in accordance with the Act. Said Act
reads as follows : Clause 27, section 1 : “ The amount of
taxable income from all sources for the year immediately
preceding the year of assessment shall be taken as the
basis of calculation.” This distinctly intimates that the
tax payable in 1896 shall be calculated on the income for
1895. And there is absolutely nothing about any right
to alter the arrangements afterwards and calculate the
1896 payment on the 1896 income— i.e., to use as the
basis of calculation the year of assessment instead of the
year preceding the year of assessment. Again, the Act
says (clause 31, section III.) : “ Provided further that
if ... it is at any time found that the assessed value
or amount of any land or income respectively is higher
than is declared in the return or returns relating thereto
such excess shall be liable to taxation.” The returns for
1896 are distinctly “relating to” the income for 1895.
Therefore, if a man paid tax in 1896 on a smaller income
than he really received in the year (1895) to which that
tax “ relates,” the Act provides that he can be made to
pay up the deficiency. But as the 1896 returns don’t “re-
late to ” the income for 1896 the fact that the said income
was larger than the one (for 1895) set forth in the tax-
paper gives the Commissioners no authority at all to col-
lect the discrepancy twice over and call it arrears. It
merely gives them the right to collect a larger tax in
1897.
Again, clause 53 provides : “If any taxpayer
proves to the satisfaction of the Commissioners that his
income during any year fell short of the sum in respect of
which the tax for that year was paid, the Commissioners
shall cause the assessment to be amended accordingly,
and the overcharge to be refunded.” This also seems as
explicit as anything can be. The 1895 income, as set forth
in clause 27, is the basis of the 1896 assessment, therefore
the tax for that year (1895) is the one payable in 1896.
If it is afterwards proved that the tax for that year ( 1895),
as charged and paid in 1896, was excessive, the taxpayer
may recover the difference. The obvious meaning of the
Act is that every taxpayer pays income-tax this year on
last year’s income—the year which forms the “basis”
above alluded to. If he pays the correct amount, that
transaction is absolutely ended. If his income for ’96 is
more or less than the income he paid tax on in ’96, the
result is that he pays more or less tax, as the case may be,
in ’97, but there is nothing at all to justify the idiotic
device of re-opening the completed transaction and wast-
ing ink and stationery upon it. And how such a clumsy
and obviously illegal device came to be invented passes
comprehension. So far, the Commissioners have given no
explanation ; they merely assert that their proceeding is
“ according to the Act.” How is it according to the Act ?
• • * •
Though the N.S.W. Tax Commissioners collect
“arrears” vigorously when this year’s income is larger
than last year’s, they don't, seemingly, refund with any-
thing like the same velocity when things are the other
way about and the balance is against them. At least one
infuriated taxpayer publishes in the S.M. Herald a copy
of a document received in such a case, said document
being apparently a fiat refusal to recognise a claim against
the department. Also, the Tax Commissioners, who seem
to have swelled head very badly indeed, announce that
“while the present Commissioners hold office and the
present Act is in force” the administration will not be
varied. Even if the Supreme Court directs that it shall
be varied the Commissioners are apparently quite ready
to treat that institution with cold derision.

Twelve months ago—in its issue of 2nd M av
1896— The Bulletin wrote in this strain of the ColnmVi
Bank, Melbourne: “If a bank which has written off if,
losses-and got into a sound position should pay 5 p er cent
dividends—which doesn’t seem unreasonable when the
bulk of its deposits cost only 24 per cent.—and the Coin
nial proposes to get to this destination, it will have tn
write off, on the present basis of its affairs, £74i) 000-all
its paid-up ordinary capital and two-thirds of the prefer
ence capital.” This oblique prophecy of a rear a<m turn,
out very nearly true. The Colonial Bank announces that
it has written off £71,466 of forfeited share capital tn
cover losses and has in addition £558,750 of old losses
still to provide for—equal to about 20 per cent on its
advances and investments. To cover this it is proposed
to write off balance to credit of profit and loss (‘'B634V to
write off all the paid-up ordinary capital (£507,472) •’ to
call up £2 per share, and apparently to write off £42 644
of the proceeds, and start the bank afresh with the re-
mainder. As 20,540 shares out of 125,000 are forfeited
and a good many more will probably have to be forfeited
when the new call comes along, the chances are that the
Colonial will not have more than £200,000 of ordinary
capital to work on when it makes its fresh start. There is
also £406.000 of preference capital, but even with this it
will be a very small institution.
The half-year’s business looks as if the Colonial
Bank had at last got round the corner—not very far
round, certainly. The profits are £3043, against £2004
last half and £2564 at 31st March, ’96. At March, 1895
the accounts showed a loss of £4694 for the six months’
then the reduction of deposit interest from to 2| per
cent, made a half-yearly saving of £15,973, so if the
Colonial was in as good a position now as it claimed to be
in two years ago the profit should be over £11,(100.
Therefore, though things are improving, there is still a
great deal of lee-wav to make up. About half the present
increase in profits is through increased earnings ; the rest
arises through a further reduction in working expendi-
ture. The present rate of profit is only equal to a
dividend of 2 per cent, to preference shareholders, leaving
nothing at all for ordinary holders and no surplus with
which to begin a new reserve fund. Deposit and
note circulation show a respectable increase, and cash
and liquid assets (£573,861) are £120,000 more
than they were six months ago. Altogether the Colonial
Banks looks as if it was moving slowly in the right
direction—very slowly, and with a very' long
travel. Since 1892 the Colonial has w r ritten off—including
the sums now provided for—£9l7,ooo, which is a large
order for an institution of its size. In 1890 the private
wealth of Victoria was put down by Coghlan at
£344,000,000. The Colonial Bank alone accounts for the
vanishment of nearly one million, and when the Commer-
cial of Australia, Royal and Premier Permanent Building
Society are added, these four institutions alone knock off
*i .J 4 £4.000.000 among them. It is about time for the
issue of a revised estimate.
* ♦ * *
The National Bank (Melbourne) presents an
eminently gratifying report. The profits for the half-
year ended 31st March are £14,712 as compared with
£11,707 last half; £10,523 at 31st March, ’96, and £13,762
at September, 1895. It thus appears that the downward
movement in the profits definitely ended 12 months ago,
and the improvement which then set in is being well
maintained. This is assuming, of course, that the profit is
all made in the ordinary course of business, but it is a
notable feature that the holding of Government and
other debentures has decreased, during the six
months by no less than £493,000. If this means
that the securities in question were sold at the top
of a rise, and the profit realised on the sale is included
in this half-year’s returns, the profit of the actual bank-
ing business may be less than it appears. This is a
matter entirely beyond calculation in the absence of any
data to go upon.
The National pays a 5 p.c. div. (-£7645) to pre-
ference shareholders, and the balance is added to profit
and loss account, which now- stands at £23,566. The
general business seems to improve steadily, and the re-
constructed deposits, which two years ago stood at
£5,300,000, are now reduced to £3,000,000, while another
instalment is in course of repayment, and still another
is to be released in June. Despite these repayments, the
bank has £1,890,880 cash on hand, and £531,000 in
Government debentures and other easily realisable securi-
ties, equal to about 35 per cent, on all liabilities to the
public, reconstructed deposits included. With such pon-
derous resources the National could undoubtedly release
all the remaining reconstructed money in one operation if
it felt so inclined, for the great bulk of the deposits set free
are promptly re-deposited at reduced rates, and the
release is a mere book entry. The only point to which
any definite exception can be taken is the absence of any
steps towards establishing a new reserve fund. The old
reserves, £670,000 in all, were entirely written off in the
luckless period after 1893. If the National directors are
confident that all the bad debts and depreciation have
been provided for, there is no visible reason why they
shouldn’t carry £20,000 of their accumulated profit to a
new reserve, and thus make at least a small start in the
right direction. If they are not sure that all losses are
provided for, then the payment of a dividend to prefer-
ence shareholders is entirely objectionable.

To barmaids and other young ladies who
contemplate going to W.A. goldfields: “Maud
Howard succumbed to fever at midnight on Satur-
day. She was 20, a native of S.A., and was
buried yesterday, not a solitary individual follow-
ing her remains to the grave.”
The masterful Mr. E. M. Young, general
manager of the Australian Mortgage, Land and
Finance Co., Ltd., has been released by death
from all further harrying over the Mercadool
selection cases. He caught a chill in Sydney last
week and died of broncho-pneumonia.
That enterprising Sydney political boss
and illicit-distiller, M. T. M’Laughlin, Esq., has
again been caught and this time fined .£500 or a
year’s gaol. This gentleman is in gaol accordingly
—for, it would appear, most of the divs. in the
business go to the men who keep poison-shops
licensed by Govt, and ladle out M’Laughlin rot-
gut per medium of Miss Tottie Peroxide.
There are two Richmonds in the field now
—old Meston and young Meston, father Meston
and son Meston, bow Meston and boomerang
Meston. Father Meston has been named depart-
mental Providence of the Cj. aborigines, and is
going to restore ’em to the happy-hunting-ground
stage when Malachi wore a collar of brass that he
prigged from his next-door neighbor. Son Meston
is in charge of an embryo h.-h.-g. near Mary-
borough, and, according to the local paper, when
two boatmen landed the other day for water, the
happy hunters set upon ’em with nullahs and
knocked ’em silly. That’s their yarn, anyway ;
but if son Meston has inherited father Meston s
Lempri&re he should be able to knock beans out
of it in two pages and 387 mythological allusions
to Diana the mother of Proserpine, and Chaos the
step-sister of Eurydice.
Sydney D.T. reporter on Butler at Auck-
land : —
He wore a short beard and looked . . . fit for any-
thing. . . Once or twice he exploded in ferocious out-
breaks. . . The newspaper men were warned by the
detectives to keep as far as possible from the criminal.
And so on. Now, suppose the American system
of empanelling a jury were in force in Australia :
suppose the acceptance of every man summoned
as a juror depended on his giving satisfactory
answers to (1) Have you read newspaper accounts
of the alleged murders? (2) Have you formed
any opinion as to who is the guilty person ? (3)
Have you formed any opinion as to the guilt or
innocence of the prisoner at the bar ? —how long
would it take to compile a jury ? Happily, how-
ever, N.S.W. is far ahead of the U.S. in such
matters and doesn’t fiddle-faddle about the cases
of people already condemned in the opinion of
press and public.
The Corbett - Fitzsimmons battle will
shortly be reproduced with faithful details at
the Edison Electric Parlor, 162 Pitt-st., Sydney.
It was cabled that the great fight was recorded
all through by the Kinetoscope, and the colonial
branch of the Edison Laboratory before the fight
took place made its arrangements for securing the
record. The Edison Yitascope, Kinetoscope, and
Kinetophone, and the wonderful X-rays are still
on show in the parlor, and are attracting great
public interest.*

According to the report of the London account-
ants appointed to overhaul the A.J.S. Bank's affairs, the
dividends declared since 1893 should not have been paid,
so presumably it is meant that they were not really
earned. The A.J.S. started booming again almost imme-
diately after its reconstruction ; in the first half-year
of its new existence it gaily paid away something very
near £23,000 to shareholders, and the total amount ex-
pended in dividends is about £115,000.
The Union Trustee Oo. (Melb.) presents a
balance-sheet that is by no means cheerful reading. The
year’s net profit is £2742, compared with £553 in 1896,
£3648 in 1895, and £2797 in 1894, which, so far as it goes,
is a distinct improvement. Then a 4 per cent, dividend
absorbs £2953, which is £211 more than the earnings.
Last year the Union Trustee paid £1476 in dividends, or
£923 more than its earnings ; and in 1894 it paid £2953
dividends—£156 more than it earned. The Union has no
reserve fund to show after 12 years’ business, and its only
visible accumulations are £1451 at credit of profit
and loss — nearly £600 less than it had three years
ago. There also appears for the first time an item,
“Investment Fluctuation Account, £2789,” but this
doesn’t come out of profits ; it is created by writing up to
their present market value Govt, securities hitherto put
in at cost. Apparently the trustee business proper is
again a heavy loss ; the company’s own funds are invested
in 3) and 4 per cent, securities, and in mortgages which
presumably yield at least 5 per cent., yet the net earnings
are less than 3J per cent., so it would seem that it takes a
good part of the interest earnings to pay the cost of run-
ning the trustee business, which latter is kept up mainly
for amusement and healthy exercise. The Union seems a
good enough concern for its clients, but for the share-
holders it is lamentable, and if the board doesn’t swear off
paying dividends which are not earned, it may become a
great deal more lamentable.
* * * *
That stupendous monopoly, the Australian Agri-
cultural Co., which grabbed some hundreds of thousands
of acres of good cornstalk lands and the best of the pro-
vince’s coal deposits (mostly out of pure philanthropy
and with a desire to promote settlement), and has been
making a good thing out of them for over 60 years, is
still alive. The profit for 1896 (distributed in London)
was £46,000, and the profits for the last three years were
about £150,000. As N.S.W. gets nothing in return for all
this, and it has been paying this tribute regularly to the
British philanthropists since the 30’s, the argument in
favor of an absentee tax, and a graduated land - tax
which will smite the very big landholder with a tremen-
dous smite, is very apparent indeed.
* * * *
Sydney Assets Realisation Co., which was
founded to realise other people’s assets and can’t realise
its own, reports that the shortage through bad debts and
depreciation has grown £15,000 worse during ’96, and is
now £65,000. The miserable shareholders have paid up
£14,000 calls during the year, so life is a constant race be-
tween them and the depreciation, and the depreciation
wins by a short head.
* * * *
In view of the continuous wail about the want
of outlets for the investment of capital, Tub Bulletin
suggests one fine large opening. Watson’s Bay township
occupies the finest site on Sydney Harbor. There are
greater possibilities about it than there are even about
Manly. The trouble is that there isn’t one wide straight
street in the township—the best of them is hardly wide
enough to be called an alley, and hardly straight enough
to be called a corkscrew. Also, nine-tenths of the build-
ings are disgraceful hovels, and there is only one
structure in the township proper that shouldn’t, for the
benefit of humanity, be shifted by dynamite. If an in-
telligent syndicate got an Act of Parliament authorising
it to compulsorily buy up the whole township at its pre-
sent value, Which, thanks to the degraded condi-
tion of the place, is not much ; and then levelled
every building in it bar one, and ripped up
every alley, and built a model township with wide,
straight thoroughfares on this lovely site, it would have
a magnificent speculation on hand. That is to say, the
spec.'would be magnificent if it was done with the syndi-
cate’s own money ; if it started by borrowing in England
at 5 per cent, the interest would kill it before the work
was half finished. If Sydney and the 40 or so adjacent
municipalities were combined under one municipal
government the County Council might tackle this con-
tract as a municipal undertaking, and, with common-
sense management, do well out of it. As things are, what
ouo-ht to be the prettiest township around Sydney is
about the saddest-looking place to be found on the sea-
ward side of Dubbo.

Tooth and Co., the great Sydney beer co.,
limited, has a half-year’s profit of £42,556 against
£35,772 and £43,568 for two previous half-years. The
latter is the true basis of comparison, as representing the
thirst season of last year, so there is a slight falling off. A 7
per cent, dividend absorbs £31,500 ; £5000 goes to reserve,
making it £70,000 ; £2500 to provide for depreciation of
plant and machinery, and the remainder, with previous
balance, makes £8442 carried forward.

Illawarra (N.S.W.) Steamship Co. shows a half-

lls 5d. and four-sevenths of a farthing per cent, per
annum) absorbs £2489, so the co. has a balance of £133.
United Insurance Co. pays a half-yearly dividend at 12)4
per cent.

The number of houses to let in Sydney and its
immediate suburbs advertised in any Saturday issue of
S.M. Herald now runs to about 800. The corresponding
list in Melb. Age runs to about 200. Either things are so
much better in Melb. that there are much fewer empty
tenements, or they are so much worse that advertising for
tenants is only waste of the harmless, necessary shilling.
* * * *
At a certain far inland Australian town a local
genius has invented a new patent ship, and a syndicate
composed of four nightmen and a publican has been
formed to run the invention. One result is that all the
four night-carts stop at a certain corner in the small
hours of the morning while the drivers discuss what they
will do with their share of the profits, and the result is
far from salubrious.

A. N.S.W. Bench refused a license to a would-
be publican last week because he confessed being the
agent of a bank with a mortgage over the property. But
if a bank can’t put its man in to run a publican’s business,
why should a brewer or distiller be allowed to do it ?

That languishing institution, the Australasian
Institute of Mining Engineers, has held a session in
Sydney in which the public took not the slightest in-
terest. The society has served one purpose —it has
enabled men who are still at large to place M.A.I.M.E.
after their names in reporting on Westralian “ cats.” The
institute has been hopelessly disgraced on account of the
absence of discrimination re admission to membership.

* * * »
“Wild Cat” readers will not have been sur-
prised by the British B.H. dividend. Five months ago it
was said here that it would be possible for the co. to pay
a div. in March or April, but that the announcement
would probably be deferred till the meeting of share-
holders in London in May. What has actually occurred
is that at the beginning of April the Australian
directors announced that, subject to the approval of
London shareholders, a Is. div. will be declared
at the May meeting. One shilling means £15,000 in
this co. Of course, this cannot be paid monthly
—even a sixpenny could not legitimately be
maintained on 300,000 shares, although there is a big
amount in hand which might be worked on. The manager
—Retallick, a man whose name is seldom heard or seen —
has been in Adelaide conferring with the local directors
on these and other points.
Just as the British div. will not have surprised
Cat students, so the creep in the Junction will not have
startled them. Time after time reference has been made
to the extreme difficulty which the ore body presented
in this respect, and the big outlay for timber
which it necessitated. Manager Phillips might
have been able to do better had not so much of the
best quality stuff been called for during the past 12
months : it was impossible to carry on filling-up work
effectively while the workings were blocked with ore.
The manager reported on Easter Sunday (the collapse
took place three days before) that “our output will be
affected for a week or 10 days. After this, I hope to
resume shipments in the usual way. It is my
opinion that stoping will be carried on north and
south of the main stopes at a less cost than before
the collapse took place.” Rosy, this. Managers of course
always persuade themselves, and try to persuade others,
that creeps are good things, until one wonders why they
spend so many thousands in trying to keep the ground up
at all. Fact is, where it is hoped to work below a creep,
the creep is a curse every time. There is almost al-
wavs, too, a great loss of ore, which is buried in a
confused mass in the wreckage. It wouldn’t
matter much if the falls took place in depleted ground ;
but they rarely do. It hasn’t occurred so in this case ; it
didn’t occur so at the British or in Block 11 of the Pro-
prietary. Besides, one of the main shafts, MTntyre’s, is
twisted—a matter no more serious than the manager re-
ports provided the disturbed ground has finished moving,
of which, however, there is no proof. The Junction
North shaft and some of the stopes in that mine have also
suffered. As showing the uncertainty that must always
exist with regard to a mine which has started moving, it
may be noted that whereas the main fall iu the Junction
took place on Thursday, the principal collapse in the
Junction North did not occur till the following Sunday
night. Naturally this uncertainty piles up the timber
bill—and reduces" profits.
Perrow, mine manager of the Junction North,
has left office, but the creep has nothing to do with it.

The purchase by N.S.W. Government of Rose-
hill private railway is a certainty of the near future.
Noses have been counted—five Ministers for and none
against. Thus one more private railway spec, will follow
the ancient Hunter River Railway Co., and Sydney-
Goulburn Railway Co.
« * * «
A miserable little sheet called the West Aus-
tralian Review, printed in London obviously in the
interests of “ cat ” promotion, accuses the mining writer
of The Bulletin of damning Westralia and “ cracking up
N.S. Wales low-grade mines, in which he has, for many
years, been dabbling unprofitably.” As a sample of
which he quotes this Bulletin par. Great Western,
Cobar : New batterv crushed 300 tons for 120 oz. Good
enough if the battery can keep up this average from a
good, big face, and not by gouging out streaks in the ore-
body.” If the West Australian scribe was as well
acquainted with Cobar as he is with the collapse of the
W A. boom, he would know that, like The Bulletin’s
criticisms of Westralian “ cats,” the extract quoted as a
sample of “ cracking up ” has since been more than jus-
tified by events. The Great Western yield alluded to did
not come from a big face, but was got by pig-rooting,
with the sequel that the mine’s next crushing went about
2dwt. Then the manager resigned, and his successor is
looking for good ore.
* * * *
The Occidental Mine Co., Cobar (N.S.W.), is
rewarded for its enterprise in tackling its great ore body
in a decent way. The ore, which for some years has not
milled more than 3£dwt. has gone up to sdvvt., and if that
average can be kept up it is enough fordivs., or to furnish
a much bigger plant.
♦ * * *
“Poor Value for the Money” is the heading
under which the Financier (London) jumps on the pros-
pectus of the N.S.W. Goldfields (Ltd.), formed to take oyer
some mines in the Swamp Oak district, N.S.W., of which
the Highland Mary is the chief. The prospectus covers,
it seems, 22 pp., and has been severely criticised by other
papers that only indulge in this sort of thing when they
are quite sure the boom is as dead as Caesar. The writer
notices one thing—that 2723 tons crushed returned 74980 z.
gold, an average of over 2oz. 15dwt. per ton, and the
tailings show an additional value of about an oz. per ton.
How many Westralian mines floated in London did as
much crushing before flotation? Whether these mines justify
the £200,000 capital or not the writer cannot say from per-
sonal knowledge, but such bulk crushing before flotation
is a good feature distinguished only by its entire absence
from 99 in 100 Westralian “ cat ” floats.
* * * •
Inverell (N.S.W.) : “An expert has arrived
from London to investigate the truth of Mr. P. J. Fitz-
gerald’s glowing account of the Crow Mountain mining
claims, and this speaks volumes for the enterprise of the
local syndicate. Mr. Fitzgerald, on behalf of his syndi-
cate, has secured the famous Dodger claim, the richness
of which is well known.” The local syndicate will pro-
bably find that London is buying mines with much judg-
ment now. The everlasting richness of the Dodger,
which, so far as the public knows, has not yet been mon-
keyed with by any battery, has made people rather tired.
It is about two years since it struck good gold, and there
is nothing more certain than that it will stay rich so long
as it keeps off crushing.
• * * *
A big Westralian mine lately got six months’
exemption, through some of the machinery being mis-
carried (a common occurrence there). The exemption was
apparently granted without question, for it afterwards
turned out that the only machinery missing was a few
planks of timber—which could be bought locally.
♦ * * *
Published in London, a list of Westralian mines,
owned and managed there, where the money paid in
directors’ fees and administration amounted last year to
more than that spent on the mines. Chaffer’s spent in
development, £4187; in London, £4741. Hannan’s
Main Reef, in London, £4415; on the mine, £3089.
Central Boulder, on the mine, £2094 ; in London, £2116.
There are some more. It should appear in the balance-
sheets as follows :To mine-developments—so much. To
Bull’s bingey-development—so much. The wild extrava-
gance of London mining management has not escaped
notice bj’ Australian mining men. Speaking to George
Lansell recently, a Sydney man asked if, in the event of a
mine, in which Mr. Lansell held about half the shares,
being sold he would be willing to take a considerable part
of the purchase money in paid-up shares in a London co.
The shriek and prolongation of the veteran’s “ W-H-A-T?”
with which this query was met was remembersome, but
the height of the jump into the air performed by a man
over 70 years of age was the most surprising that
Sydneyite ever saw. Bull might do worse than interview
Lansell.

“ The Croesus mine, Hannan’s, is still bagging
rich ore. A start has been made to vigorously open out
the mine, which is attracting much attention. It is esti-
mated that the lode will, for the 24ft. it has been opened,
go lOoz. to the ton.” The writer sincerely hopes so, but

bearing the name of Croesus that nothing but the pro-
duction of bullion will now attract their serious attention.
* * * *
The Water Trust, Mining and Public Crushing
Co. of W.A., is about to start its 80-head stamp mill at
Northam, and for many months past has been adver-
tising for ore. Ore seems to be the great want of the
country, not batteries. The marvellous mines of Kal-
goorlie are the one saving feature.
* * * *
Clarke and Co., in their excellent circular for
this month, give a list of the batteries on the W.A.
goldfields. They account for 1555 stamps erected, as
well as Huntingdon and other mills equal to 300 heads,
while there are 1200 stamps either being erected or con-
templated. “No doubt many coming under the first
head will never be used, for it is no secret that already
where several plants have been erected there is no ore
that can be treated under existing conditions.” At
Hannan’s there are only 115 stamps altogether at work,
and yet this camp is turning out more than half the
country’s gold with less than one-tenth its crushing capa-
city—a very fine thing for the camp, but a shocking con-
dition of things for the rest of the fields.
• * « *
Claude Maitland has furnished Clarke & Co.
with a report on the prospects and recent developments
at Kalgoorlie. He says that during the last three months
the field has made strides which even the most sanguine
could not have foretold. The rich auriferous formations
have been found to extend through many properties
which in Dec. last were regarded as second-rate concerns.
Several of the recent discoveries were a complete sur-
prise ; their value cannot be realised, more particularly
in Europe, and the consequence will be that mines on the
verge of abandonment will resume work. The out-
put for the quarter was 50,8070 z. ; that of the whole
of W.A. being 113,2060 Z., while last month the output
from Hannan’s exceeded half the total yield. “ The ex-
perience gained during* the last three years makes it ditn-
cult to secure the confidence of possible investors. The
fact of the matter is, the public has been cajoled so often
that now, when really good developments do take place,
they are looked upon askance. But surely such outputs
and developments as we have had of late must before
long have a beneficial effect.” The writer is afraid it will
be a long time. All booms are short-lived, but the bigger
the boom the longer the depression. That of W.A. has a
shocking aftermath. It will live in history as the greatest
mining bubble of the age. Nearly two years ago the
writer advised the W.A. Govt, to do as was done by
Queensland Govt, at the birth of the Mount Morgan boom
— to warn the English public of the “ wild cat ” nature of
most of the mines being sold in London. That and that
only would have stopped the boom, as was done in
Queensland. They did not stop the boom because they
were more or less in it. The day rapidly nears when they
may be sorry they didn’t do something of the^sort.
“ The money-order remittances forwarded dur-
ing March to London from W.A. amounted to £4005. The
total sent away was £71,038, the balance going to the
eastern provinces.” Very interesting, but it would be still
more so to know how much came from London to W.A.
in the same time.
* * » *
Menzies Mining and Exploration Corporation,
London : “ Chairman O’Driscoll said they had to carry an
enormous load —a great and wide area which ate up the
working capital with extraordinary rapidity. The car-
riage of machinery had run into an enormous amount of
money. In the present condition of the market, it seemed
hopeless to float subsidiary co.’s, and the policy of the
new board was to shear off anything that was not really
payable, and retain only five or six leases which would
give good returns. Gold-mining always went through
various vicissitudes. When the leases were purchased
they were very rich indeed on the surface, but, after sink-
ing 50ft. or 60ft., they began to look very rocky, and,
having a large sum in hand, they purchased others. It
was thought £25,000 would be ample to put them in a
good position, and it was proposed to get the money by
the issue of debentures.”

“ A young clerk in one of the Agent-Generals
offices was put forward as defendant in the English
courts recently in connection with an alleged blackmail-
ino- case against one of the numerous minor naming
papers,” writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent.
“ There are a score of these papers here which judge the
solidity and prospects of every new company put on the
market by the evidence given them in hard cash of the
financial standing of its backers, and thus influenced by
sterling facts bestow unlimited praise or blame on the
spec.—often a veritable quid pro ‘ quids.’ This youth in
question is registered as the owner of the rag concerned,
though in reality but a moneyless dummy and occupying
the position of torpedo-fender to the big-pots in the dark
behind him.”

Q. gold-returns for quarter ended March were
167,4880 z., against 147,6440 z. for same quarter in ’96, or
an increase of 20,0000 z. No use to sling useful dollars
after Westralian wild-cats or to throw useful men into
New Guinea gorges until Bananaland shows signs of
getting played out.
Gympie turned out for the March quarter 11,260
tons for 16,1880 z., or loz. Bdwt. 18gr. per ton—over the
average for same quarter of last year, which was about an
oz. per ton. Divs. for quarter were £20,700; calls,
£20,736. Decrease in stone crushed of 2130 tons ; increase
in vield of 26080 z.

Towers yield for March quarter was 64,8800 z.,
beinglo,ooooz.in exoessof amountforcorrespondingperiod
of ’96. Papuan, Brilliant and Victoria crushed 630 tons for
12680 z. ; Day Dawn, 3260 tons for 32030 z. ; Brilliant Block,
617 tons for 2910 z. ; Brilliant, 3259 tons for 42060 z. A
cake of 23380 z. gold from this crushing, valued at £B5OO,
is to be sent to the Exhibition, Brisbane. Total Queens-
land yield for quarter, 167,4880 z. an increase of
19,8240 z. on first quarter of ’96.

Anticipated that Q. Mining Commission will
recommend extension of areas of leaseholds, and a relaxa-
tion of labor conditions—essentially a wild-cat move.
Quite true, though, that the Q. mining laws sadly need
consolidation, and that the regulations require re-draft-
ing. Why doesn’t Govt, spend the few thousands it pre-
tentiously places on the Estimates every year for the
development of the mining industry' ?
* * * *
Thirty insolvencies on Croydon (Q.) field have
provoked the formation of a trade protection association,
whose principal business will be to probe the affairs of
future bankrupts.
* * * *
Clark’s Brilliantand Worcester, Charters Towers,
reports another splendid crushing of just under 500 tons
for about 4oz. per ton. Old man Clark, who finds himself
the lucky controller of one of the best Australian mines,
is entitled to congratulations on the tenacity with which
he for long years stuck to and paid calls on his 40,000
shares, despite the efforts, fair and foul, of the smarty
brigade of Charters Towers to make him quit. The clever
division, which is now kicking itself out of pure fury, long
managed to keep down the market, but it never once
succeeded in getting old man Clark’s shares out of his
safe or leading him off the track.
* * * •
Additional testimony to the deep interest which
is being felt in Gippsland (Vic.) mining is supplied by the
rapid flotation of the Loch Fyne Central G.M. Co. in Melb.
This was offered to the public in 20,000 10s. shares, and
the majority of them were snapped up within 48 hours of
the issue of the prospectus. Mr. G. W. Smith is manager,
and Messrs. J. B. Were and Son are brokers for the new
venture. Mining men in the Southern province begin to
realise that the gold at their doors is easier of access than
that in the distant West, and quite as marketable at the
local Mint. *

From S.M. Herald’s report of the Brand-
Ferguson wedding : “ . . . The bridegroom’s gal-
lant figure in the steel corslet and helmet of the
2nd Life Guards.” Further on: “The dazzling
uniform of an officer of the 2nd Life Guards. In
his case a helmet of dazzling brilliancy ” (Brookes’
Monkey soap, no doubt—it won’t wash clothes)
crowned with magnificent plumes.” And yet
once more : “ Wearing the full uniform of the 2nd
Life Guards, with its glittering breast-plate ”
(Monkey soap again !) “ at once became the central
figure of the great assemblage. ”
* * * *
A few presents at Sydney vice-regal tying-
up last week included “ a pedigree pug-puppy, two
live kangaroos,” and “ book of poems— 1 The Man
from Snowy River.’” “Banjo” thus takes the
place of the “ white ivory prayer-book,” generally
the orthodox presentation reading-matter of Hing-
lish marriages.
* * * *
At a recent meeting of Melb. Hospital
committee it was proposed that the buildin"
should be illuminated in honor of the Jubilee"
Certain mean-spirited members objected to “ the
spending of the hospital s badly-needed money in
such a direction when hundreds of patients were
being turned away every year for want of accom-
modation.” Their objection was crushed with
contumely, and it was decided that the hospital
should have a loyal flare-up at a moderate cost
Hooray! Fancy picture Patient, in last stage
of illness, is presented at hospital gates on Jubilee
night. Refused admission. Reasons given—no
beds to spare; no money to buy more beds.
Patient (feebly, but cheerfully) : “Never mind
I’ve seen the Hospital Illumination and can die
happy. Gorsave ! ” (Dies.)
* * * *
The real reason why the members of the
N.S.W. Ministry absented themselves from the
Dailey statue inauguration appears to be that the
chief talking on the occasion was to be done by
their political opponent, G R. Dibbs. They even
fully persuaded Gov. Hampden not to attend, and
it is no secret that the Governor would have
stayed away but for the interposition of Leg
Council President Lackey. The Ministers’ plea
was that they had not been invited in their official
capacity, but the fact remains that they all re-
ceived personal invitations—just as did the Judges,
the Cardinal, and others who put in an appear-
anoe. The excuse tendered for the Ministers’
absence convicts them of a most paltry-minded
act —eminently characteristic of several of their
number. Gov. Hampden’s apology for the Minis-
ters’ absence only made matters a thousand
times worse, in that it did not fit in with the
Ministers’ own excuse.

the battle on scraps of blood-stained paper.
* * ♦ *
Some interesting possibilities as to the
dissemination of phthisis are suggested by the
discovery of an apparent tubercularium in the
compiling branch of Sydney Lands Office. Out
of 35 men therein employed, some have died of
consumption, and others are suffering from that
fell disease. “The officers in the other portions
of the building, however,” remarks S.M. Herald
m a loftily confirmative way, do not attach
much importance to the matter, and look upon
the fears of the ‘compilers’ as groundless.” But
these insouciant persons haven’t to put in their
time in the consumption-mill, and, in any case,
the health authorities “attached sufficient im-
portance to the matter ” to cause the suspected
rooms to be stripped of everything “ which could
possibly convey contagion.” The blood-inherited
theory of phthisis has so long obtained that the
community doesidt even begin to realise the extent
to which that bacillus is lying around in wait for
its lungs.

♦ ♦ * *
Nature is about three centuries too late
with the human sleuth-hound charged, the other
day, at Newcastle, N.S.W., with slaughterous in-
tentions towards a woman whose evidence had
procured him, years ago, a long imprisonment.
Deported to Brisbane upon release—tramped back
to N.S.W—traced out the woman, and was
arrested by forewarned police. Said it didn’t
matter !—time no object !—do for her in the long
run . Sent to Maitland as insane ; recently cl is-
charged ; arrested again on information for
threats; discharged once more upon declaration
that, if he felt the revenge-thirst again, he’d kill
himself sooner than the woman. Odds, of course
that the wretch is cranky; but longer odds still
that no justly-convicted man would have main-
tamed such a long and grim vendetta.

* * * *
A recent N.Q. indecent assault case casts
a useful illumination upon the pleasing little ways
of the case-greedy constable. The two girls con-
cerned —12 and 8 years—deposed that they had
been instructed by the police to put themselves in
the way of being tampered with by defendant,
and thereafter to inform the constables, who, with
the father of one of the girls, were to be waiting
close at hand. “The police-sergeant,” testified
the elder girl, “didn’t explain to me what was
expected to happen ; but I understood. I didn’t
go away when defendant was called out, because I
expected him to be more offensive when he came
back.” The disgusted magistrate refused to com-
mit ; but how many men have been gaoled and
flogged upon the evidence of just such sweet little
innocents as these !

Her Reverie
A question Golden Hair now I have broken
the spell that bade your thoughts reman unspoken
Were you remembering
As you lay Still your great eyes wide unseeing
some brave deed of a former being?
Or did you but recall
Your last ball?
ESA KAROS
LOVE’S LODGERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When first my love went forth to roam,
Thy heart was big and free,
And that my love might find a home
Thou gav’st that heart to me.
Alas ! my love was poor and small
For such a noble heart,
And, like a dwarf in palace vast,
Filled but a tiny part.
So thou let the rest as lodgings
To lovers young and gay,
Till ’midst so many I was lost
And sadly stole away.
The years rolled past, and in their course
My love grew mightily,
And, feeling it could fill thy heart,
It came again to thee.
Thou tookst it in, by kindness moved,
And that it should not grieve :
But to make room for it to stay
Those lodgers had to leave.
And now it would be happy quite,
And of all woe bereft,
If only there were hid from sight
The tracks those lodgers left.
Melb.

Townsville Star’s But-
ler competition didn’tevoke
a paralysing amount of
fiendish ingenuity after all.
The prize suggestion offered
was the very antiquated
one that the prisoner should
be seated on a box of dyna-

* * m
Both Maoriland and
Westralia have passed
Adoption Acts. Isn’t it
about time Victoria and
N.S.W. were falling into
line? The incubus that
illegitimate children have
to carry through life in
our smug civilisation is so
terrible that even the ease
such an Act would give
would be merciful. It
would give homes to the
homeless, children to the
childless, and do harm to
nothing, except an absurd
old prejudice of English
law against introducing
Roman law customs. It is
in force in most European
countries, and is a social
amelioration wherever it
obtains. “ Pity the innocent children ”is its key-
note, and that should be sufficient to ensure its
enactment in Australia.

A Melbourne weekly, in its foolish cham-
pionship of “ 'Willie ” Phillips, states that the
murdered man was addicted to cruel and cowardly
forms of sexual immorality.” Which is—as there
is no evidence of it but the murderer’s statement —
very likely a cruel and cowardly lie founded on the
morbid imaginings of the goody-goody crowd.
* * * *
Brisbane (Q.) is putting on frills with its
latest fashionable complaint. If a man gets a
cold, it’s “dengue fever,” if you please. If he
feels seedy in the morning—dengue fever ! If he
can’t turn up to his office on account of bunged
head, his medical certificate reads—dengue fever.
The old “ fever-’n’-ague ”is dead. The man who
doesn’t have dengue fever at least twice a week in
Brisbane is out of the fashion.
* * *
The N S.W. aboriginals who have been
travelling “ on the make ” visited Melbourne and
attempted a corroboree at Richmond Park, one
night during Easter week. In recognition of the
piety and propriety of the smellfu metropolis, the
nigs, danced their famous war-polka in pants, out-
lining the bones with pipeclay on the trouser-legs ;
the result was about as entertaining and elevating
as a pantomime ballet danced in peg-tops and
blankets. No native can corrob. with any effect
in trousers ; in fact, no native can corrob. any-
where without corroborative detail: the nigh ,
the dark bush background, the blazing 1 fires, the
drumming gins, and a costume consisting of a
little Chinese white and the tail of a Manx cat. A
corroboree in a town park in trousers r is a melan-
choly thing, but piteously emblematical of the
general condition of the great Australian native
as was.

The official correspondence in connection
with recent enquiries in Parramatta Gaol is said
to be of a character to open the eyes of those who
think such institutions are the home of harmony,
and not the chrysalis of Kilkenny-catism. Ihe
medical fraternity are reported to have the best of
the argument, wherefore an attempt may be made
to suppress the correspondence.
The average Vic. encampment is a pressing
invitation to the “ foreign foe,” of the great dailies,
to come along, have a good time, and invade I oots-
cray. The other day a batch of Queenscliff
gunners were engaged trying to p°t a
target towed by a launch. The line towing the
target was 300yds. long, yet one shell so narrowly
missed the launch that the helmsman was nearly
drowned by the water it threw over the vessel, and
the crew made a mad raid upon the life-saving ap-
JUMPING TO A CONCLUSION
pliances, under the impression that their craft had
her bottom blown out and was going down.
“ Pursey ” Chirnside is not serving the best inter-
ests of his class in spending =62000 to convince the
Russian and the Chow that the healthiest place in
the world is right “forninst” a Queenscliff gun
in active operation.
* * * •
The breath of the back-blocks ! Between
Birdsville and Hergott Springs last January cattle
under John Clarke were travelling. They were
making the 120-mile dry-stage and were within 20
miles of water, going strong and fresh. About 8
a.m. in sand hill country the sirocco came down,
lasting eight hours. Almost total darkness ac-
companied the hot blast, and the cattle were drop-
ping thickly. At 5 p.m. it cleared up, and the
drovers found 100 bullocks dead out of 500. The.
men had waterbags and were compelled to protect
their heads by covering them in various ways.

Melb. : “The pink of condescension.
Yesterday, on St. Kilda road, in a dust-storm, I
saw a coachee’s belltopper blown off. Lady
jumped out of carriage, ran back a hundred
yards, and got it. Enough to take a hundred
years off Purgatory.” But she was a wise woman
Never on any account let the driver get down
while you are in the trap. Never get into a cab
unless the driver is in his place. If you do, your
life is at the mercy of the horse’s whims. Also,
when engaging a cab, especially at night, always
speak to the driver to see whether he’s sober. But
don’t ask him if lie's sober.
The Kanaka is essentially imitative. The
other day a Maekay “ boy ” of some note died.
The funeral was gorgeous in the extreme and ex-
ceedingly impressive. Coffin in black and silver
rested in the regulation hearse. In the mourning-
coach sat the intimates of deceased, duly lugu-
brious in crape streamers ; while half-a-dozen cabs
were filled by his friends, one vehicle being speci-
ally reserved for ladies. Maekay was much edified.
The Gilbert islanders at Bundaberg carry on a
regular correspondence with their countrymen at
home. The other day a “boy” received a letter
from a relative on the Gilberts, which was unique
in its get-up. The islander had enclosed his
epistle in an Immigration Dept, envelope received
from Queensland. In addition to the address
written thereupon he had superadded a most
faithful imitation of a departmental stamp—the
little drawing was really creditable to the
Polynesian’s artistic ability.

The least joyful result
of Protection in this country
is chewing-gum. Protection
is responsible for Macßobert-
son’s imposing lolly manu-
factory at Fitzroy, Melb.,
where 200 hands are em-
ployed, and Mac., alas ! is
responsible for the introduc-
tion of a glutinous pastime
into Australia. He supplied
chewing-gum to the trade
long before the trade had
any demand for it from the
public. In vain this far-see-
ing producer strove to create
a demand for the Yankee
notion up to the time of the
new bicycle boom. Then,
and not until then, chewing-
gum “ arrived. ” Bilmartin,
or some minor hero on
wheels, was observed to be
working his iron jaw in a
weird, peculiar manner as he
plugged around the track.
Thanks to the emissaries of
Macßobertson, who lay in
wait with infernal machines
labelled “ Chewing - gum
Polka,” the cycling world
took the bit between its
teeth, and to-day, at every
gathering of ‘ ‘ all the cham-
pions,” the steady falling and
rising of innumerable chins
proclaim that Macßobertson
has done the trick.
* • •
Perth (W.A.) has a fine
reservoir, but the local insani-
tary authorities quite forgot
the necessity of reserving the
ground around it and along
the streams which run into
it, so as to prevent pollution.
Cattle get bogged and perish
and become inflated with gas
in these streams, and houses
and dairies and market gar-
dens are situated on the slope
of the reservoir itself, so that
when heavy rains set in the
refuse of many cows and
back-yards washes into the
water-supply. The natural
result is that the dirty city
of the Six Families has the biggest death-rate and
the highest pressure of stench to the square-mch
among all the capitals.
The Mayor of Hobart announces that he
will celebrate the Record Reign by giving a
treat to the poor. This probably means that he
will give them a free feed, during the consumption
of which some fat aldermen will make loyal
speeches to them, telling them what a noble thing
it is to be citizens of the great, glorious British
Empire, and how glad and thankful they should
be to know that they are subjects of the good,
gracious, august, illustrious, &c., sovereign who
was then, &c. And during the deliverance of this
fervid gush some plain-clothes policemen will most
likely be detailed to the duty of seeing that none
of the loyal gorged subjects walk away with the
forks or spoons.
ADVICE TO HEBES.
Heed not the gushing trickster with attractive
curly hair,
And turn your thoughts away from little noses;
Of eyes set close together you must certainly
beware ;
Distrust the sentimental “ toff ” who poses.
But to faults in face and manner Love is usually
blind,
As girls at last discover to their sorrow ;
So carve this precious maxim on the tablets of
your mind —
Beware of any “ boy ” who wants to borroiv.
There is danger in the Gussie with a deeply-
dimpled chin.
Though the prettiest of compliments he passes ;
O, ever be prepared for possibilities of sin
In patriarchs who ogle you through glasses.
All men are vain deceivers, as regards weak
womankind,
They squeeze your hand to-day and skip to-
morrow ; . ,
So carve this special maxim on the tablets ot your
mind —
Beware of one and all who want to borrow.
