A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, April 27,1897.
Mr Dear Myee—
Last week’s sensation, the vice-regal wedding,
was about the biggest pageant of the kind yet
seen in Australia. The most notable circumstance
about the affair was the thoroughly English tone
prevailing. The affair was a combination of the
top-lofty ceremony at St. George’s, Hanover
Square, and the rural simplicity of the squire’s
daughter’s wedding-day in the ancestral manor-
house with a rejoicing tenantry feast in the park ;
only in benighted Sydney there are no tenantry,
and their parts had to be filled by selected “poor”
invited for the occasion. And, as in this low-
down democratic community everybody is, more
or less, related to everybody else, most of the
“ poor ” were probably sisters and cousins and
aunts of the wedding-guests inside the mansion.
And that rather spoils the ancestral-retainer effect.
They didn’t know their lines either—the “ shouts
outside ” were very faint. Six carefully-trained
supers, could have made ten times the “vociferous
cheering ” remarked in the dailies —and nowhere
else.
The event raised all the women in Sydney to
fever-heat but left the men calm and unmoved,
and a crowd of women is not a demonstrative one.
The most thrillingly-absorbed spectators of the
day were the little tattered girls who pressed
themselves against the Cathedral railings and
criticised all the finery on the women on
their way inside the building with as much
bitterness as some of the guests showed
towards each other’s purple and fine linen.
The scene inside the decorated Cathedral was
quite reposeful; the organ hummed, and the
guests faintly rustled with excitement over each
new arrival. As it was a military function, some
local lancers gave verisimilitude to an uncon-
vincingly non-uniformed assemblage, by standing
in the aisle in brown kbakee ; but, oh, how much
more glorious they would have looked had they
been life-guardsmen in block-tin vests, plus any
quantity of gilt string and white horsehair ! The
bridegroom himself was the only steel-cuirassed
man there, and, as he stood at ease waiting for his
future pa-in-law to bring along the bride, the
lighted gas-jets sparkled on his waistcoat and
made him gleam like a newly-polished dish-cover.
To fill in the pause, the choir took three encores
for the bridal hymn; and here I should like to
record, with modest pride, that The Bulletin did
its little share in tying the aristocratic couple for
life, because the office-boy, in a showy freshly-
laundered surplice, was one of the choir voices
that breathed over last Wednesday’s Eden. lam
told that there is yet another church chorister in
the office of this paper; but it is distressing to
find that these examples of pious harmony are
confined to the commercial department.
The Hon. Margaret’s long white satin train was
held by the Brassey children, aunt and nephew,
and there was an innovation about the arrange-
ment of her bridal mosquito-net, which was frilled
all round the edge; it was held primly and
firmly in place by diamond ornaments. The
four bridesmaids were be-chiffoned in white
over yellow satin. Mr. Ferguson, gushed
over in the Herald as a “good best man,”
was a foil to his glittering brother in black velvet
coat, knee breeches trimmed with shiny buttons,
and black silk stockings. A man has moral
courage of a high order to get into a fancy cos-
tume in the day-time, and go outside into garish
sunlight looking like a liquorice-stick. But he
was supported by the two Govs., who were in
gorgeous military rig, and by every man—there
were but few—who had any sort of distinctive
toggery to call his own. The Herald man should
have copied the bold Sir Bedivere in the poem,
who closed his eyelids lest the gems should blind
his purpose. The scribe set out to describe the
show, but the glittering shine of the bridegroom’s
waistcoat evidently got into his eyes and dazzled
him, for his column absolutely scintillates with
steel cuirass.
The bridal coacn was drawn by the four cele-
brated Brassey chestnuts—l don’t mean the navy,
the Sunbeam, “ my late father,” and the Empiah,
but equine chestnuts—lent for the occasion, and
manned by postillions with gigantic floral horse-
shoes pinned to their livery. Inside Government
House a lightning change had been worked since
the previous evening’s ball, for carpets were down,
stale refreshments cleared away, and the wedding-
gifts displayed conveniently. All the jewellery
was in carefully-locked glass cases, which gleamed
with diamonds loudly ticketed with donors’
names. Most of the presents from England were
in their cases, and looked small and ugly, so I
suppose they were family heirlooms of great value.
There were some queer donations—an antiquated
silver shoe-buckle looked quaint-—and some cen-
tury-old paste buttons ; also a venerable walking-
stick used as an umbrella-handle. Relics, how-
ever, were rare—most gifts were modern, including
a pair of kangaroos and a pedigreed pug, the latter
being appropriately the gift of a blue-blooded local
connection of England’s premier duke ; the live-
stock wasn’t on view.
The most swagger gifts now—barring jewels—
seem to be silver photo.-frames or leather pocket-
books and cases monogramed in raised metal.
There were whole flights of fans, all tiny except
one vast ostrich-plume creation, and many offer-
ings which should have had advt. stuck on them.
Some donors posed proudly before the shrine on
which their own gifts were laid inviting comment.
Women pushed and elbowed each other to get
near the cases, and men stood around limply and
helplessly as men always stand at weddings. The
bridal cake and champagne were absorbed in
silence without formal speeches, and towards the
end of the proceedings many of the house-party
drifted and returned, minus their wedding-finery,
in everyday attire. The borrowed equipage was
still to the fore to drive the couple away on the
first stage of the honeymoon—they have gone to
Dangar Island on the Hawkesbury— and the
family hurled rice and slippers at them in the good
old-fashioned way, which fact has been already
reverently noted by many scribes and the throwers
of the “pearly grain ” respectfully named.
About the two Govt. House balls—one on Tues-
day, 20th, and the second on the 27th—there has
been much discussion as to whether it was really
more distinguished to have been asked to No. 1 or
to No. 2. Celebrities were invited to both, but
smaller fry were distributed equally between the
two evenings. At the first ball, which crowded
the two dining-rooms and verandah, there was a
distinct flavor of forthcoming wedding about the
proceedings. The bride of next day was
much in evidence, wearing a yellow trained
gown ; in the grounds the engaged couple’s
monogram was displayed in colored lights, an
arrangement which a sudden shower prevented
many folk from admiring, and the band played a
specially-com posed waltz. Any man possessing
a uniform wore it. Lord Brassey’s coat-tails were
stiff with gold embroidery, and the visiting Kil-
morey—“ Killmurray ”is the c’rect sound—had
blue ribbon tied round his manly chest. Windsor
uniforms and military and naval coats were quite
common, but the impression lingering a week
after is that the white-silk calves mostly wanted
fattening. Our own Gov. could never get a billet
as a Jeames, unassisted by cotton-wool.
The gowning at the ball was quite up to begin-
ning of the season mark, though some of those
elderly dowagers who are animated X-rays at
revealing other people’s family skeletons pointed
out two or three renovated last year’s confections.
A cloud of debutantes, looking as innocent as
Spring chickens just come to market, blanched
the scene with their white frocks ; to balance their
juvenility, several sucking Nelsons from the war-
ships were present, and the midshipmites were led
up to the dais and introduced to the occupants.
One of the prettiest girls was in heliotrope with a
fluffy bodice, and one of the prettiest dresses was
of white satin with turquoise embroidery on the
well-hung skirt. One dress had a bodice like a
full-blown pink rose ; and bright green, suggestive
of a John’s vegetable-cart, was much worn. For
hair-dressing, paste-combs—they look like dia-
monds and cost eighteenpence or so—are now in-
dispensable. Some heads had feathery aigrettes
sticking in them —in white they look as if the
wearers had been sleeping on a feather-bolster and
had forgotten to comb their hair.
A big crowd of the “Nicest’’was beautifully
had over the departure of Capt. Ferguson and
wife on their honeymoon trip. In the morning
papers it was officially announced that they would
leave by the express for Moss Yale, and some 300
persons gladly bought railway platform-tickets;
but the gallant wooer and his consort had arranged
their plans well, for they quietly slipped off to
the railway-station, catching the seven to five train
to the Hawkesbury. There were only a few rail-
way officials on the platform who were in the
know,” and a few people seeing friends off. The
crowd on the express platform formed all sorts of
conjectures as to the non-appearance of the newly-
wedded couple, and little dreamt that they had
left a quarter of an hour before.
The Nicest of this city slaved at pleasuring all
last. week. Amidst all the numerous outings the
anniversary of the Hampden Club was celebrated
at Centennial Park on Thursday afternoon when a
few bedraggled enthusiasts met in the teeming
ram to inaugurate the second session. Nothing
went on except talk and tea-drinking, and although
the blessings of two gubernatorial houses were
bestowed on the function, the general impression
is that it was not only the wet which threw a
damper on Thursday’s proceedings-interest in
the club seems to be fizzling out. Perhaps “ the
Nicest people ” see so much of each other that they
are tired of meeting, even in—immortal phrase !
the nicest possible way.”
Fashionable waltzing is fast and furious; the
dreamy glide is out of form, and reversing never
was in, though in every ball-room an occasional
couple may still be seen indulging in crab-like
gambols. At St. Vincent’s ball, in the Centennial
Hall last Wednesday evening, Mrs. Freeman
Thomas and her partner darted around the room
in double-quick time, as did all the other youthful
members of the vice-regal set—several of whom
by the way, were gloomily attired in black gowns!
In the first lancers, fancy sets were formed ;
flowery shepherdesses were partnered by men with
colored facings to their black coats, little Japs
tottered about with badly-fixed sashes, making
the wearers appear like brown-paper parcels that
wanted pinning ; a girl, got up k la Lalla Rookh
hung about with many pearls, was the belle of the
Indian set. The “basket figure” of the state
set was badly mixed, owing to a bashfulness about
putting arms round vice-regal waists ; the others
just romped through it in the usual free-and-easy
way. Friends of the dancers were in the bal-
comes, bringing strong glasses to bear on the
dresses, and audibly counting the girls’ chances of
partners. The body of onlookers stayed until
nearly midnight, swapping anecdotes about the
folks below, raking up old family histories, and
generally commenting on things like a Greek
chorus. People have odd ideas of enjoyment—it
isn’t a very brilliant role to be cast for a leading
part at a charity ball, but to wander wearily on to
the fence as a mere spectator must, I should think
be most depressingly dull.
This festive season has brought out some bright
new dance-music, and the popular remnants of last
season, especially the sentimental strains of “ Ma
belle adoree,” are still included in every high-class
programme. De Groen’s waltz, “Les Fiances”
was blared forth for the first time rather too loudly
at Govt. House, but in the larger area of the Cen-
tennial Hall next night sounded very catchy and
inspiring. They are playing everywhere now a
rattling good polka, “ Rum-tum-tum,” which
would make you want to get up and circulate if
you were 90 and crippled in both feet; the air
I rank Lawton used to whistle as he mixed the
drinks in Chinatown is also a rousing polka. Of
women’s music “ Amorayle ” is chosen for one trip
every programme, but Maud FitzStubbs’
Bridal is late in appearing. New lancers are
just a gay jumble of comic songs and tunes of the
moment.
The unobtrusive flower-show opened in the
rainy dark of Thursday last in the Centenary
Hall, York-street. Chrysanthemum-worship is
not such a craze here as in Melbourne. There
when they have the annual show, they hire the
Town Hall, get Lady Brassey to open it, and
invite all the big-wigs and any stray aristocratic
visitors available, turn on a brass band or the citv
organist to play “God Save,” and announce a
highly attractive musical programme ” each
evening as long as the flowers hold out. It is the
proper thing to gloat over bloated “Edward
Molyneaux ” and “Lady Lawrence,” and if you
don’t know the difference between the Jap and
Chinese types of blooms you are quite out of it
socially.
A well-frilled grand-stand at Randwick on
Saturday showed the Nicest satellites still going
strong after the week’s outing. Even Thursday’s
rain didn’t damp their ardor, but brought about
some new ideas in wet-weather cloaks. A visitor
wore an alpaca, one shaped like a man’s, and
defied the elements. Saturday’s sun brought out
all the plummy gowns again ; it is an aggressive
tint, but hugely popular, and wearers have
encored their triumphs in purple many times.
Next to purple, green holds the day, the red reign
is over except in scrappy ties and belts, though
one of the most fascinating gowns of the Autumn
meeting was of a white silky stuff topped by a
flaming felt hat with artistic curves and black
plumes.
At Lawrence Campbell’s recital in the Y.M.C. A.
Hall last Saturday evening the elocutionist’s
newest piece, “Rio Grande’s Last Race ” (by
“ Banjo ”) was the most applauded by the
audience. “The Faithful Soul’’ was well done
and accompanied by Miss Lilian Frost on the
organ, but the poem is a chestnut now, and
those “ 1000 years of torture ” are being taken
out by listeners to the uttermost moment.
A little girl in Kate Greenaway frock was quite
charming in a French dolly style, and Burns-
Walker baritoned twice and was encored twice,
and between songs went to the Palace and sang
four times there—which makes eight ballads in
one evening. The vocalist will have to hire a
special composer to write him fresh songs, for the
present output of sentimental ditties can’t keep
pace with the demand.
The annual ball in aid of the educational fund
of the Women’s Society for the Prevention of
Cruelty to Animals, N.S.W. —this lengthy title is
colloquially boiled down into “the Dog Ball”
will be held as announced on the cards, which are
already out, in Paddington town hall, on Thurs-
day evening, May 20th. It is to be a costume
dance, so dress will depend on the taste and fancy
of the wearer. Mrs. Russell-Jones is the hon.
treasurer, Mrs. F. Chapman and Miss Martin hon.
secs.
Parliament was opened on Tuesday morning
with gun-firing, military turn-outs, and all the
usual convulsions. The feminine crowd in the
Council Chamber was large, and suggestive of the
country member’s wife in its dressing, which was
not very smart. The vice-regal party included
Lady Hampden, in grass-lawn with blue spots,
and. Lady Brassey, in white silk with pale-blue
bodice. His Ex. was in one of his many uniforms,
and was supported by the Vic. Gov., also in much
gold-lace. Vice-regal changes in clothing have
been so numerous of late that it is quite hard to
keep up with them, and even Government House
officials are sometimes in the dark as to the parti-
cular meaning of the uniform their chief is wear-
ing. The Gov. read his speech about the rabbit
plague, and the drought, and other cheerful
subjects, in a tone of aristocratic languor, and he
hasn’t a single final g.
On Tuesday morning last Mr. Coghlan, of
N.S. W. Public Service Board, was married at St.
Patrick’s, Church Hill, by Rev. Le Rennetel to
Miss Helen M. Donnelly, daughter of the late
M.L.A. for Cowra. The newly-wedded couple
intends to catch the outgoing mail-boat at Melb.
and go on a world’s tour.
The confidential raffle is highly popular just now
among women as a useful way of getting rid of
unconsidered trifles at a reasonable profit. Times
are so hard that the maid doesn’t get her mistress’s
misfit costumes as she used to. The smart woman
“ puts up ” the garment for a shilling sweep among
her friends, and acquaintances not in such a toney
set as the raffle-promoter are very often proud to
recount how they have joined in Mrs. Blank’s
raffle —it is a sort of guarantee of respectability
with which they can awe bosom friends on a still
lower rung of the ladder. Bikes have come for-
ward so freely of late in this form of barter that
raffle-quotations have been much lowered in conse-
quence.
A banquet was lately given by a recently-
elected Mayor to his aldermen in a southern
suburban town hall. When hungering guests
arrived half an hour before feeding-time, they
found others waiting; the invited crowd
turned up to a man. But when the “ ban-
quet was at length before their expectant eyes
and mouths, corned beef, mutton, and save-
loys, washed down with “ tanglefoot ” and whisky,
was what the disappointed diners fed on instead of
the expected turkey and fizz.
A rubicund ex-mayoress declares that it took
her three years from the expiry of her term of
office to “drop” the ineligible people she was
forced to know in her capacity of mayoress.
A distracted musical public is never able to dis-
cover, from the wrangling of singing-masters,
what really is the right way to sing. A would-be
pupil with a voice is deterred from one instructor
because his “ method ” is wrong, and, perhaps,
after talung a spell of another vocal teacher, finds
out that “serious defects” have to be remedied
when a third is tried, till the discouraged possessor
of a fine singing faculty is like the old man with
his ass. According to these experts, the one
thing you vnustn t do, if you have a good voice is
to open your mouth and sing; until you have
learnt several new and expensive methods of
swimming, you may not on any account go into
the water.
The Women’s Franchise League in England,
through its hon. sec., has invited “all women
who have been elected to public bodies or have
achieved eminence in any walk of life,’’ etc., etc.
to sign a petition to Queen Victoria, and Austra-
lian women are asked to join in the movement,
f 6 i e - I s no^li . n 8' m the wordy document, copies
of which are printed in divers “women’s columns ”
expressing a yearning desire for any definite
object except a general wish to grovel. H.M. is
fulsomely flattered on her “sympathetic heart”
which rejoices in the fact that women are now
enabled to earn their own living in many wavs
implying an immense deal of hard work
and she is also informed gushingly that one
Royal word of Sympathy with the progress
which women have achieved during your Ma-
jesty s illustrious reign, one expression of gracious
confidence and hope in the happy results which
may be expected to follow from still further en-
larging the area brought under the Influence of
Women, would greatly promote and consolidate
throughout your Majesty’s realms those principles
of Justice and Equity which will sustain in per-
manence the vast Empire over which your Majesty
reigns The whole petition is in the same vaguely
grandiloquent strain, but the “loyal sentiment”
gets there all the same. Why not write “advt. ”
at the end, after all the signatures ?

the departing braves. Going down in the tram,
ih o t;^ vec * °/ anything but the severity of

overhauled, weighed and measured every man-jack
oi the chosen. I act never comes up to Fancy. The
boys looked very neat, and almost too spruce in
brand-new hats, donated by a local manufacturer
but hardly gigantic enough for exacting feminine
requirements. Brown uniforms«are trim and
smart, but unimposing. Besides, few Australians
look, as big as they really are. At all events these
didn t, as they hung over the side, beguiling the
long wait by the time-honored device of weeping
fictitious tears and wringing out their handker-
chiefs. General Holled-Sinith, impressive in dark
blue picked out with scarlet, and flowing military
frock-coat, took up a commanding upper-deck
situation, and, supported on either side by a short
brother officer, stood haughtily surveying the
civilian crowd below until it was time to g 0
ashore. The pretty little kangaroo, travelling’Ome
as regimental pet, seemed half-dead already with
excitement and terror. As a rule, Joeys are not
good tourists. They need fresh grass and green
stun, and, unless you take along boxes sown with
small salad and such-like for their benefit, gener-
ally give up the ghost in the tropics.

“ If the Easter Baby Show at the Exhibition
was confined to offspring of Druids it reflected
some credit upon the Ancient Order of mas-
queraders. Exhibits, as a rule, showed an improve-
ment upon the average baby in avoirdupois weight
and general conduct, and the class of infant nose
which requires constant attention was conspicuous
by its absence. All the same, the entertainment
lacked thrill. One looked in vain for anything
like a replica of the Brobdingagian infant who
starred at the great original show and drew a
cheque for =£6o from manager Dampier. But
that was during!! boom-time, when every kind of
Melbourne property went in for inflation. Liberal
genuine specimens of the cap and pinny nurse-
brigade flitted round and supplied the mothers
with milk, &c., at intervals, the juvenile competi-
tors getting the benefit of t|ie committee’s gene-
rosity second-hand. There were several sets of
twins and a highly-deservingbatchof triplets, who
appeared to rank second in human interest to a
dropsical-looking girl labelled ‘Special prize—7
years, Bst. 101b.’ This alarming object had been
compelled by bulging cheeks to cultivate a hard,
uncanny squint, which gave her an appearance of
being more than 7.
“The pathos of a baby-show is worthy of a
poe.t s best Sunday pen. It comes in at the tail-
end of the contest, when the numerous mothers
who had expected to capture a JllO prize, and
were prepared to see their respective exhibits
borne in triumph round the building at the head
± n ban . d ’ come slowl y to the conclusion
that the baby in the next pen is running rings
round their blighted hopes. They are a sad sub-
jeet for reflection, these newly-awakened mothers.
JLn some oases they don’t know what to make of
the situation. But in most cases they catch one
another s wistful eye, over the head of the trium-
phant infant who is holding a levee in between
their deserted compartments, and mutely agree
that the multitude has entered into a vile con-
spiracy to boycott the brightest gems in the show
and tickle the legs of vastly inferior productions.

An unmarried medico who has a propensity for
theacrical skirts-or tights for that matter-is
struggling once more in the throes of love with a
fair actress at present in our midst. There are no
impediments this time in his way. He may brave
matrimony if the lady be willing to share the risk.

display at theatres and concerts has been the

ment by Melb. women, recently lost one of her
most gaudy and glittering diamond-brooches, and
affected to be much concerned at the loss. Her
fi lends urged her to offer a reward for its recovery,
whereupon she confessed that it wasn’t worth 55.,
as it wa,s merely a paste replica of one of her
famous jewels, ‘ don’t-you-know.’ By way of
explanation she added that ‘one never brings
genuine jewels out heah, merely monuments of
them in paste, so to speak; the real stones one
leaves at one’s bankers’ at ’Ome.’
. Melb. sassiety modiste par excellence is
said to be laying plans to visit New York with a
view to giving Yankee women refined gowning in
exchange for many dollars. On the principle that
only a lady knows how to wear a dress,’ this
clever woman asserts that only a lady knows how
to design one, and with the influential letters she
will carry to the land of the dollar, and her really
excellent taste and invention, she should certainly
have success. After a period in New York it is
her intention to storm that stronghold of conser-
vative woman London, where she will set up in
the most aristocratic quarters.

Personal Items.
Henry Lawson has gone to Maoriland
again.
In Castlemaine Benevolent Asylum is a
nonagenarian, John Dunn, who was born in Sydney
in 1802.
Graham Berry, first elected in 1860, has
sat more years in Vic. Assembly than any other
present member.
N.S.W. Governors Biigh, Bourke, Her-
cules Robinson, and Hampden have had daughters
married in Sydney.
M. L. Krotel, at one time organist in
Ward Beecher’s famous church at Brooklyn, is
now residing in Sydney.
Ben Tillett.: “ Broken Hill is dear to my
heart, and I will certainly pay it a visit. It was
from Broken Hill we received our first assistance
in our hour of need.”
Pathology-professor Allen, of Melb. Uni-
versity, is the H. B, Allen who perpetrated that
awful “ Australian Anthem ”in the Argus. He
should stick to cancers and let cantos alone.
Mrs. Ann Taylor, the oldest known native-
born Australian, died the other day at Parra-
matta, aged 98. She was born at Toongabbie and
never was 50 miles from home. She leaves over
150 descendants.
One result of mutual interests in the
representation of S. A. at the Convention has been
to heal the personal breach between Kingston
and Symon, Q.C’s. Baker, however, refuses to
forget his animosity.
The appointment by Chief Justice Griffith
of Mr. Edward Douglas as associate to himself is
a graceful reminder of the time when Premier
John Douglas (Edward’s father) appointed young
man Griffith to the Attorney-Generalship in his
Cabinet.
Dan. O’Connor has been wondering
whether the whole fun of his famous “ App’int
Maloney ” minute “ lies in the fact that the man’s
name happened to be Malone}'.” Only half of it;
the other half is in the fact that Dan.’s name
“ happened to be ” O'Connor.
The De Moleyns, brothers of Governoress
Hopetown, are reported to be doing well at coffee-
growing on their plantation near Cairns (Q.).
Coffee cultivation is driving the sugarcane off
much of the high lands in that quarter, as a com-
paratively small capital can work a plantation.
Englishmen say there is no aristocracy in
Australia. Nonsense ! Take Queensland, for
instance. There, there is an aristocracy of three.
One cooks for a surveyor, one travels for a soft-
goods house, and the other is a wood-and-water
joey at a Western hotel. No aristocracy, forsooth !
To be Irish was once, in popular opinion,
anyhow, synonymous with being disloyal and
revolutionary. Times have changed. It was the
wild, broguey wail of Hennessy and MTnerney
which at last decided Melb. Hospital Committee
to fool away part of its scanty funds on an illu-
mination in honor of the Record Reign. Most of
the members were indifferent or mildly hostile,
but the rich, fruity brogues of Hennessy and
MTnerney rose on the air turn about until the
committee caved in.
MUTUALLY DECEIVED.
HIDER (who has returned to stable with hired horse, indignantly) : “ You have deceived
me; you said the horse was quiet.”
DEALER (affably): “We have both been deceived, sir. You told me you could ride.”

In the first criminal libel trial heard in
Sydney—that against Dr. Wardell, of the Aus-
TRAHAN —and in the most recent —that against
John Norton the other day—the juries failed to
agree. Dr. Warded, however, insisted on being
tried afresh at once or released from bail, and the
latter course was adopted.
The late James Fletcher, whose statue
was unveiled, the other day, at Newcastle,
N.S.W., was an aggravatingly popular man. If
you set out to walk a street-block with him, you
would not manage to get to the other corner in less
than three hours, so patient was he " ith the billet-
seekers and beggars who bailed him up.
N.S.W. Justice G. B. Simpson’s old chum.
Sir Baker Russell, Commandant of the Bengal
Army, has been appointed to chief command, with
Lieut.-General rank, over the Indian forces.
Simpson and Russell were such inseparables in the
dmgy-looking old King’s School, Parramatta, that
they were often guyed by the other boys.
The memory of the fight at Eureka Stock-
ade in December, 1854, is revived by the death of
Samuel M'Kee, one of the 40th Regiment engaged
in the storming. M'Kee was a corporal in the
regiment, and was 75 years of age at his death,
which took place last week in the Ballarat Bene-
volent Asylum. Martin Cusack, another 40th
man, occupies the bed next to that in which
M'Kee died.
The Adelaide shorthand writers raised a
paean of thanksgiving when, on the third day of
Convention Committee, Toby Barton, owing to
bronchial irritation, temporarily retired from the
leadership in favor of Downer and O’Connor.
Barton had a way of immediately answering every
questioner, instead of waiting a reasonable time,
and replying to them all in one speech : conse-
quence, endless repetitions.
Bendigo’s Mayor, it seems, has been con-
nected with the city’s Easter Fair from his
earliest years. First of all he was a boy engaged
in leading a blind man around to collect money
for the fair; then he figured prominently as a
member of an amateur nigger company, and now,
in his municipal greatness, he has achieved the
honor of declaring the fair open. Thus it will be
seen that nothing is impossible to perseverance.
Ballance has been gone five years, and his
,£OOO statue has just been unveiled in Wellington
(M.L.) with a £5O mortgage on it. Ballance never
cadged from anybody when he was alive, and now
that he is dead the mournful, soulless hat is being
sent round in his name, until the brim is flabby
with much handling and the nap is all worn away,
to lift the debt off his cenotaph. He had a deep
contempt for statues, nevertheless he has got one.;
and he had some regard for art, therefore his
image is naturally as inartistic and as wholly un-
like him as it well could be.
Said a politician to a Bulletin correspon-
dent shortly after the funeral of Ash, M.P. : “I
couldn’t help thinking, as I stood at the grave and
saw the widow and her children, now left without
money to fight the world, that poor Ash had
always opposed every measure likely to help the
working-classes or to ameliorate their lot. And
the thought would come also that this stalwart
Individualist, whose political principle was that
everyone must have full liberty to oppress his
neighbor, was hardly cold in death before a sub-
scription was started for the members of his
family.”
“Pope” M’Eachran, the G.O.M. of Vic.
Presbyterianism, entered his 72nd year on April 27.
In the old days, M‘Eachran used to preach in
English in the evening, and in his native Hielan’
brogue in the morning. His church in Carlton,
Melb. (St. Andrew’s) is known as the Gaelic
church to this day.
It is currently reported that Attorney-
General Want will personally prosecute the alleged
arch-assassin, Butler. If he does, it will be in
harmony with a professional usage, ignored by
modern holders of the office, which regards it as
incumbent on the highest officer of the legal pro-
fession to be personally associated with the prose-
cution of all the more celebrated offenders, so that
he may personally bear the credit or odium which
attaches to the success or failure of the Crown’s
action. Of late years this practice has fallen into
disuse.
Instead of honoring the record-reign, as
suggested, by liberating Davies and Sampson, the
two insurrectionary prisoners who refuse to petition
for release, President Kruger proposes to Celebrate
the occasion by clapping another year on to their
sentences, unless they cave, in and pay their fines.
And yet people say there’s no humor in Com Paul!
LOCAL COLORING .
“ Now, my little boy, what were the seven plagues of
Egypt? ”
“ Rabbits, Bathurst burrs, grass-hoppers, native

rabbits! ”
Apropos the Dailey statue. “Plain Bill”
once had a bad half-hour with Archbishop Polding
because he went to a fancy ball, given by Mayor
Charles Moore, in the garb of a R.C. priest.
Dailey in appearance was half-priest, half-come-
dian, and he had the best instincts of the best
men of both professions.
A gallery-man made note of the bows of
members of the Federal Convention towards the
President’s chair. He found them strangely
assorted—and some of them characteristic. The
palm for courtliness is awarded to R. C. Baker and
Solomon, President Kingston’s greatest enemies
in the Convention. Generally, the South Aus-
tralians are more punctilious about the bow than
the others. Both Turner and Trenwith content
themselves with curious swings of the head ; and
G. H. Reid—well, he isn’t built for bowing, as he
bows too much the other way. Most of the Wes-
tralians, after the first entrance, took the bow as
read.
There has been a violent quarrel between
two quondam friends in the office of a certain
Australian weekly paper, and the following passage
in a leading article is supposed by some to be a
stroke in the game :
“ Man is the only animal who makes no friends amongst
his own species.” How many out of the thousands of the
genu? Homo who scan these lines can lay their hands on
their hearts and say, with conviction, that they have a
friend in whom they trust But most persons
know at least one dog that is more truly “ the friend of
man ” in selfless fidelity and mute unreasoning sympathy
than any human being he can number amongst his
acquaintances. When Sydney Smith was asked by Land-
seer, the celebrated animal-painter, to sit for his portrait,
he retorted with a quotation from the Scriptures : “ Is thy
servant a dog, that he should do this thing ? ” Who has
not, at some crisis in his life, when he was heartsick,
world-weary, and betrayed by the friends he had counted
on, looked at a dog with the unspoken question, “ Will
you too ‘ turn dog’ on me? ” and seemed to see in those
faithful eyes the answer, “Is thy servant a man that he
should do this thing?”
Col. Angelo, Governor of Rottnest Island
Native Prison (W.A.), an old Crimean and Indian
Mutiny man, claims to have been the first in the
province to officially wire to headquarters the
news of the earliest local discovery of gold. A
miner went to him when he was Govt. Resident
in Roebourne, and, showing a piece of stone with
s ime gold in it, said that he had picked it up to
throw at a crow. Whereupon, so goes the story,
the Colonel excitedly rushed away to wire the
golden tidings in these terms : “A miner picked
up a stone to throw at a crow, but, seeing it
glisten, found it was gold-bearing.” More par-
ticulars were to be forwarded later. The wire
reached Perth with only the words : “A miner
picked up a stone to throw at a crow ” in it.
There was some stray humor in the Govt, offices
then, and a message was sent back to Angelo
thus : “ Did he ? What became of the crow ? ”
The gallant Colonel doesn’t like any talk about
that wire.

When murderer Deeming was being con-
veyed by rail from Perth to Albany, all the
wayside .stations were crowded with the
local populations eager to catch a
glimpse of the Windsor hero. At
every stoppage the assassin would
be welcomed with loud hoots and
execrations, and at one township
a bibulous spectator was so filled
with horror at the man’s iniquity
that he rushed to the open
window of the carriage and
lashed out a vicious blow at
the prisoner. But his aim
had been spoilt by alcoho-
lic revels, and instead of
landing on Deeming’s jaw the
fist struck the prominent pro-
boscis of the police inspector on
the opposite seat. “ Arrest that
man,” yelled the infuriated officer
to a local constable smirking on
the platform. The copper rushed
diligently to a gaping small boy,
and scruffed him violently up to
the window. “ Not that one—
not that one,” explained the in-
spector, letting the blood drain
from his nose to the permanent
way; “that man there in the
brown suit and the white hat,”
And he pointed directly to the
offender, who was standing alone.
The constable rushed round ex-
citedly and grabbed an inoffen-
sive old fellow in a black suit
and a belltopper. “ Net that
one, either,” yelled the exaspe-
rated officer. “ Are you mad or
blind or what ? ” The local copper
released the aged one, and, sidling up to
the window, pleaded pathetically : “Sh ! It’s oil
very well for you ; but I’ve got to live here. That
chap’s one of the councillors. ”
John Nairne, an old Cairns identity, now-
identified with New Guinea business, once saved
a Chow from drowning in the Condamine. The
pig-tailed one was in possession of £9O hard cash,
and he promptly and effusively proffered Nairne
a “saxpence.” Nairne has very decided opinions
on the Chinese question.
Fox’s painting of Softgoods Sargood is
said, by a gushing lady correspondent, to represent
that immortal warrior in his “favorite attitude
when addressing the ’Ouse on any important
measure.” He stands “straight, soldierly (!),
calm, resolute, with glasses in hand and a liatid-
somfPerimson flower in his coat ’’—the crimson
flower being probably an emblem of his educated
taste for berlud and battle. Artist Fox was a
long time waiting for a chance to catbh the Great
Man in one of his “great ” moments, but at last
he got him in the act of calmly cursing the Com-
panies Bill. Consequently Sargood wears—in th 9
words of the lady aforesaid —“ his most becoming
expression.”
Q. Premier Nelson, now on the sea, is a
very bland old bluffer. He will receive any-
body cordially, but rushes people through in this
style: “ D'you want employment ? H’m ! Have
a whisky goes to a cupboard and produces
bottle and soda-water —“ sit down and have a
smoke ! What are your qualifications ? What’s
your line?” The visitor thinks he has dropped
into fatness. Ten minutes later he’s outside, with
a free invitation to “ call often ’’—while Nelson’s
messenger has his instructions. Also, if you ask
Nelson for “ a letter ” recommending either your-
self, or a scheme, he scratches one out at once,
and reads it to you, in such a manner that you
feel sure of your achievement. When you get out-
side and study the sentences, they are all non-
committal and meaningless. The writer saw one
such letter, now famous among business advertis-
ing firms in Brisbane. But the holder was cute,
too. He showed the letter always when canvass-
ing, with the signature, and he read the letter
with impressive unction, but he never let it out of
his hands ! And he nearly always got an order.
Eugene, the prophet, is reported to have
turned up again in Maoriland. On his last ap-
pearance this wild-haired seer announced that
M.L. was shortly to be demolished by an earth-
quake —now nearly two years overdue ; and his
followers affrightedly packed up their aggregate
carpet-bag and fled with him to the interior of
Africa, somewhere by the Zambesi or the Limpopo.
There the prophet left them, a forlorn assemblage
of lost cranks deposited on the damp grass in a
strange land, and started for the Cape and thence
to America. What he profited by the game was
never accurately known. Some of his followers
set forth to walk back, and the rest are either
there yet, or else they wandered further into the
interior and were chewed by lions or run over by
the inconsiderate buffalo, or some other fate befel
them. Many respectable old maids in poke bonnets
and blue spectacles and mittens were left out in
the cold, to associate with the affable giraffe and
make a precarious existence by pursuing the
hartebeest on foot, as the result of Eugene’s un-
fulfilled prophecy. Now, the leader of the expe-
dition has apparently announced himself as the
Messiah, while a local man who has joined him is
in business as John the Baptist, and instead of
having his head shoved into a boiling spring,
which looks like a fair thing, this howling dervish
is gathering followers and doing well. There iB
no pious impostor so blatant and vulgar and
obviouslv bogus that he can’t do well in Maori-
land. Strangely enough, too, the law in that
paternally-governed country seems to have no
machinery which can deal with prophets, and
people who raise the dead, and similar hairy
shams.

S.A. Hansard report of the Convention
proceedings covers well over 1000 pages.
The Australian* Star’s first great go to
constitute itself the David Syrne of Protectionist
N.S. Wales has dismally slumped. Down went
M’Ginty Cowlishaw.
Speaker Abbott : “ What prejudices have
I ? I have never been out of the colonies ! ”
Abbott evidently imagines that you can’t get
prejudices inside “the colonies."
Premier Seddon told a Wellington (M.L.)
audience, the other evening, that the more he had
seen of Australia during his recent visit therein,
the more he thought of Maoriland and its people.
Quite so. Maoriland made “ Dick ” its Premier.
Noblesse oblige.
No split in the Labor-party in Bananaland f
Talk to some of the Labor-members for five
minutes and you’ll soon find whether there’s a
split or not. Glassey. Hoolan. King. Cross, Keogh
and M’Donnell may be said to form a pro-Q.N.B.
party —a party that will certainly lose more than
one constituency next election. Western men are
very bitter on the financial question.
It is the constant boast of the Mongrelian
Govt, that all resumed portions of runs are as
good as open for selection, and only require to be
had for the asking. For three or four years
would-be selectors have been vainly petitioning
the Nelson Ministry to thro v open the resumed
portions of the Hamilton run, near Mirani. Why
vainly? Well, does the run belong to a Cabinet
Minister?
Labor member Glassey’s speech at Bun-
daberg makes the men who b‘lieved in him feel
sick. And the logic of it! There are 500 persons
in his constituency directly and indirectly em-
ployed by the Q.N. Bank, w hose votes and billets,
according to him. would have been the price of
liquidation. As if the industries and the men to
work them wouldn't go on all the same despite the
mere change of hands. It is no: that, though. It
is—which of the trimmers will get the expected
portfolio?
W. H. Groom, M.L.A., told a Hughenden
interviewer that the supply of land in Queensland
is far short of the demand. Probably true, since
the “ eyes ” of the country have long since been
picked out. Groom is chairman of the Lands
Commission, and those who think they know say
that this apparently unpremeditated outburst of
candor on the part of “the Daddy of the House,’
ia the first move to gently lead up to the recom-
mendation that Govt, shall repurchase many free-
holds. Watch, therefore, those particular free-
holds bought, and note those that are in pawn to
the Q.N. Bank.
Premier Reid, speaking on the proposed
retention of the right of ap|>eal to the British
Privy Council, said ’’ He had full sympathy with
the desire for scientific law, which, unfortunately,
only great banking institutions could afford to ob-
tain.” On the other hand, Minister Carrnthers
fiercelv denounced the proposal to abolish Privy
Council appeals as “ one of the first steps towards
separation from the mother country.” They had,
he added, too few links binding them to the old
country. He urged them not to jeopardise
Federation by adopting a proposal which meant
one step nearer to separation from the mother
land. Mr. Carruthers should be “Sir Joseph”
at a very early date.
Turner. Isaacs. Berry. Wise, Kingston,
Coekburn. Trenwith, Deakin and Gordon voted
for Gordon's motion that members of the Federal
Parliament should be paid £5OO instead of £4OO
per annum. When Trenwith said that £5OO a year
would not be too much to pay a man who devoted
all his time to the work of the Commonwealth,
and that the choice of the people could only be
made wide enough by giving fair pay to those
who could not otherwise afford to become mem-
bers. Zeal, m the arrogance of his Pimperial be-
baublement. interjected : “Yes, and keep a lot of
idle fellows doing nothing.” But Trenwith much
riled Zeal by showing now, before payment erf
members, members paid themselves.
It was decided by a majority of only one
that under Federation no State shall have the
right to prohibit the use of opium or alcohol or
anv other article within it* borders —there must
be Australian prohibition or no prohibition at all.
This is almost the only ir.>tance in which the
Federal idea prevailed over the provincial one.
It was Deakin who raised the provincial Cold Tea
theorv. but the idea of Yictoria prohibiting drink,
and thus blasting its splendid and ever-growing
wine-industry and cutting off £S>.O,OOO a year erf
its contribution to the Federal revenue, and yet
getting its share erf the surplus all the same, made
the hair stand up on the head of every member
who felt that he was the only member fitted for
the billet of Federal Treasurer. And there were
15 of them.

Ex-Separationist Philp at Cairns : “ When
things are good in the south the north seldoms
reaps any benefit, but when they are good in the
north the south invariably reaps the benefit.
Northerners always go south to spend their money.”
Also, when there's a rat in the southern political
camp he never rats in favor of the north; and
when there's a rat in the north he always rats in
favor of the south —doesn’t he, Bob ?
John Forrest, who returned to Perth
from th<- Federal Convention on April 14, runs a
general election in Westralia, and leaves for Eng-
land in the middle of Mav -if he is still Premier.
He is so sure of being still Premier that he has his
bag packed for the journey to London. In no other
Australian province could the Premier run through
such a programme : but, then, John Forrest does
not fear any trouble at the polls. More than half
the population is disfranchised.

Observantly compare the Hansard reports
of the "91 and ’97 Conventions, and you will
realise at a glance that, whatever Federation was
then, it is a live, people’s question now. Whereas
often whole pages of the ’9l report read without
an interrogation, the ’ST report bristles with them.
Most of the Westralian delegates to the
Convention did their best to live up to the desig-
nation given them by Walker —“Government
nominees."’ Leake (Lee Steere) and Taylor each
made one short speech in committee, and these
were the only ones to come out from under John
Forrest's wing.
Dugald Thompson and Adrian Knox are
the only members of N.S.W. Assembly who don’t
pocket their Parliamentary salaries. Knox’s goes
back to the consolidated revenue account: Thomp-
son’s goes direct to a committee in his electorate,
which divides it among charities. In a word, Mr.
Thompson buys with it .£303 worth of popularity.

The Maoriland National Council of Women
is now called the “ National Cow ” for short. If
the National Ass were distinctively male, a lot of
experience might be gained by marrying it to the
Cow and waiting results. But there is too much
old woman about the Ass.
By one of its many compromises the
Federal Convention has decided that the states
may have a separate Governor a piece if they
want, or they may abolish the office and the gaudy
frills attached to it, and save the salary, and sell
the local Government Houses to a boom-syndicate
if they prefer. Also it seems, in the absence of
all direction to the contrary, to be left to the dis-
cretion of each state, supposing it wants an Ex-
cellency of its own, to have him elected or to
import him. Apparently, with a Governor-
General at headquarters, there will be nothing for
the purely local Excellency to do save patronise
theatres, and open flower-shows and bridges, and
be a perennial cause of “ Gorsave.”
Premier Forrest, in his recent policy
speech : “ Can anyone truly say that our policy
has not attracted population to this great colony?”
But what sort? A few of the names (writes a
Bulletin' correspondent) up over business-places
in one or two of the main streets of Perth not five
chains from the railway-station : G. Lung and
Co., Chen Hop, On Lee, Hung Lee, Van Damme,
Sun Lee, Sbara Hayta and Co., Tong War, Ah
Long, Visobord. Schrutb, Steinberg, Mendoza,
War Lee, Ah Ling, Mahomed Bux, Seeigson,
Rubinstein (in two places), Ah Sam, Wing Lee,
Kaganski. Way Key, Sam Sing, Way” Lee,
Saleeba, Mayrhofer and Co., Krug. Korborswkv,
Wing Sang Woo, Kensler. Also Forrest, when
ever he journeys to his native Bunburv, is faith-
fully waited upon by two pug-faced Japs.
The Convention has resolved that the
Federal Government shall be put on a strict
allowance—as if it were an infant in the eye of the
law—as regards its expenditure. There is to be so
much and no more for defence, for official salaries,
judiciary, and so on, and nearly everybody rose to
say that this sort of thing was absolutely necessary
to keep the misguided Federal infant from plung-
ing into extravagance and blewing its substance
in drink and riot. The Bulletin now wants to
hear something about putting the Provincial
Governments on a similar cast-iron allowance. If
the system isn’t to apply all round there would be
a lot more sense in putting a fixed limit to pro-
vincial expenditure and letting the Federal Govern-
ment have a free band. It is much more essential
to keep an eye on six spendthrifts than on onlv
one.

MELBOURNE. ADELAIDE. CHRISTCHURCH.
At Poverty Point .
Referring—in an article published in Melb. Argus—to
one of North Pole Nansen’s London lectures, Oarlyle
Smythe (son of the Most-Travelled) remarks in an airy,
off-hand way :—“ The Duke of Newcastle declined to sit
in the place allotted him, and Scot-Keltie, the secretary
of the society, hearing I wished to be present, sent the
Duke’s ticket to me.” The strange part of the matter is
that a son of R. S. Smythe should so far demean himself
as to take the leavings of a common duke.
The septuagenarian C. E. Jones, of “ Paschal Lamb and
Mint Sauce ” celebrity, erstwhile Vic; Minister of Rail-
ways, goes to Westralia in his old age in quest of a living
as a lecturer. He is still hale and vigorous, as good a
raconteur as ever, and prides himself on having been a
life-long teetotaller and non-smoker. He will probably
receive a cordial greeting from many of the descendants
in W.A. of the “gentlemen of Ballarat and savages of
Bungaree.”
Business is surprisingly iarge at all Melb. shows this
Easter. Money turned away from “ Romeo and Juliet,”
even the fourth night. Unaccountable the ebb and flow.
Melb. Shakespeare spells bankruptcy.” Bosh. Here
is “ Romeo and Juliet ” jammed out for four nights, beat-
ing “Trilby” and “Chinatown,” and each night, per-
haps, beating any two houses at the “ Prisoner of Zenda,”
or any three at the “Two Little Vagabonds.” Williamson
ought to stage “ Titus Andronicus ” right off.
Writes someone : “ Cleo erred in last issue (April 24) in
writing down little Alma Grey as Ada Baker’s daughter
—which she is not. The responsible parties are hubby
Alf. Boothman and wife (Jessie Grey), the latter the
original drummer-boy in Melb.’s first real comic opera,
‘La Fille du Tambour Major.’”
W. Walshe from London to Tub Bulletin “ Lilian
Tree is singing soprano ballads at the Palace of Varieties.
Jennie Lee is at the Royal Music Hall giving sketches
from ‘ Jo.’ H. Stockwell and Frank Morton are in the
provinces with ‘ Shamus O’Brien.’ ‘La Poup6e ’is an

three months.”
“Mummer” to The Bulletin apropos something or
other : —Mrs. , the alleged beauty, will never
be an actress. Her mouth is too small. No woman with
asmall mouth was ever any good as an actress, or very
much good as anything else. She is a sweet woman when
one is close to her—the closer the better, I may say ; but
across the footlights she is too “ fine,” and her face is out
of drawing —forehead too wide, chin too small. She is a
boudoir beauty without the voice, figure, physique, or
talent of a mummer, and she is wasting her time. The
chorus contains a dozen handsomer and cleverer women,
who can’t rise above the crowd, and an occasional —“The
carriage waits.”
N. : “ Apropos Duke of Fife entertaining London actors.
Recall his pedigree. Great-grandson of Dora Jordan,
actress, mistress of William IV., and mother of his ten
children. The King wanted to dock her allowance, but
she tore off the bottom of the Drury Lane play-bill and
sent it to him—‘No money returned after the rising of
the curtain.’ He sacked her when old, and married a royal
woman, Adelaide.”
“ Joe ” Blascheck, a young man with a game leg and a
strong faith in his powers as a humorous reciter, went
from Melbourne to London the other day, for the purpose
of bursting forth as a representative Australian humorist,
The Bulletin fears. London is a large place and Bias-
check may pick up a living there, but he will have to con-
fine his attack to that section of the public which would
rather die than set foot in a theatre. His humor is very
soft and mild, especially wjien it assumes a Cockney
character.
Does Manager Bland Holfr nave his men built to sample,
or does he select men of one size and weight so that each
man can wear the others’ clothes in case of emergency ?
Anyhow, there never was such uniformity in the matter
of size and weight in one co. before. Baker, Cosgrove,
Norman, Corlesse, Montgomery, and Kemp are all solid
men of about the same ounces and inches, and, barring
Corlesse, either of them could be the other fellow at a
pinch. Possibly Bland’s prosperity accounts for the
unusual fatness of the company he keeps.
“La now London's rage, was, like many
other pieces, condemned by the actors beforehand. All
were most despondent up to the rise of the curtain, when
it went from the jump. Miss Soldene relates the same of
“ Genevieve de Brabant,” from the first night of which
Farnie, the author, ran away in terror. Same actors’
mistake over Goldsmith’s “ She Stoops to Conquer.”
Smugglers in England, less than 100 years ago, were
hung on gibbets and their bodies were coated with tar.
These corpses stood the wear and tear of climate, &c., for
many years, and one of them is still doing duty as a
travelling showman’s chief draw. He exhibits it “to
men only.” How would , tarred, suit, say Jimmy
MacMahon, just now ?
E. : “ The archaeological ignorance of stage-managers,
even in first-class productions, is occasionally appalling.
Take, for instance, the prologue to ‘ The Prisoner of
Zenda ’as played in Me'bourne. Rassendvl’s wig was
a Ramillies one (temp. Queen Anne), while all the rest of
the characters wore the conventional tie-wig (temp.
George III.) which did not appear till fully 50 years later.
Among the stage-props we find a large statuette of the
Venus of Milo, which, according to all accounts, was not
fished up out of the vasty deep till early in the present
century ; yet the date marked on the programme is circa
1731. Which recalls that Kyrle Bellew nearly had a fit
recently during his Maoriland tour on discovering, just as
he was making his first entrance in ‘ She Stoops to Con-
quer,’ that the walls of the Hardcastle mansion were
liberally adorned with photographs —‘ Put there,’ as the
irrepressible property-man remarked, ‘ just to give the
blanky ’ouse a ’omely appearance.”
Bobbie Peel, grandson of the Sir Robert, has published
an “ autobiographical novel ” entitled “ A Bit of a Fool,”
in which he duly dilates upon his youthful infatuation for
Mrs. Langtry and other ladies.
Harry Paulton has had a half-share in the literary pro-
duction of several successful extravagancies, besides
being a pantomime and burlesque writer “ on his own.”
“ The Black Crook,” which scored at the Alhambra five-
and-tw'enty-years ago, was partly Paulton’s work, like-
wise the “ Erminie ” libretto, and, best of all, “ Niobe.”
Minnie Palmer gravely informs interviewers that she
was born in 1865, and made her first appearance, in a
play specially written for her, at the early age of 11. At
this rate the flossy little creature was 22, or less, when
she visited Australia. Thb Bulletin took her to be some-
what older than that. (Somewhat is a good word.)
From Maoriland : “Asa married man of much theatrical
experience I intend never to allow my wife (she has
theatrical aspirations) on the stage, even as an amateur.
I have never known —perhaps that’s my exceptionally bad
luck, but it seems to chime in with the experience of
others—more than two amateur ladies to remain long oh
the stage without coining to ‘ the usual theatrical
arrangement.’ The last lady amateur (it was about nine
years ago) who joined a well-established co. of which I
Was a member, fell in at once. Within a month she and
the leading man eternally sat in the green-room gaziiig
into each other’s eyes so intently that the rest of the co.
didn’t like to enter without knocking. After the show it
was : ‘ Are you ready, dear ? ’ ‘Yes, darling. Will you call
the cab, dear?’ ‘lt’s here, darling ; come along, theri,
let’s get home at once.’ Yet she was a much-married
woman more or less in society and with several children.”
A concert at Haw-hawthorn (Melb.) Town Hall, for the
benefit of the Indian Famine Fund, was advertised in the
Melb. dailies, the other week. It was a three or four-inch
advt. stating in lai-ge type that the Hawthorn Mayor had
promoted the concert, and that Mr. and Mrs. Sargoodand
other great people were likely to attend. Way down at
the bottom of the announcement the names of the ".rtists
who would supply the music were huddled togeth :r into
a quarter-inch of space. They were not the attraction.
The strength of the programme lay in the fact that a tin-
pot Mayor had “ promoted ” it under Sargood’s patronage.
An English exchange mentions “ a remarkable feat ”
accomplished by Madame Angelo about the year 1873, when
she walked 1000 miles in 1000 hours for a "wager of 12000
at Vauxhall Gardens, Birmingham. Old Melbourne men
will remember that about the year 1860 Mrs. Douglass,
wife of the prompter at the Princess’ Theatre, performed
the feat in order to raise money for her sick husband.
She was small, wiry, and elderly, and walked on a track
built in the old Olympic Theatre" (the Iron Pot), Lonsdale-
street. She successfully accomplished the task dressed in
bloomer costume.
At a send-off smoke-concert to Majah Reay, of Melb.
’Erald, a very good recitation of “Banjo” Paterson’s
“Man from Snowy River” was given by an A.N.A.
aspirant named Watt, who, however, thought it necessary
to introduce the well-known subject with a few remarks.
Previously Colonel Price had alluded to The Bulletin’s
unkind habit of calling him “ Lay ’em out,” from which
this journal is glad to assume that the Colonel repents his
bye-one ferocity and would like to be forgiven.
Haresfoot: “Met Jenny Nye in Melb. just now, the yet
fresh, blooming, youthful, taut, sprightly little colum-
bine, harlequina, and harlequin in Melb. and Sydney
pantos, over 30 years ago. The beauty of Jane is that she
admits ‘ Matsa ’ beats her time. The sickening sixty-
sevener is so apt to enthuse on frowsy Coppinian Artful
Dodgery.”
Melb. :Twenty women had their hats off in the Prin-
cess amphitheatre last night.”
Australia now boasts the best living soprano (Melba)
and contralto (Crossley).
Williamson is the only Melbourne theatrical manager
who does not ornament his programmes with his own
portrait. The other three give away sweet, boyish pictures
of themselves with the usual bill of fare.
The judicious grieved when they did not blaspheme
over a fragment of a “turn” supplied by Conway and
Clarke at Rickards’ Melb. show during last week. Clarke,
the male element of the team, warbles a jingo stanza in
assertion of the idea that Amurka “kin lick creation,”
and don’t you forget it. Whereupon, Conway, who is
the skirted “ pardner,” responds for the “ Beritish Hem-
pire,” with a whoop to the effect that J. Bull can climb
all over Jonathan without damping his hands. The duet
ends in the complete triumph of Bull. The agony lies in
the fact that, although Clarke is Yankee, Conway is fifty
times more so, and the glory and might of Hingland are as-
serted in a shrill Old-Kentucky dialect every time. Jingo
ditties of the British, for the British, by the British are
bad enough ; of the British, for the British, by the Yank,
or another “ furriner,” they provoke violent acidity of the
stomach and homicidal tendencies.
S.S. : “Has it ever struck you as remarkable that the
mummer acting a Shakespearean hero always wants an
unfortunate man about to hang on to and paw over?
Bellew’s Romeo is only exceptional in this respect inas-
much as he mauls Mercutio, and Benvolio, and Friar
Lawrence, and others more persistently and aggravat-
ing! y than former R. Montagues have done. He hitches
himself on to these unfortunate people at all sorts of dis-
comforting and uneasy angles, and moans at large ; or,
hooked to them, hangs limply, and discourses of his last
mash, or, leaning up against them, and pawing them with
the familiarity of a best girl, pours his confidences down
their briskets or into their back hair. Perhaps Shake-
speare intended this—he seems to have created a charac-
ter or two in most of his plays merely to serve as itching-
posts for his heroes —but I would rejoice to see a Romeo
who would stand on his own hind-legs, and not expect to
be shored up by the rest of the co. A tendency to flop is
a nauseous thing in a man, and not to be endured in a
hero who skewers his enemies as neatly as Romeo does.”
Haresfoot: “ Bland Holt’s fire-scene is so actual that it
depicts the burning of the play-house. People, especially
women, will be frightened off, and I don t expect this
piece to draw. Also the play is very repulsive with the
Panther and her crowd of fashionable prostitutes.”
Bellew is faking up a version of “ Joan of Arc” for Cora
P., with which they intend to astonish London on their
return in June, if they can get a suitable theatre.
Editor Bulletin,—With reference to a paragraph in
your issue of April 24,1 desire to point out that Alma Grey
is not my daughter. My daughter, Miss Trixie Baker, has
sung “I’ve Asked Johnny Jones,” but only with the ob-
jectionable verses omitted. The mistake has caused me
much annoyance, and I must ask you to correct it. —Ada
Baker.
One Reid, a social purist, has been landed for .475
damages and costs, for stating that a certain damsel
appeared upon a London music-hall stage in bare legs,
whereas she wore fleshings at the time. Probably the
loss of his money affected the good man less than the dis-
covery that he had been grossly deceived.

Hunk of academic law from N.S.W. Judge
Coffey : “ People who agree among themselves
not to bid for goods and chattels put up for sale
under a distraint for rent may be prosecuted for
conspiracy if the landlord doesn’t obtain the full
amount of his claim.” It should be very hard to
convict a person of conspiracy to do nothing.
Bacteriologist Pound notices a singular
tendency in rabbits to travel northward. Where-
ever an east and west fence existed there was a
constant effort on the part of Bunny to get round
it, even when not chased. Pound also notes adapta-
bility of color. On red sand-hills the rabbit was
also red, and the scientist is of opinion this will
yet be the prevailing color among the little pests
in Western Queensland.
A delicious cooling drink. The juice of
the lime fruit, as prepared in Rose’s Lime Juice
Cordial, supplies a beverage particularly whole-
some and refreshing in water—effervescing in all
mineral waters and an excellent stimulant
blended with spirits. It possesses valuable medi-
cinal properties, cooling and purifying the blood,
stimulating and assisting digestion. It is entirely
free of spirits. Recommended by the Lancet.
Sold everywhere. Purchasers should be careful
to order only Rose’s Cordial, all others being
imitations.*
From Glen and Co. (Melb.)come “Good-
bye for Ever,” a neat ballad to the music of
Davan Wetton, that should find its way around ;
also, two acceptable dance pieces. The stronger
of these is the “ Fairy Bower ” waltz, by Herbert
Chandler, a harmonious composition with con-
siderable work and go in it. The “Zenda”
waltz, by clever Miss Einilie Kaeppel (of St.
Kilda, Melb.), is a light, catchy little thing,
which was very effectively treated by the Prin-
cess’s orchestra during the run of Anthony Hope’s
red-headed romance.
Public-meeting Geraldton (Q.) orator :
The Kanaka is secured against all ordinary risks and
losses by the almost too paternal guardianship of the
State, and can never be “ hard up ; ” as a last resource he
can always fall back on his island, live without work, and
die happy.
Apropos of which : Polynesian Inspector Caulfield
gave public evidence at Bundaberg, the other day,
that the Kanakas often complained to him about
getting short weight in their none too luxurious
dietary, and that on several occasions he had
found the complaint amply justified.
London Referee (Heaven forgive the sin
of it !) offered prize cups, value 7 guineas, for the
best slabs of rhyme dealing with the historical
events of V.R.’s gracious reign. A chip off the
block which captured the colonial cup :
Victoria’s reign of record length’s my theme—
Her reign for sixty years, her reign supreme !
To Kensington at dawn the Primate hies :
“The King is dead. Long live the Queen !” he cries.
Three years pass by, the Queen then weds in joy ;
Next year—that’s ’4l—is born her boy.
The Bulletin may as well amend the poem in
accordance with facts, considering that correct
dates were demanded of the competitors
Three years roll on, Her Nibs then whispers “ Yes ” ;
And in due course produces a princess.
At duty’s call her courage never fails —
In ’42 she gives the nation “ Wales.”
A Girl’s Life Saved at Neutral Lay.
In the picturesque suburb of Neutral Bay, Syd-
ney, resides Miss Mashford, a young lady of pre-
possessing appearance, who seems likely to furnish
her friends and neighbours with a topic of conver-
sation which will in all probability be discussed
for some time to come. In an interview with a
representative, Miss Mashford made the following
interesting and surprising statement: “lam now
nearly 21 years of age, and I have never been
strong in my life. About the beginning of March,
1896, I began to feel worse than I ever had felt
before. I had a terribly languid feeling—the
least exertion made me feel tired, in addition to
which I suffered excruciating pains from neuralgia
which quite unfitted me for any active pursuit.
My appetite was also very poor, and I had almost
a dread of food. I spent a great deal of money in
buying tonics, trusting that some of them would
relieve me; but none of them did me any good.
But one day, quite, by accident, I heard of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People through one
of my friends, and a few days after a pamphlet
was left at our house (Woodville), and on reading
it I thought one case in which a permanent cure
had been effected was very similar to my own,
namely, bloodlessness, or what is called anaemia.
I, therefore, determined to get some, and bought
my first box from Mr. Medd, of the Military-road,
Neutral Bay, the local chemist. I commenced
taking them according to the printed directions
on the wrapper.”
“ Did this box do you any good, Miss Mash-
ford ? ”
“ Indeed, it did : and I was badly in need of
something to benefit me, for I must tell you that
at this time I was sent up to Hornsby for a change
of air, the state of my health being such as to
cause my relatives and friends considerable alarm.
However, this one box did me so much good that
I determined to continue with them, and I sent
for five more boxes, which I took.”
“ And you now feel ever so much better ? ”
“ Most certainly I do ; apart from how much
better I feel, my aunt and others tell me my com-
plexion has improved amazingly, and my mirror,
which I occasionally look at,” she naively re-
marked, “tells me the same story. lam now
able to go about in my old style, and my health
improves daily.”
“And you attribute all these improvements in
your health and appearance to taking Dr Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills ? ”
“ Well, as I have taken no other medicine, it
must be the Pills that have wrought this benefit.
I shall always fully believe in them, and shall
recommend them to all my friends.”
(Signed) E. D. MASHFORD.
(In subsequent conversation with Mr. Medd,
the chemist of Neutral Bay, our reporter elicted
that Mr. Medd knew Miss Mashford well, and
was rejoiced to observe the striking improvement
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills had effected in her. Mr.
Medd added that he had a good sale for the Pills
about Neutral Bay, and that doubtless as soon as
the recovery of Miss Mashford became generally
known the sale would materially improve.)
The facts above related are important to girls
just budding into womanhood, whose condition is,
to say the least, more critical than their parents
imagine. Their complexion is pale and waxy in
appearance, troubled with heart palpitation, head-
aches, shortness of breath on the slightest exercise,
faintness, and other distressing symptoms which
invariably lead to a premature grave unless prompt
steps are taken to bring about a natural condition
of health. In this emergency no remedy yet dis-
covered can supply the place of Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills, which build anew the blood, strengthen the
nerves, and restore the glowof health to pale and sal-
low cheeks. They are a certain cure for all troubles
peculiar to the female system, young and old.
i Pink Pills also cure such diseases as rheumatism,
neuralgia, partial paralysis, locomotor ataxia, St.
Vitus’ dance, nervous headache, nervous prostra-
tion, the after effects of la grippe, influenza, and
severe colds, diseases depending on humors in the
blood, such as scrofula, chronic erysipelas, etc.
In the case of men they effect a radical cure in all
cases arising from mental worry, overwork, or
excesses of any nature.
These pills are not a purgative. They are
genuine only with the full name, Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People, and are sold by
chemists, and by Dr. Williams’ Medicine Com-
pany, Sydney, who will forward, post paid, on
receipt of stamps or post order, one box for 2s 9d,
or half-dozen for 14s 9d. They are unrivalled as
a tonic for both sexes.*

Postmaster-General Cook states scornfully
that if a halfpenny newspaper postage was itri-
posed iri N.S.W. it Would probably so far reduce
the number of papers posted that it would only
realise .£25,000 a year. The obvious implication
is that a source of revenue which only yields
.£25,000 a year isn’t worth consideration. Yet,
N.S.W. scrambles mighty hard after a good many
sources of revenue which don’t yield anythinglike
that humble sum, and £25,000 a year would pay
for the shifting of a vast amount of sand up at
Centennial Park.
