Mr. J. M. Bruce, the Melbourne softgoods
person, left for London the other day after a
send-off at Menzies’—the Mayor in the chair.
Said “ Potty ” Bruce, in responding to the toast
of his health : “If, during my stay in London,
opportunities should arise for doing anything on
behalf of the colony in relation to the Diamond
Jubilee celebrations, I will do my best to uphold
the honor of the colony.” One hardly sees what
Bruce can do in this way—unless he parades
Regent-street humping a gas-light illumination
with “ Advance Australia ” on one side and “Pat-
terson, Laing, and Bruce ” on the other.
The Sydney parsons may now unwittingly
be raising a record dust : city and suburbs are
overrun by an army of old women of both sexes,
with long lank hair, in round hats and black
bonnets, canvassing for signatures to a petition
praying the Executive to enforce the Sunday-clos-
ing law and save the unfortunate who runs the
corner shop from being worked to death ; and so
allow him to attend matins, Sunday-school, and
evensong with his family. But, sad to say, the
benighted wretch does not want to be Saved, and
swears that if the parsons shut him up he will set
the law in motion against the restaurants, ferry-
steamers, Sunday excursion-boats, Railway Com-
missioners, milk-carts, Sunday-paper men, and all
others who scoop in lucre on the Sabbath.
A Perth boarding-house-keeper recently
applied for permission to lay down, at his own
expense, a pipe to carry waste-water out into the
gutter—and was refused. The orthodox way, he
was informed, was to dig a large hole in his back
premises and let the waste fluid putrefy and fester
there until the hole was full, and then a Corpora-
tion cart would come round and bale out the horrid
green mass, and take it away. It was explained
that Perth had always followed this system, and
the putrid hole in the backyard was a deep-rooted
institution, like the Throne or the Church, and
not to be lightly done away with ; also, that the
Corporation regarded pipes with suspicion as a
new-fangled device, which might lead to unfore-
seen consequences. Anyhow, Perth was resolved
to die at its post, if necessary, with its stink in its
right hand, and defend its smell and constitution
to the last.
Yarn from -near Broad Arrow (W.A.) :
The miners at a certain local show are working
for shares, the syndicate being unable to pay
wages. A rich patch was struck one day, and the
miners assembled in one of the tents to celebrate
the find by a repast, in which many bottles figured.
During the orgie, the grocer arrived for orders,
and was asked in. While he was inside, his horse
got into Jim’s tent and devoured three loaves of
bread. Jim at last heard the noise, and arrived
on the scene as the last loaf was disappearing.
For revenge, he made the horse finish his feed with
two dynamite cartridges, and then told the grocer
his horse was not safe to have near the camp, as
he was loaded up to the back-teeth with dynamite.
The grocer, in mortal terror, said he wouldn’t get
on the horse for a pension ; he led him to a tree,
about 30yds. off, tied him up, and then withdrew
to a safe distance and pelted the poor beast with
stones, to “ sorter test whether he was likely to go
off.” Finding he didn’t explode, he mounted very
gingerly, and rode away at about half-a-mile an
hour, being fraid to touch him with a stick. The
miners are waiting to hear if he got home all
right.
Right to Complain.
Most of as have this right, or if not we assume it;
at the same time few enjoy hearing others com-
plain. One complains of the weather; another
the dulness of business; another of poverty;
another of afflictions, too personal to be of general
interest; but Mrs. I. Clarke, storekeeper, of
M'Culloch St., Donald, complained of pains in
the back due to kidney disease, an ailment so
common that her experience will interest many.
She says : “ This is to certify that when suffering
back ache and kidney disease Warner’s
‘ Safe ’ Cure entirely cured me.” Some are always
complaining, unconsciously acquiring the habit,
but they deserve no sympathy unless they are
guided by evidence such as above, and if they are
so guided they will require no sympathy.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Despite rain on the third day, the A.J.C.
Autumn meeting was a great success. The two chief
events were won by rank outsiders whose victories, with
the exception of the stakes, scarcely benefited their
connection at all. ’Tis passing strange that the staying
capabilities of the 5-year-old Tricolor were Only made
known to his owner by the Cup performance. In the
City Handicap, no less than eight of the eleven runners
were well backed, and yet the outsider, Gozorei i,
splashed home unbacked by owner and public, and Iky
had another skinner.
“ Now and then ” Newhaven performed ih
strangely contradictory manner during the A. J.G. meet-
ing. On the first day he easily won a very slowly-ruri
Leger from The Officer, Charge and Resolute; in the
All-Aged Stakes on third day he was badly beaten by
Hopscotch, True Blue and Metford, and to wind-up with,
he simply made hacks of Coil, Fucile, True Blue, Battalion
and Resolute in A.J.C. Plate of three miles, all of which
goes to prove that he is a casual kind of cuss that can
gallop when he has things all his own way. ’Tis to be
hoped he will in England run more consistently than here,
or Mr. Cooper will have unpleasant interviews with the
stewards of the jockey club.
Had Aurum been pulled out against Hopscotch,
Newhaven & Co. for the All-aged Stakes, he would prob-
ably have won; but his party did not wish to subject
him to a severe race over a mile, and, consequently, took
on the Easter Stakes, in which event the “ marvellous
nugget” easily carried his 141 b. penalty to victory.
Aurum now retires to winter quarters the most valuable
asset in horseflesh in Australia, not even excepting the
“ now and then ” champion Newhaven.
» * * *
The S.A.J.C. Autumn Meeting begins at
Adelaide to-day (Sat.). The Goodwood Handicap looks
like falling to Staffa or Thunderer, though Areola will run
well if condition goes for anything. The S.A. Stakes is a
good thing for Fleet Admiral, who may be followed home
by Security. The St. Leger Stakes may be fought out by
Thunder Queen and Eleusinian. On Wednesday the
valuable Adelaide Cup should be retained in S.A. per
aid of Thunder Queen, whose most dangerous opponent
may turn up in the once all-conquering Loyalty—reported
as having regained some of his brilliant form.
* * • *
Carbine asserted himself as a sire at A.J.C.
meeting with three winners, whilst Trenton, Grand
Flaneur, Newminster and Gozo come next on the list
with two apiece. The Vic. jock Morrison rode three
winners, and Farrell, Cleal, and Gardiner each got home
first twice. £12,635 was distributed in stakes, of which
Mr. Royds by aid of Tricolor got the biggest slice (£2245).
The strong Kelso stable did not turn out a single winner,
for although the unlucky Sabretache got first past in
Rous Handicap, he was afterwards disqualified for boring.
* * * *
At the Sydney yearling sales last week, the
handsome Cocos, a full brother to Coil (Abercorn— Copra)
was bought by Mr. W. Bailey for 1150 guineas. A half
brother to Aberoorn, by the imported Gossoon out of
Cinnamon, went to Mr. W. Leonard for 700 guineas, but
perhaps the pick of the lot was Starbine, a full brother to
the Second Nursery winner, Giril (Carbine—Twinkle),
bought by Fred. Fielder for 650 guineas. This colt is very
like his sire.
* * * «
The jumping events at Randwick meeting were
much more interesting than usual; Irish Stew, Jack
Hanwell and His Grace, of the hurdlers, performing ex-
tremely well and giving great promise. Jack Hanwell is
half-brother to the late champion Bellringer, who won 13
races during a lengthy career. Booties, theM.L. ’chaser,
was a bit lucky in winning the Second Steeplechase, as
had not Whitefoot 11. struck the sod wall he would
have won, for he was fencing beautifully and going
steadily when he came down. Mignon is a fine fencer
and fast.
* * » *
The English-bred Positano, within three months
of his crossing the sea, won at Randwick on Saturday.
Australians do not make quite such a fuss over the win as
if an Australian-bred had won, under similar circum-
stances, in England ; but it must be confessed there is not
much likelihood of an English handicapper putting an
Australian horse at bottom weight, as was Positano.
« * * »
A.J.C. Secretary Clibborn must have arrived in
Australia further back than the “ oldest inhabitant ” can
carry his memory if, as stated by him at the trainers’
presentation, he recollects the time when horse-owners
ran their steeds for the “love of sport” alone. Money
ever did, and ever will, make the mare to go, and it must
be confessed that the ready seems quite as potent at
Randwick as On the smallest proprietary course. There
is scarcely a meeting at Randwick at which “ reversal of
form ” is not shown that would entitle the pony-owner to
a “lifer,” but, unless the perpetrator be a friendless coun-
try owner, the powers that be make no sign. O, for the
Arcadian circle, with its shingle stand, remembered by
the secretary in his salad days !
* * * *
When Newhaven goes to England punters will
have more time for considering the vagaries of Coil.
* * * *
Hopscotch, for all the talk about his in-and-out
running, has been a consistent performer, as horses go in
Australia. Up to March last (and everybody can remem-
ber what he has done within the present month), the big
chestnut had started 30 times and put 11 wins to his
credit. He was placed in four other races, and unplaced
in the remaining 15. But the 15 bad failures included
several events in which Hopscotch was running beyond
his distance. In races of not more than nine furlongs he
has more wins than losses to his account.
* * » »
The legalising of cash wagers on English race-
courses is made inevitable by the recent successful action
of the Anti-Gambling League in prosecuting bookmaker
Dicky Dunn. A strict enforcement of the law as it stands
would soon clear the general public off the turf, and the
g.p. is necessary to horse-racing. So, now that the hum-
bug of the old system (under which the law winks at
Ikey Mo’s offence) has been properly exposed, a new Act
will have to happen. In N.S.W. and Vic., where we have
the farce of permitting racing-clubs to license law-
breakers, a new Betting Act will probably be avoided by
a Tote Bill.

» 4 * *
A totalisator argument: <£21,835 was the
amount invested at Onkaparinga (S. A.) meeting on Easter
Monday—a record for S.A. Of this amount the club re-
tained £1637 as commission at 7£ per cent., thus enabling
it to offer £lOOO as the sweepstakes for the leading race,
the Steeple. From the point of view of the public, the
owners, or the clubs, which is the better system—the
totalisator and scientific odds, or Israel Moses with his
diamond scarfpin and even money the double ?
* * * *
S.A. bookmakers have now adopted an ingenious
system for overcoming the betting law. Moses now
employs a runner to reinvest in the machine a portion of
the moneys received from the public, so that bookie
always has a stock of tickets on hand in case of a police
raid—conclusive proof that he is a totalisator commission-
agent and not a bookmaker. The plan works all in favor
of Moses. He never pays out at higher odds than 10 to 1,
and frequently scoops a big machine div. The police
admit that Moses is now beyond the reach of everything
save fresh legislation.

* * * *
The final of the Druids’ Wheel Race at Melb.
Exhibition gave a fine display of track tactics. All the
cracks had been weeded out by the preliminary heats,
and, though no scratch men were left in, the calibre of the
field may be gauged by the fact that young MTnnes, of
Melb., was limit man on 140yds. MTnnes made the race
slow from the pistol, and it was amusing to hear the
crowd, who never seem to learn, saying that the boy was
throwing away his chances by not making the pace.
MTnnes’s idea was to stop in front throughout, make the
pace slow, and then win with a lightning dash in last lap.
But the astute Body saw the ruse, and pushed the pace
to run MTnnes off his legs, entering the last lap himself
in front. Middleton came round him at the last turn, and
the pair left the rest of the field 20yds. behind as they
battled up the straight to pass the line level.
The run-off was most exciting. Middleton,
whose sprint is superior to Body’s, started 30yds. ahead,
but, instead of waiting for the Maorilander and relying
on the finish, he put down his head and fatuously tried to
lose a man who was twice as good a stayer. So Body
caught him after three laps, and shooting past the Dunol-
lyite, who was dead to the world, got 10yds. lead before
the other knew it. The race seemed all over, but nobody
has yet plumbed the depths of Middleton’s pluck, and he
lay down once more and set out on the heartrending job
of catching his opponent. At one time there were 30yds.
between them, but Middleton’s head continued to jerk
and the machine crept up inch by inch until, three laps
from home, they were together again. Both then sat up
and crawled at snail-pace until" the last lap, when the
refreshed Middleton went out and won by five lengths.
The first-class mile was a gift to O. S. Prowse
with 50yds. start, the brilliant Sydney boy completely
overwhelming his field in the final with a lap-long sprint
and a win by a length of his handicap. There is more
coming from O. S. Prowse, too.
What a marvellous leap in form is displayed by
the pacing wonder, Body ! Two years in Australia and
not a win to his credit, though he is reckoned only a
second-rater and handicapped accordingly. Yet, no
sooner does he pull off a big prize than he comes out and
wins scratch races, and with a start reduced to 20yds. runs
a dead-heat in another big race.
Walne, who has the unique record of having
won a race at every meeting he has started in during the
past season, kept up his average and endorsed previous
opinions that he is the best man in Australia to-day by
carrying off the ten-mile scratch. Walne wins all his
races in the same way. The public are intently watching
the field battling hard around the last turn, when sud-
denly a streak of yellow and black (old Bill Beach’s colors
by the way) flits around on the outside of the bank and
into the straight level with the lot. Then, if Joe Megson
and Bilmartin happen to be in good form, there’s a'race
home. If they aren’t—well, that prize is the Banana-
man’s.
In the five-mile scratch at St. Kilda, the mile
scratch at the Eight Hours’ meeting, and the mile scratch
at the Druids, embracing a period of 10 days, the finish
of each was Walne (1), Megson (2), Martin (3), a fairly
accurate indication as to the best cyclists in Australia at
the present time.

Despite the support of Brassey, Madden, and
H. G. Turner, the proposal to send’Ome an Aus. eight fizzled
miserably. Anybody who considered the thing seriously
for a moment could see that the scheme had death
written on its little brow from its birth, because the Hing.
lish definition of an amateur is such that scarcely eight
men in the whole of Australia could be found to qualify
“ Nobody who has at any time in his life taken part in
any athletic exercise for cash prizes or has competed
against any person who has done so, and nobody who has
engaged at any time in manual labor or in any menial
occupation, shall be eligible to row as an amateur oars-
man.” This latter clause bars out even the M.’sL.C,
* » » *
By the way, The Bulletin hears that there is a
prospect of the newly-formed N.S.W. manual-labor ama-
teur rowing association stepping into the breach, the
secretary having communicated with the Victorians offer-
ing an eight-oar race at an early date. Everything now
strongly points to an important revival of manual-laboi
amateurism in Australia.

Cyclist Green talks of stopping in Victoria to
get himself into form for next racing season. If it is
good enough for Green to sacrifice his chances of earning
prize-money during the English summer, for the sake of
what he hopes to do in Australia six months hence, what
was the good of Megson and Co. tripping ’Ome last year?
Melbourne is the headquarters of bike racing mania,
seemingly, and will remain so for at least another season.
The public never tire of beholding the champions take
one another down in turn.

X.L. : “The Gladiator Co. (France) has ordered
24 electric triplets for pacing purposes. Pacing is
rapidly killing bicycling as a sport, and reduces racing
to a mere question of a man hanging on in torture till he
busts. Also, it produces hypertrophy of the heart from
over-exertion.”
* * * *
“If a batsman makes more actually sprinted
runs during the passage of a ball to the boundary, does
he score the sprinted runs or only the value of a boundary
hit?” This point came before the Marylebone C.C., the
other day, over a disputed case where a would-be smart
Leeds fieldsman kicked an almost spent ball to the
boundary with a view to reducing the value of the hit.
The M.C.C. ruled that the batsman, in such a case, is en-
titled to all the runs actually run plus the value of the
boundary hit. Thus, if four runs were run, and a
boundary hit was worth three, the score off the stroke
would be seven.

* * * *
Farquhar, Walker, Grummett, and Reilly are
the only smokers in David Byrne’s rifle-team, latelv gone
’Ome. The others appear to believe in the anti-tobac-
conist axiom that smoking unsteadies the nerves. Yet
Farquhar is the best shot in the team and Grummett one
of the most reliable. Tobacco-smoking, in moderation,
never injured any healthy man’s nerves. It is only when
you take it in rum that tobacco is really unsettling.
Two Australian doctors —dovetailed—about
biking. “ The unrivalled exercise of cycling ” “ produces
moral and physical deterioration.” “It rejuvenates men
who are physiologically older than their years” by “in-
juriously affecting the whole nervous system.” “It often
cures insomnia when everything else has failed ” by pro-
ducing “ broken and unsatisfactory sleep, with unpleasant
dreams.” “ The cycle is a revolving panacea,” and “ the
coming race of cyclists will be saturnine and unintellec-
tual, with hollow chests, haggard faces, and an expression
of ‘ unhappy gravity.’ ” Possibly, however, the difference
between these authorities is merely the difference between
moderation and excess.
* * * »
Dear Bulletin,—Re the Druids’ Gala tickets,
and your statement that expenses swallow a lot more
than half the subscriptions, you are not quite fair in say-
ing “Expenses don’t help on any cause ; they only help
on the people who get the expenses.” The matter stands
thus : The Victorian Act compels art-union promoters to
give the profits of their lottery-ticket entertainment to
public charities. Well, the Druids “ sweat ” the profits—
as the other “ art-union ” promoters do—by allowing 33
per cent. com. upon sale of tickets to their various lodges.
Each lodge, as a branch of a bona-fide benefit society,
benefits by the tickets it disposes of, so “ Expenses” do
help on a cause in this case—the cause of the Druids as a
whole. “The contention that the gala exists in great
part to supply comfortable billets for various people looks
justifiable ” on paper only. The commission device is not,
I suppose, laid bare on the balance-sheet, although it is
already quite an open secret. —Melbourne.
The Eight Hours and Druids fete lotteries, with
their solid gold prizes, remind The Bulletin that the
first prize (Cleopatra’s needle), offered by the Jewish Car-
nival management, at Melb. Exhibition last Nov., was not
claimed. A drawing took place, and the pyramid of gold
fell to a ticket which had, presumably, been purchased
by some improvident Christian who lighted his pipe with
it. This was fortunate for the guarantors of the success-
ful show to whom the subsequent sale of the needle, for
,the benefit of “ expenses,” represented the difference
between a loss of several hundred pounds and a net profit
of 15s.

The Dunlop Pneumatic Tyre Co. secured last
year 56 decisions against infringers of their patents, while
11 injunctions were granted and 17 verdicts are pending.
No less than 70 other actions are yet to come to trial.*
* * • *
Who are the bike champions, anyhow ? Walne,
Megson, Martin, and Porta are each of them likely to
beat the other three in a scratch race over any distance,
whilst none of them seems thoroughly capable of giving
20yds. to Body, 15yds. to Relph, or 50yds. to A. A.
Middleton in a 2-mile handicap. At the present moment
Body is the best goer of the crowd when there is a big
stake sticking out at the business end of the journey.
• * • *
Of 18 men who got into the semi-finals for the
Druids’ Wheel Race, 10 had been placed for their heats in
the E.H.A. handicap, a couple of days earlier, and five
had struggled into the final.
* * * *
A Wairarapa (M.L.) cyclist, the other day,
found himself pursued by a big buck red-deer. Having
600yds. start, he set out to make the pace, but, finding
he was losing ground, shinned up a tree, and dragged his
bike after him just as the deer struck it. After half-an-
hour’s contemplation, the buck trotted off, satisfied that
his honor was duly vindicated.

First in 1888, foremost ever since is a good and
true motto. Dunlop tyres have been exceptionally vic-
torious this Easter, all the important races run in Mel-
bourne, and throughout New South Wales, being won
upon these tyres.

Dick Seddon has finally left for ’Ome,
departing from Auckland by the ’Frisco boat.
When the Tutaenakei, with Dick on board,
arrived off the Manukau Heads, the bar was so
bad that they signalled from shore : “ Don’t try
to cross it.” Dick, however, couldn’t afford to
miss the ’Frisco boat, so the Tutaenakei came
over, followed by the Takapuna with the mails on
board. Both vessels ran a very good chance of
having their backs broken on the bar. But, as a
political opponent said: “ One had the mails
aboard, and the other had the king.”

Melb. : “Sign of the times. At Pastor
Strong’s Australian Church, Easter Sunday, they
sang a National Anthem, tune “ God Save the
Queen,” without a word about the Queen in it,
the theme being God Save Our Native Land, suit-
able to either Republican or Loyalist.”
Gillen, sub-protector of aborigines in S. A.,
who lives at Alice Springs and is recognised as
about the best authority in Australia on the habits
and customs of the natives of Central Australia,
reports thus:—“These interesting people are, I
think, rapidly passing away, and I am of opinion
that the decadence set in many years before the
white man became resident in the interior.”
Naturalist Mask ell informed Wellington
(M.L.) Philosophical Society, the other night, that
ticks which in other countries afflict beasts only,
attack birds in Maoriland. In this connection it
may be put as a parallel that the flea infesting
Yankeelanders is not the human flea of Europe
(pulex irritans), but the dog-flea, which in Europe
is only found on humans in exceptionally un-
savory circumstances.

More crow 1 “ The crow,” writes “ Marie
Blaas ” to The Bulletin, “ needs no champions.
He’s only seen at his best when he’s dead or when
something else is dead. The crow is as black as
he is painted—for cowardice, cruelty, and ounning
he is unmatched. He is the pariah of the feathered
world and is hunted by the little soldier-bird over
plain and bush for miles, cawing with fear and
flying f° r his life all the time. His vigilant and
shifty eye is ever on the watch, and if it spots only
a young rabbit, it is a race to the death between
striking a hole or hollow log and being struck by
the crow. The sleepy, easy-going iguana is
a potential meal for this aerial pirate. A couple
of crows get a lot of amusement and, sometimes,
meat, in sticking up one of these retiring and
inoffensive animals, especially if he doesn’t retire
smartly. The iguana, on scenting danger, gener-
ally a crow or a cocky, at once rushes for his
favorite tree. The object of the crows is to block
him from reaching the cavity between the two
limbs of the tree. It then becomes a fight for life,
one of the crows cutting off his retreat and the
other trying to pick his eyes out or rip him open.
Generally the iguana becomes exhausted, drops to
the ground and affords a meal to his conquerors.
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had post free’ or 1/3. Senior,
chemist, George-street, Sydney.*
“ England seems to have had for mission
to show the rest of the world how the superior
races of Europe are destined to conquer and
civilise the inferior races, and to occupy vacant
territory in other parts of the globe ; but, to be
just and accurate, it must be said that she has
shown rather how to exploit inferior races than
how to civilise them.” The italics are The Bul-
letin’s, but the extract is from Paul Lacombe’s
official “ History for Schools,” for the use of
French school children.
~ Love’s old, tough, toothless dream ! In
Auckland (M.L.) recently, a man of 73 and a
woman of 70 were married. They had been
sweethearts when he was 23 and she 20, but she
married another, and he sat down to wait patiently
for her widowhood. The chance came at last, and
in the spring-tide he lightly turned, on his
crutches, to thoughts of love, and his long-expected
reward came at last. Probably it isn’t much of
a reward now that it has come, but such as it is he
has got it.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures • 9
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition •
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland •
13 a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room; 16, a Tea Room :
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
mam walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. *

Queen Victoria will hold the mediaeval
assemblage known as a Chapter of Knights on
26th or 28th June next. All knights will be in-
vited to attend the function, and, provided appear-
ance and behaviour are approved, all will after-
wards be commanded to dine with the sovereign.
The last Chapter was held in 1855. Then the
Duke of Buckingham did something which dis-
pleased H.M., and was omitted from the list of
diners.
louring the W.Q. towns just now may be
seen a remarkable vehicle. The cart in question
consists of a very large box, mounted on four
wooden wheels sawn off a round log. The eon
veyance is drawn by a bullock, through whose
nose a ring is placed, to which are attached two
wires by means of which the animal is guided
Owner carries all his necessaries in box, and also
camps therein at night-time. Evidently a 19th
century Diogenes with his tub on wheels.

AN INTERESTING NOTICE OF A MOST
SUCCESSFUL CAREER.
MARVELLOUS TESTIMONIALS.
FLATTERING PRESS OPINIONS.
- ■ ■ MIRACULOUS CURES.
A few words as to the progress and growth in
public favour of the Registered Alfaline Herbal
Remedies would not be out of place here, and
would be additional evidence as to their real
genuineness and great virtues as claimed for them
by Herr Rassmussen, their discoverer and sole
proprietor.
The great virtues of the Alfaline Herbal Reme-
dies are so well known that it is quite unnecessary
to say much in regard to them in this notice; the
very fact of their having been before the public
for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and may be seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should be no difficulty in predicting a great future
for these health-giving Remedies unprecedented
in the History of Herbal Remedies.
About twelve years ago Herr Rassmussen intro-
duced these Remedies to the Australian public by
opening a Depot for their sale at 547 George
Street, Sydney, N.S.W., his present Head Office,
and although at that time he did not advertise in
any journal, the wonderful virtues of these Reme-
dies were known far and wide within a few
months. Orders for them came from every
quarter, not only from all the Australian colonies,
but from all over the world. In 1891 the clamour
for the Alfaline Herbal Remedies had become so
great that it became impossible to supply everyone
from the Sydney Depot. Letters arrived by thou-
sands, many orders came by telegraph, ana some
people even travelled over a thousand miles to
make sure of obtaining some of these God-inspired
Remedies. What was to be done ? It was Herr
Rassmussen’s duty to see that every sufferer from
the Blood, Skin, Nerves, Liver, and other affec-
tions, whose very life and happiness depended on
these Remedies, should be able to obtain same
without undue delay. Herr Rassmussen had thus
no other alternative than to open Branch Depots
for the sale of the Alfaline Remedies in every
colony, and, if possible, in every country in the
world, as he desired to keep the sale of his Herbal
Remedies in his own hands entirely, and thus
avoid the agency of chemists, who generally have
a substitute which they prefer to sell instead of
the real article. A start was made at once in that
direction, and a very large and palatial Depot
was opened at 150 Bourke Street, Melbourne, in
1891. Immediately after, a fine Branch was
established at 154 Rundle Street, Adelaide, fol-
lowed by the opening of an extensive Branch at
91 Lambton Quay, Wellington, New Zealand. A
large Branch was then established at the corner of
Hay and William Streets, Perth, W.A., and
shortly after another Branch, next to Coolgardie
Hotel, Bailey Street, Coolgardie, and N 0.2 Lin-
dell’s Chambers, Hannan street, Kalgoorlie, W. A.,
not to forget the very extensive Branch opened
some seven years ago at 160 Queen Street, Bris-
bane, which is now the Head Depot for Queens-
land, and finally a Branch just opened at Charters
Towers, North Queensland.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “ Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
Herbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common ? ” The
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of Testimonials to that
effect.
The whole of these Branches are each doing a
large and flourishing business. There is not a
country on the face of the globe where these Re-
medies have not been heard of, and orders arrive
daily from the uttermost parts of the earth.

Mr. Button writes later:—“The Pills I had from you
were of great benefit to me and others. In one case,
when three hunters and trappers were travelling about
the ranges near my place on the Rose River, one was taken
very ill; his mates intended taking him in a waggon to
Wangaratta (about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor.
I gave him a few of your Pills. I am pleased to inform
you that, having to pass their camp next day, I found
him up and pegging-out his skins and looking well. This
speaks volumes for your valuable Remedies."

The Australian Press has fully recognised the noble
mission of Herr Rassmussen, and praise bestowed upon
him from this important source has not only been most
whole-hearted, but also very generous. Our space will
not permit to repeat one-tenth of his best Press notices,
but the following will suffice to show the public the esti-
mation in which Herr Rassmussen is held by the Austra-
lian Press:—
Sydney Bulletin. —“Herr Rassmussen’s well-known
Herbal Remedies have been the means of performing
many great cures, which his numerous testimonials will
prove.”
Evening News, Sydney.—" Herr Rassmussen has
shown us some highly complimentary testimonials, certi-
fying to important cures effected by his remedies.”
North Melbourne Advertiser. —“ Herr Rassmussen’s
wonderful Herbal Alfaline Remedies have done more good
than all other known remedies put together.”
N.Z. Times, Wellington.—“ Herr Rassmussen manu-
factures some excellent Blood and Nerve Remedies.”
Bally Telegraph, Launceston, Tas. —“Herr Rass-
mussen is a clever botanist.”
S.A. Register, Adelaide, S.A.—“Herr Rassmussen’s
Press notices agree as to the skill he possesses as a
botanist, and the bona fides of his testimonials appear to
be well established.”
Cooktown Courier, Queensland. —“ Herr Rassmus-
sen, we are glad to say, holds the highest place in the
colonies as a botanist, and enjoys the confidence of many
leading people of New South Wales, Victoria, and Queens-
land.”
Flashes (Society Paper), Brisbane, Q.— “ Herr Rass-
mussen’s labors have resulted in a manner most gratifying
to himself, inasmuch as he has received the highest re-
cognitions of the most important scientific authorities.”
Chemist and Bruggist Journal, Melbourne.—
“Herr Rassmussen has opened a branch depfit at 150
Bourke-street, Melbourne, and his Alfaline Pills will pro-
bably become as well known in Victoria as they are in
New South Wales.”
Evening Journal, Adelaide.—“ Herr Rassmussen
has achieved a high reputation for his skill in manufac-
turing botanical remedies.”
Sydney Truth. —“ Herr Rassmussen is sufficiently well
known not to r< quire any additional praise from Truth.
His remedies are simply wonderful.”
Sunday Times, Sydney.—“lt may not be out of
place to mention that Herr Rassmussen has now been be-
fore the public for over ten years.”
N.Z. Mail, Wellington.—“ We have among us at the
present time one of Australia’s foremost botanists, viz.,
Herr Rasmussen, the celebrated Danish botanist of
Sydney.”
The Australian Workman, Sydney.—“ Having
seen and examined letters from all parts of the world, we
are assured that Herr Rassmussen’s Alfaline Remedies
have saved a world of suffering, and his remedies have
cured many people of complaints which were held to be
incurable.”
The Nord Anstralisclic Zell ling, Brisbane.—“ We
speak from personal knowledge, and can vouch for the
great cures effected by Herr Rassmussen’s wonderful
Remedies. Having seen many cured sufferers, and ex-
amined letters from all parts of the world, we are satisfied
that he is deserving of universal thanks.”
Australian Star, Sj'dney.—“Herr Rassmussen is a
gentleman who has gained for himself a reputation in the
medical world of herbal science, and his name has become
known throughout New South Wales and Australia.”
The above selected Press opinions are quite sufficient
to show the esteem Herr Rassmussen is held in by those
who have the best opportunity to watch his career.

This Easter, the Hotel Australia, Sydney,
was full to overflowing with visitors. Reasonable
rates and excellent service are making the Aus-
tralia the most popular Hotel on the whole
continent.*
That fine horseman, Dean O’Donovan, years
out of number parish priest of Mudgee, N.S.W.,
goes to Britain for a couple of years’ holiday.
The dean’s parish included Rylstone and Gillgong,
and frequently he has held service in Gulgongand
Mudgee—22 miles apart —on the same morning,
his mode of conveyance horseback “ as the crow
flies.” Fences and creeks counted nought to the
dean.
Editor Bulletin. —Sir, —We wish to direct
your attention to the deplorable state of things at
the Weir Contract, Hillston, where fifty men have
been left by the contractor without wages for the
last five weeks. A great portion of us have wives
and families dependent on us. We are starving,
as we have no money to buy food. The boarding
houses are unable to keep open any longer, as we
cannot pay them. We are here in the middle of
a plain and, being destitute, cannot get away to
where there is a chance of employment. Govern-
ment has helped us here on to the contract; now
we cannot get paid. Our wives have to part with
our furniture bit by bit to procure the necessaries
of life. We are indebted to the kindness of others
for existence, and we hope that Government will
take steps to see us paid at once, or provide transit
for us to where we can get a living without de-
pending on charity.—Yours truly, John Ryall,
H. Hartridge, D. Shine, P. Stevenson, F. T.
Denver (on behalf of men). —The Weir, Hillston,
April 14th, 1897.
Judging by the remarks one overhears, it
is evident that men often forget they are mortal.
On Easter Monday an angular-faced individual
with a corporation and spats remarked to a friend
when a harbor collision seemed imminent: “What
a blank of a ducking we’d get if we got capsized
with all this crowd.” There were perhaps 1000
aboard the steamer.
The building of the first Lager Bier
brewery in N.S.W. is nearing completion at Dow-
ling-street, Moore Park, Sydney. The erecting
superintendent brewer is Mr. A. J. Metzler, one
of the three world’s builders of breweries. The
brewery covers acres, and is an object lesson in
construction and arrangement. Some months will
elapse before the Bier is fit for market. The
N.S.W. public has been well prepared for the
Bier’s advent by the judicious persistency of the
well-known firm J. Kidman. This firm has been
pushing the Victorian Lager Bier as a means of
introducing the coming N.S.W. article, which
will be the same Bier locally brewed. The firm,
too, has not only been distributing circulars with
every parcel of groceries, but has also, by general
advertising, made the advent of the N.S.W. Lager
Bier a household fact. When the first brew comes
Amusements.

THE EIGHTH REPORT of the DIRECTORS, to be presented to the Shareholders at the Eighth Ordinary General
Meeting, to be held at the Bank, 126 ELIZABETH-ST., at Noon on TUESDAY, 4th MAY, 1897.
REPORT.
The directors beg to submit to the shareholders their eighth report, with a balance-sheet and statement of profit
and loss for the half-year ended 31st March, which are made out in the form prescribed by the “ Companies Act” now
in force, and are duly audited.
After providing for expenses of management, interest accrued on deposits, rebate on bills current, and tax on note
circulation, the net profit, including the balance brought forward from previous half-year, amounted to £8,634 15s 7d.,
which has been placed in reduction of bad debts, as shewn in the balance-sheet.
Since the last meeting of shareholders, the new “ Companies Act” has been passed, which will enable the bank to
write down lost capital.
A complete investigation of the bank’s accounts has; been made for the purpose of ascertaining the amount re-
quired to provide for all losses, and, as shown by the balance-sheet, there is an estimated deficiency of £550,115 7s sd.
Your directors will take the first opportunity to app'ly to the Court for authority to write off this amount; but,
before doing so, there are certain preliminary difficulties with regard to the adjustment of the amount paid up on the
ordinary shares, which will require to be completed before such application can be made.
It is only right to point out that the whole of these losses have been incurred by the old bank, and are largely due
to the general depreciation in all classes of property which has since occurred.
In order to provide additional working capital the d irectors recommend a call of £2 per share on the ordinary
shares of the bank, which will be made payable by instalments, spread over a considerable period of time, and a
special meeting has been convened to make the necessary alteration in the articles, as it is essential that the paid-up
capital on the preference shares should remain intact.
The directors are pleased to be able to report that the new business of the bank still continues to improve. This
is, no doubt, largely due to the provision in the bank’s s cheme, which makes all new business preferential.
During the past half-year the Hon. S. Williamson, Ki.L.C., tendered his resignation as a director of the bank.
The agency at Ascotvale has been closed, and the ajrency at Moe is now open once a week, instead of daily as
formerly.
The eighth ordinary general meeting of shareholders will be held at the head office of the company, 126 Elizabeth-
street, Melbourne, on Tuesday, the 4th day of May, 1897, at noon.
By order of the board.
Melbourne, 22nd April, 1897. SELBY PAXTON, General Manager.

To the Shareholders of the Colonial Bank of Australasia Limited. Melbourne, -_nd April, 189/.
Ladies and Gentlemen,—We beg to report that we have audited the accounts of the Colonial Bank of Australasia
Limited for the half-year ended 31st March, 1897, and that the accompaying balance-sheet is, in our opinion correct
We have also to report that during such audit we have not observed or become acquainted with anv breach of the
“Companies Acts ” committed by the company or any director, manager, employee, auditor, or shareholder thereof
and that, so far as we are in a position to form an opinion, the balance-sheet and accounts have been drawn un in
accordance with the provisions of the “ Companies Acts,” and present a correct view of the state of the comnanv’s
affairs, and that all our requisitions in regard to the shareholders’ as well as the private balance-sheet have been diil v
complied with. u •’
We are, ladies and gentlemen, yours faithfully,
J. C. DUIGAN I

DALLEY STATUED.
One of those bright three or four Australia’s soil
may call its own,
One of those its suns had ripened into wealth of
mind and heart,
On receiving honor lately in the old-style bronze
and stone
Had the virtues which attained it graded for the
public mart.
First a “Scholar.” Well, he was that in a girly
sort of way ;
Little odds and ends from all books he would
fondly dally o’er.
Then a “ Patriot ” —but patriotic ’neath Imperial
sway,
And a “ Statesman ’’—who, not knowing why,
demanded niggers’ gore.
Ah, the Dailey whom his people love was but a
Man whose heart,
Lighted by a brilliant fancy, gave its service as
a gift,
Stumbling here and there, but ever proudly glad
to take the part
Of the men and women round him who required
a gentle lift.
G.G.

James Edward Naylor, who in 1877 lived
at 115 George-street, Sydney, and in 1880 (it is
believed) went to sea in the Edith Deep, is entitled
to a sum of money payable to him or his repre-
sentatives by Mr. Napoleon Argles, solicitor, 81
Gracechurch-street, London, E.C.
Hermes : “ Re the alleged seizure of the
regalia of the Footscray Knights of Labor.
Writer has been a K. of L. for about six years,
and learns for the first time that the grimy
brotherhood has such things. In the Maoriland
lodges the ‘ regalia ’ consists of a piece of chalk, a
chairman’s hammer, a book of rules, and about
of democratic literature. The passwords
are absolutely necessary. The Vic. lodges may
have regalia, but the whole thing sounds like a
bit of leg-pulling.”

The question as to whether tobacco-grow-
ing in Victoria can possibly end in anything
better than bad smoke should be set at rest very
soon. A first crop, planted by the American
tobacco-expert at his experimental farm, has been
gathered, and is now in process of curing. The
leaf in its green state “ comes up to Mr. Bondu-
rant’s expectations.” When it has been cured and
reduced to ashes by an independent jury of
smokers, they will be able to judge the altitude of
Bondurant’s expectations.
Melb. : “ There is a lot of hypocrisy over
this Methodist Union. You see how, in Parlia-
ment, a Harcourt and a Morley will ramp for
English interference with Turkey, when they
know it is impossible, but if the case was otherwise
they would be patriotically silent. Thus with
the ferocious clamorers for union in the Wesleyan
Conference. They are only too glad to see bolder
men trample on the agreement which the poor
Methodies plead for.”
The Eye-sight measure per post by the in
ventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough, the
well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney, enables

P|X.Z. “An organization is afoot in
America to collect the songs and music of the dis-
appearing Red Man—Iroquois war-songs, Sioux
ghost-dance music, and the like. When are the
Australian corroboree chants, the spell-music for
increase of iguanas and kangaroos and wild duck,
and for the rain to come, and the dirges for the
dead, to be collected ? The Australian aboriginal
lore has been woefully neglected. Barring the
good, careful, observant work of Fison, Howitt,
and Taplin, the uninformed hotch-potch of Brough
Smyth and the unscientific compilation of Curr,
very little trustworthy exists. Of written-down
music there is none. ”
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
Louis Becke, from London to The Bul-
letin :—
I read with interest in the Christmas Bulletin Evison’s
“ Secret of the Sea,” especially that portion referring to
the mysterious ball of fire seen by the barque Marion in
1859 —“The light was seen straight ahead and coming
down with great speed,” etc., etc. Now (unless I have
previously told The Bulletin the following), an account
of what the crew of the Island trader Bannockburn and
myself saw will prove of interest. Evison’s uncanny
“ ball of fire ” was seen at night-time ; ours in the day.
In May, ’82 (I think), the Bannockburn, bound from the
Marshall Islands to Auckland, was passing to the wind-
ward of the Fiji Group. The weather was close, damp,
and muggy, and our native crew saw every indication of
a hurricane. At a little after 4 p.m., the wind being very
light at the time, and all hands on deck, we saw a small,
but very bright light about five miles astern. It quickly
increased in size, and we at once saw it was travelling
fast towards us, but bearing a little on the port hand.
In a very few minutes it was abeam of us—a huge
ball of clear white light (I had never seen
electric-light then, but that is what it exactly resembled)
emitting scintillations of steely blue. It swept onward
with a waving, undulating motion, keeping, however, a
uniform distance of apparently twenty feet or so above
the sea. In two or three minutes it had passed, appa-
rently not increasing in bulk and with diminishing speed
till it was about two miles ahead of us. Then, without
any report, it went out—vanished. Now this is perfectly
true. We had neither whisky nor any other liquor on
board, and, in fact, were deplorably in want of both grog
and provisions. The only man on board who seemed
deeply concerned at the awesome visitant was the ship’s
cook, who had two tickets on the Melbourne Cup, and
was in great agony of mind to get to Auckland in time to
cable money on a horse named Meteor.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page

X.Z. : “Working all night in a Little
Collins-street, Melb., printing-office, I saw a
eomp. go sound asleep as he stood at case, yet still
go on mechanically filling his stick. This went on
for about five minutes. Then, seeing him begin
to rock, the foreman held him gently in his arms,
while I, as gently, annexed his stick. A proof
therefrom showed a senseless conglomeration of
letters, figures, and stops, showing that his body
had gone on working after his brain had ceased to
be conscious. Three akin cases after brain injury
are given in the 8.M.J., Dec. 12, ’96.

Dear Bulletin, —Can anyone mould a Fac-
tory Act sufficiently comprehensive? One little
shirt-factory opposite my boarding-house is
limited to a family of three and the servant-girl.
The pjace is kept going all day and until about
9.30 at night practically the whole year round.
The slavey answers the milkman at 7 a.m., scrubs
and sweeps and cooks all day, and at night takes a
hand with the shirt-making—and ceaseless
machinery by gas-light with the temperature in
the nineties is probably no joke. Somebody ought
to marry that girl out of pity.—S.D.
A certain popular Queensland black labour
agent had an admirably off-hand style of going
to business. “ Want to come alonga Queensland
work alonga sugar plantation ? ” he would say
gently to a likely dusky recruit. Invariably the
novelty of the suggestion was the first thing to
strike the savage’s perception, and the reply would
be “ Yes, me come.” “All right, you blanker,
come on ! ” and the astonished nig. would be
seized by the neck and hurled into the bottom of
the boat without being allowed to speculate upon
possible domestic objections. So much for the
“ consent ” safeguard. Some time ago a couple of
comely Polynesian maidens wanted to join their
spouses in sunny Queensland, but the agent did
not see how he could manage the business in view
of the regulation prohibiting the transportation of
lone females. A solution suggested itself, how-
ever—the gay blackbirder hauled the pair up
with a brace of males selected hap-hazard from
the hold and “had ’em hitched.” When the ship
returned the rightful husbands turned up and a
row was raised with the result that Govt, has
since regretfully accepted the agent’s resignation.

An Easter Song .
[FOR TH BULLETIN.]
The gateways of Gethsemane
Have mouldered in decay ;
Rank poppy and anemone
Make red the sacred way;
The cry of Christ the crucified
Rings dimmer with the years ;
A fiercer sound has deadened it
The fall of the World’s Tears.
The cry of Christ the crucified
Came ringing from the Cross,
Mixed with the two thieves’ at His side,
And the world beheld His loss
In a grand and glorious martyrdom,
Made by His own commands ;
It’s heard the Garden Agony,
It saw the Bleeding Hands.
Its hardened heart has thrilled to these
For many a hundred years,
And many a life has robbed its ease
To win its Christ more tears—
More tears for such a martyrdom !
So exquisite a death !
That lends the saints their hope of Heaven,
And lightens Sin’s last breath.
But the gateways of Gethsemane
Have mouldered in decay ;
Rank poppy and anemone
Make red the sacred way ;
The cry of Cn. ist the Crucified,
Rings dimmer with the years ;
A fiercer sound has deadened it—
The fall of the World’s Tears.
The World goes weeping on, but nob
For Christ its tears are shed ;
The sadness of a living lot
Is keener than a dead.
The World is weeping tears of blood,
Wrung from a tortured heart;
The tears that flow for one’s own want
Sting with the sorest smart.
Even the children’s tears are poured
Into this sea of tears.
They know the story of the Lord
Who gave a life for theirs ;
But what is Christ to Hunger ?
Dead Thorns to living Thirst ?
The children’s wails are loudest, or
Their little hearts would burst.
Listen ! Along the centuries
A bird has sought to sing ;
And every wind has beaten it—
Even the winds of spring ;
And all along the centuries
One flower has tried to blossom,
And every Spring has withered it,
And every Winter frozen.
If from the dark Gethsemane
The cry of Christ rings down
O’er poppy or anemone,
And ruined gate and town,
It is to tend the flower
And give the bird his wing—
Poor bud that never opens,
Poor bird that may not sing.
The bird would sing supernally,
If anyone would hear ;
The flower would bloom eternally,
If any held it dear ;
The World would stem its sea of tears,
The Christ-eyes smile above,
If in the straitened places bloomed
The Bud and Bird of Love.
Sydney.
LOUISE MACK.
His Key-Note,
[FOR TH BULLETIN.]
Swiftly round the summer-graced harbor she
swept an eye blue as the water. She had a falcon-
like carriage of the head, and her tones were
resonantly imperious.
“ I am glad I came. The day leaves nothing to
be desired —which is more than I can say— — ” _
“ For the company ! ” he concluded, with his
eyes fixed on a Manly steamer, pounding power-
fully past the boat he was steering. “ No; I don’t
suppose I’ve sparkled much, so far. Hardly to
be expected, in the circumstances, is it ? ”
“That’s just it!” A fine scornfulness was
about her nostrils. “ You were always such a
... jellified kind of creature ! No edge to you !
—no flavor ! —no —in short, no key-note ! ”
His little mild eyes parried for once the thrust
of her sword-like glance. A small, sad smile
came into the small, sad face. “ My key-note,”
he said, “may not have been easy to find ; but it
has been found at last, and struck ! ”
The amplification of her trope surprised her ;
she hadn’t thought it was in him. But the habit
of contempt is hard to break ; she put it down to
a chance shot.
“ No doubt! ” she said, with a little shrug.
“ But I’m not partial to the minor; so, if you’ve
brought me out to-day merely to show me how
lugubriously you can wear the willow ”
“ You’ll know in good time why I’ve brought
you out. But I hardly thought you’d have been
allowed to come.”
There was a quiet indifference in his manner
that was new to her —some change in the whole
man that, because it impressed her in spite of her-
self, impelled her to still greater arrogance of
sp ?f Allowed ? I ! I am my own mistress, I
should imagine! Were you thinking-did you
presume to think of Captain Plungerford ? ”
He nodded—looking straight over the bows.
“ Yes,” he said, quietly, “I was thinking of—of
my successor ! ” .
“ Your successor ! ” She started up m the boat,
and angrily shook off the hand with which he
sought to steady her. “How dare you! You
never occupied ”
“Didn’t I?” he put in, as quietly as before.
“ Well, you did your best to make me think so, at
all events ! ”
“You are generous ! ” she sneered, as she sat
down again. “It is like you! Your successor,
as you call him, is at least a man 1 ’
“ There is nothing ungenerous in citing simple
matters of fact. And people’s notions differ as to
what constitutes a man. Your idea, it appears to
me, is but a school-missy one, after all ! ”
Astonishment at his unwonted temerity kept
her silent for a few moments. “ What! ” she
said, then, “You dare?”
“ There is no daring in talking a little straight
to you. You want it badly ! Your notion of a
man, I take it, is built on women-writers’ rubbish
about * curt, incisive accents,’ and ‘ one accus-
tomed to be obeyed,’ and so forth. That’s the
sort of swagger men like Plungerford bring from
the army ; and precious foolish they look when
they find that nobody outside it cares a brass
button for their ‘ ringing tones of command,’ and
all the rest of it! ”
With a harsh little laugh, he eased-off the sheet
to get the boat more before the wind, shifting now
to south-west, and urging them straight towards
the mighty ocean-portal of the Heads. Without a
word, the woman sat, and looked, and wondered if
this could indeed be the man she had refused to
credit with any opinion of his own —the man she
had once told that he was without a single angle,
either of mind or body. The angles were showing
out now with a vengeance ! Even physically he
looked darker and squarer and stronger ; his hand
was clenched firmly on the tiller ; his eye looked
steadfastly ahead into the great Pacific-perspec-
tive —purpling now to the red rays of the sun that
was sinking over the city far behind them.
A vague apprehension seized upon her, as she,
too, looked out into the illimitable. The breeze
had not freshened, but the long sweep that was
coming into the boat’s rise and fall told already of
the ocean’s giant pulse-beat. But she spoke with
calm insistance.
“ Put the boat about. It is getting late. I do
not wish to go any further.”
“ Do you hear ? ” she said, with the half-fierce-
ness of sudden alarm. “ It will soon be dark. It
will be very late when we get back.”
He turned upon her the face she had so often to
herself called “ wooden ” ; she shuddered as she
saw that it was now of stone.
“ Back ! ” he said. “ Back, where ? ”
“This is trifling, Mr. Plain john '.—let it end!
Back to Sydney, of course ! ”
“We are not going back to Sydney.” He
looked at her tranquilly, and the deadly calm of
his voice assured her that here was no trifling.
“We are not going back to anywhere. We are
going on ! ”
“ On ? ” she echoed, beginning horribly to realise
that the man was mad—practically, if not tech-
nically. “ On, where ? ”
“On into the infinite!” He waved a hand
towards the fast-darkening ocean. “Away from
Plungerford ! —away from everybody and every-
thing but ourselves ! On, and on, and on ! ”
The castle of her arrogance collapsed. The
woman who most bravely faces deadly peril—the
calmest woman in desperate emergency—is seldom
she of the firm front and haughty voice—assured
with an assurance which is but the artificial pro-
duct of the deference of men impressed and
women overborne. She flung herself abjectly
down before the man she had despised—this
haughty soul of half-an-hour before —clutching
wildly at his arm, and half-shrieking out her
words.
“Mr. Plainjohn ! —Harry! what dreadful
notion is this? You would not drown me?—me,
whom you said you loved. I—l have behaved ill
to you, Harry !—I acknowledge it—but let us
only go back, and ” .
He never looked at her—never moved a muscle
of his face. It might have been from the lips of a
statue that came the words: “ You have struck
my key-note at last 1 And the song is a Song of
Death l ” , „ ,
It was darkening fast now. The hollow thunder
of the seas under North Head made gloomy
menace to the ear ; upon the outer South Head s
lofty column appeared its mighty intermittent
star ; higher and higher heaved the boat upon the
long Pacific swell; and still the woman, lying at
the man’s feet, implored and threatened and im-
plored again. And the man sat like marble.
At last, with a sudden re-action, the woman
jumped up and made for the main-halliards. A
turn or two, and —just as the man caught mo-
mentary sight of something looming large and
black in the dimness close ahead —down came the
sail upon him, the gaff striking him heavily upon
the shoulder. Then —with a hollow thump-
thumping, a seething rush of water, a hoarse
shouting from above —came a shock, a splitting
crash, and Plainjohn’s boat was in splinters.
* * * «
“ The gentleman who was with me —did
you save him, too ? ” •
The master of the Blackamoor (steam-collier)
dismally shook his head.
“Unfort’nately, no, mum! I thought, of
course, you must a-had somebody with, you, but
we never saw nobody but yourself. I’m afraid
the screw must have struck him as he drifted aft;
you had a very narrow squeak of it yourself,
mum ! ” .
“I had,” she said —not referring to the screw,
and with a tremble in her voice which is in it to
this day. Also, when next she looked into a
mirror, she started incredulously back —her hair
was streaked with grey !
It is all grey now, for the story is old enough to
make many people wonder how this timidest and
gentlest of old maids could ever have been the
haughty beauty of tradition.

perance Hall, Fitzroy, within an easy stone s throw
of the Salvation Army barracks.”

Hobson’s Bay.
[FOR TH BULLETIN.]
L have seen your boasted Harbor, and it's worth a
nation’s boast —
So many miles of pictures in an inlet in your
coast;
Parramatta’s panorama, Darling Harbor, and the
Cove
Are mints of myriad beauty—each an artist’s
treasure-trove.
Tis a bit of Eden left on earth to lighten dismal
days,
It redeems you from the System and its old
Satanic ways ;
But now I’m off Nepean, and the rollicking old
Rip
Gives a parting fling finale as I’m ending up my
trip
In homely Hobson’s Bay,
Where I love the lavish way
She flings her wide horizons open-breasted to the
day 1
We have no flirting ferries trailing skirts of lacy
foam;
No hills of hanging gardens like a Babylonian
home ;
Though views between the ti-tree scrub are merely
vacant sea,
Enough ! the open sky-line is the sight o’ sights
for me !
A heeling, hull-down clipper, just showing top-
sails furled,
Has a touch of old-time rovers starting out around
the world ;
But a steamer slipping suddenly behind your
Middle Head
With true land-lubber instinct seems to hug the
shore in dread.
I am outward-bound and far,
Where the Happy Islands are,
When I see a bellied canvas sinking down upon a
spar.
I’ve known the Bay when shadowed by an arch of
angry cloud,
And seen the sails scud shelterwards a flying,
frightened crowd;
A derelict ship’s grating washing on and off the
beach,
And watched the brown hawk steadying and heard
the grey gulls screech,
The white surf redden angrily, dissolving crum-
bling cliffs,
Beneath whose lee the fishers beached their patched
and battered skiffs ;
When the wind’s a Polar roarer and the livid
rollers break
The Bay’s a child of Ocean, not an ornamental
lake,
And she sings the fo’c’s’le note
From a hoarse, deep-water throat
To the youngster reading Marryat behind an up-
turned boat!
I’ve watched the long black liner with her brass-
work all ablaze
Growing mellow in the distance through the
golden summer haze;
When bouncing hills of sea-green caught the sun-
light on their peaks
And in shimmering reflections flashed the summer
on my cheeks;
Till a calm came down from Heaven, and I let the
tiller go,
In the listless warmth of evening saw the crimson
sun sink low;
Then the peace of nearing night-time, smoothed the
water till it shone
With the stars of half the universe : it mirrored
all and one.
Then the broad quiescent Bay
Had its own mesmeric way
Of giving me communion with the lustrous Milky
Way !
There’s a spell in Middle Harbor with its mingled
shade and sheen
As though hallowed by the presence of some hid-
den vestal queen ;
Every headland curtsies backward with a grace
that seems to feel
The beauty of the vistas that its profile did con-
ceal ;
But Beauty has a mournful soul that saddens
while it cheers,
And the bays brimful of shadows bring a sudden
tide of tears—
A vague and utter wistfulness—a hopeless, bitter
love
For something out of all the world—beyond—
away—above—
The air of pause and peace
Wakens longing for release
From the tangled web of living, with its harsh
perplexities.
Now Hobson’s rarely beautiful, but rugged, strong,
and wild ;
Her south-wind is a sailor with land-fragrance un-
defiled !
Out upon her waters all alone with sky and sea
You’re friends with every comber curling under
you to lee!
No dim poetic vexings plant their poison in your
mind
W hen spray has soaked your guernsey and your
hair is in the wind ;
And if no dainty anchorage can tempt your eye
ashore,
You’ve solely sea to gaze upon and learn to love
it more !
It is spring-tide in your blood,
And life is at its flood
When a sweltering sou’-wester sends you right
about to scud!
THE STROLLER.
Australia’s recent visitor, Lord Maghera-
morne, originally M‘Garel Hogg, at one time con-
trolled the principal London water-supply. Prof.
Thorold Rogers exhibited a phial of the muddy
fluid in Parliament for purposes of debate, and
nick-named it “ Hogg’s-wash. ’ Under these cir-
cumstances a title and a change of name became
imperative.
A pressman recently went to a billet in
Maori land, determined to knock off whisky. He
took out a prohibition-order against himself with
that end in view, and has since spent most of his
time in convincing sceptical publicans that his
twin-brother is the prohibited person.
There is jealousy in all professions. Mel-
bourne’s foremost beetle-catcher and tick-worrier
is greatly aggrieved because the local monkey and
lion-expert trotted to Queensland and back on an
tour without letting him know.
The idea of this interloper having, perhaps, dis-
covered a new specimen on the strict Q.T. is
almost more than the great Insectarian can bear.
Maoriland Bush- Whacking.
[FOR TH BULLETIN.]
So you reckon that any fool can knock down a
tree ? So did I—about ten years ago. But I’ve
changed my mind since.
I shan’t forget my first turn at bush-whacking.
I and my mate took a contract to fall 40 acres—
everything except pines and ratas, and them up to
3ft. We’d never seen heavy bush before, but were
of the same opinion as you.
Our troubles started with the scrubbing. It was
all right as long as we could see daylight, but as
we began to get into the heart of it we found our-
selves completely at sea. We kept a line about
as straight as a dog’s hmd-leg, and had no idea
which way we were going. One night when we
knocked off we tried to take a short-cut, and
didn t strike camp until 10 a.m. next day. An-
other niglit we left our slashers sticking in a
twisty koinui, a mark that we couldn’t miss ;'next
morning, when we came to look for them, that
tree had shifted and there were over 20 twisty
koinuis in its place. It took us till dinner-time to
find the right one.
Then we went over our line and scrubbed about
five acres for another fellow by mistake. We
might have been scrubbing there now, for there
were over 10,000 acres of standing bush to work
on, but the fellow we were falling for called in and
put us right with much derisive language. Ev< ry
now and again we would miss half an acre or so
that we didn’t find out till we started to fall.
Well, we finished the scrubbing somehow in two
months more than twice as long as we ought to
have taken. “Now,” we thought, “all our
troubles are at an end—all is plain sailing.”
We had both been used to hard graft all our
lives, and if anyone had told me my hands would
blister with two days’ axe-work I should have
laughed at him. Blister! They did blister.
We dreaded the first half-hour every morning, as
every blow of the axe would set our hands going
make us feel as an eel feels when you skin him
alive and put him on the fire. It was all right
when once we got warmed to our work, and in a
week or so our hands ceased to trouble us.
We hadn’t the knack at first, and would take
enough wood out of a 2ft. tree to bring down a
10-footer. We would often scarf a big tawa front
and back, and if the beggar wouldn’t go, we’d
start on the sides till she looked like a giant pencil
sharpened at the point. Then most likely she
would go back on us into the standing bush, where
there would be a nice job cleaning up.
As for cutting on a jigger (a jigger is a piece of
plank or limb with a spike on one end which is put
into a nick cut in the tree, so that the axeman can
stand on it and cut up higher), the first time I tried
I caught my axe in a vine and took a somersault
backwards. My mate in his turn missed his stroke
and went a header on to the ground.
Driving was another thing that took us some
time to get into. We would scarf five or six trees
and try to drive a big one on top of them ; the
brute would most likely miss them altogether, or
else we wouldn’t have had the scarves deen enough,
and two or three would go and stick in the last
one. Then it was a case of a rat pulling away the
prop in a catch-’em-alive trap and getting away
with a whole skin.
We mastered the falling of the big trees in a
few weeks, but the whitewoods long puzzled us.
A whitewood is generally a bottle-bottomed tree,
hollow in the centre, with the limbs starting from
sft. to 10ft. off the ground. A bushman will climb
up and lop off all the limbs, just leaving the hollow
stump A nevv-ohum tries to cut them through at
the bottom, and finds that it takes him most of the
day We soon got sick of that, and tried the
climbing dodge. It s very awkward at first, as you
can t get a decent swing for your axe : bes ; d»«
when you cut one limb through, the tree won t so
until you have cut all the others, and, as you are
in the centre, perhaps 10ft. above the ground it
makes things pretty lively. ’ lc
Dangerous? Well, you have to be careful at
hist, but you soon get into the way of looking
after yourself. We had no accidents, except° that
my mate cut his shin a bit, and I was knocked
silly for a few minutes by a falling limb. Next
camp to us, though, a poor beggar had the butt of
n 'i a I trf i e J ulu b U P an( J «ateh him in the stomach
We helped to carry him out. There were 14 0 f
us altogether, and we took turn about at the
stretcher. That was hard work if you like • the
track was up to our knees in mud, and the ’noor
devil hollered most of the way in. It took
three days to get him into the township, and after
all, he died before he was there an hour.
rt’s not bad work though, if you are fit and the
packers can get the tucker to you ; it’s the verv
devil when you have to swag everything in for five
or six miles through mud and supplejacks
specially if you have done a hard day’s work
already. To see a drive of half-a-dozen big trees
go crashing down is a sight to be remembered and
if once you learn how to use an axe properly other
work seems very tame. Whatever you do don’t
go falling by yourself. You might get pinned
down by a small sapling, and it might be lona-
enough before your corpse was found! And the
death that comes to a man pinned down by a
fallen tree isn’t one to hanker after. J

Her Blood was Out of Order and She was all Run Down,
Read what Ayer’s Sarsaparilla Did for Her.

No one to look to-day at Miss Sophie Kiefer, a pretty and stylise young belle,
whose portrait is given above, and who lives at the northwest corner of Fourth and
Queen streets, Philadelphia, would imagine that she had ever been in anything but
the pink of health. Her skin is clear and creamy, her teeth are white and even, she
nas luxuriant dark hair, and her eyes are large and bright, of a lustrous brown. Yet.
notwithstanding Miss Kiefer’s splendid appearance, only a year ago she was in
extremely poor health, and her blood was in a very bad condition. Miss Kiefer’.:
story is best told in her own words :
“ "NY hen I began to take Ayer’s Sarsaparilla I was not only run down, but I was
thoroughly discouraged. Doctors and proprietary medicines both failed to help me.
My blood was disordered. But the first bottle of Ayer’s Sarsaparilla helped me so
much that I decided to keep it up, and did so. Then, inside of a month, 1 began to
think that the Sarsaparilla was a wonder. It cleared my skin completely and restored
my appetite to its former condition. My general health was recovered and the third
bottle removed every appearance of disorder from my blood. Not only that, but it
left me better than ever. For several months I had been ill and complaining. My
health in general, aside from the condition of my blood, was very poor. I remember
one day, after attending a ball the night before, when I felt so miserable that I was
afraid I was going to die, almost. My appearance, of which I always took considera-
ble care, was the thing that worried me most of all, I will acknowledge. But as I
tell you. Ayer’s Sarsaparilla rejuvenated me. I would take it any time in preference
to toilet preparations and skin soaps and washes, for it worked wouders with me. I
can recommend Ayer’s Sarsaparilla to anyone whose blood is poor or whose general
health is unsatisfactory.”
